THE PLAN BEHIND THE WAR NO ONE IS EXPLAINING W/ MIKE BAKER

Jillian Michaels(628.0K) ·69:33 ·9.1K views · 2026-04-01
> "The decision of whether the strait stays open doesn't actually get made at the Pentagon or even in Tehran.
TL;DR: While the U.S.-Israeli "Operation Epic Fury" has successfully dismantled Iran's conventional military and IRGC command, Tehran has successfully retaliated by blockading the Strait of Hormuz, triggering a global energy crisis and forcing a high-stakes 15-point peace...

TL;DR: While the U.S.-Israeli "Operation Epic Fury" has successfully dismantled Iran's conventional military and IRGC command, Tehran has successfully retaliated by blockading the Strait of Hormuz, triggering a global energy crisis and forcing a high-stakes 15-point peace proposal from the Trump administration.

Military Dominance vs. Economic Stalemate

  • Operation Epic Fury: As of April 2026, allied forces have established absolute air and sea supremacy.
      • Air Defense Neutralized: Russian-made S-300 and domestic Bavar-373 batteries have been destroyed.
      • Naval Attrition: Conventional Iranian naval forces and Kilo-class submarines were sunk at the pier; IRGC fast-attack fleets are largely neutralized.
      • Command Decapitation: Precision strikes have eliminated senior IRGC leadership and obliterated command-and-control hubs for regional proxies.
  • The Asymmetric Counter: Iran has successfully transitioned to an economic war by closing the Strait of Hormuz.
      • Energy Impact: 20% of the world’s oil supply is offline; prices have surged to $116 per barrel, and domestic gas prices have exceeded $4.00 per gallon.
      • Tehran’s Strategy: The regime is betting they can inflict enough global economic pain to force a Western retreat before their own domestic stability collapses.

The Strait of Hormuz: A Strategic Trap

  • Geographic Kill Zone: At only 21 nautical miles wide with two-mile navigable lanes, the Strait forces U.S. ships against the rugged Zagros Mountains, eliminating reaction time against hidden coastal threats.
  • The Drone Math: Iran utilizes "missile cities" (underground tunnel networks) and commercial trucks to launch $20,000 suicide drones.
      • Cost Imbalance: The U.S. Navy uses interceptor missiles costing millions of dollars to stop $20,000 drones.
      • Saturation Risk: Destroyers carry a finite number of interceptors; if Iran launches 100 drones and a ship has 90 missiles, the remaining ten drones create a catastrophic threat.
  • The Insurance Barrier: Mike Baker emphasizes that the Strait is governed by London-based maritime insurance, not just the Pentagon.
      • War Zone Classification: If the U.S. attempts a physical takeover, insurance premiums will become unviable, causing commercial tankers to drop anchor regardless of military protection.
      • Environmental Risk: A single strike on an unarmored supertanker would create a burning wreck, physically blocking the narrow shipping lanes.

Trump’s 15-Point Peace Proposal

  • Diplomatic Channels: Emissary Steve Witkoff has delivered a "blueprint for peace" to Tehran via Islamabad, Pakistan.
  • Core U.S. Demands:
      • Nuclear Cessation: Iran must permanently abandon nuclear weapons, surrender its 450kg stockpile of highly enriched uranium to the IAEA, and dismantle facilities at Natanz and Fordow.
      • Proxy Termination: Immediate cessation of funding and operational direction for Hezbollah, Hamas, and the Houthis.
      • Maritime Freedom: Reopening the Strait of Hormuz as a free international corridor.
  • U.S. Concessions:
      • Sanctions Relief: Lifting the "crippling" international sanctions currently suffocating the Iranian economy.
      • The "Snap-Back" Gamble: Washington has offered to remove the UN "snap-back" mechanism—the ultimate veto-proof weapon that allows the U.S. to unilaterally re-impose global sanctions.
  • Iranian Counter-Demands: Tehran has rejected the plan as a "maximalist demand for surrender," demanding reparations for strike damages and a total withdrawal of Allied forces.

Iranian Leadership and Geopolitical Dynamics

  • Leadership Vacuum: Following the death of the Supreme Leader, his son Mojtaba was nominated by the "Assembly of Experts" but has not been seen in public. The IRGC currently appears to be "calling the shots."
  • The Key Negotiator: Mike Baker identifies Mohammad Baqer Qalibaf (Speaker of Parliament) as the likely primary contact. Baker warns that Qalibaf is a "hardliner" and a "wolf in sheep's clothing," despite any Western efforts to frame him as a moderate.
  • Foreign Interference:
      • Russia/China Support: Baker notes that Iran is likely receiving sophisticated targeting data from Russia, explaining recent accurate strikes on Allied bases.
      • China’s Interests: While China benefits from discounted Iranian oil, the current instability threatens their long-term economic interests and may force them to reconsider their support for the regime.

Notable Quotes

"The decision of whether the strait stays open doesn't actually get made at the Pentagon or even in Tehran. It's being made right now by executives in business suites in London. Commercial shipping runs entirely on maritime insurance." — Mike Baker
"There is no reason to have 60% enriched uranium if you are claiming that you have a civilian peaceful nuclear program... Getting from 60% to weapons grade is the easy lift." — Mike Baker
"You broke it, you paid for it. Look, we kind of went in, we’ve broken it—not to say it didn't deserve breaking—but we also need to figure out what else can we do now to try to get the strait back on track." — Mike Baker
Operation Epic Fury has been a showcase of American military dominance. Iran's air defenses have been turned to scrap. Their navy is on the ocean floor, and their IRGC command structure has been decapitated. So from a purely military standpoint, this has been a huge success. But Iran had a backup plan, and it is working. They slammed shut the Strait of Hormuz, and 20% of the world's oil supply was gone overnight. Energy prices are spiking, and the global economy is rattled. Joining us today is Mike Baker. Mike spent 17 years as a CIA covert operations officer. He is a premiere specialist in counterterrorism, intelligence, and Middle Eastern geopolitics, and he's going to help us break it all down. Here is the story behind the story on the Hormuz blockade and Trump's 15-point peace proposal. Let's get into it. All right guys, it's April 2026, and we're now roughly a month into the joint American and Israeli military campaign against Iran. Right now, we're witnessing a dangerous, high-stakes stalemate. Militarily, the allied coalition has established absolute supremacy over the past few weeks. The US and Israeli stealth platforms have systematically dismantled Iran's integrated air defense system. We've basically turned their Russian-made S-300 and domestic Bavar-373 missile batteries into burning scrap. The skies are uncontested, and the coalition then went to work on the sea. The Iranian conventional navy, along with the IRGC's fast attack swarm fleets, are effectively neutralized. Their Kilo-class submarines are sunk at the pier in Bandar Abbas, and their coastal missile boats have been turned into artificial reefs. Precision bunker-buster munitions have decapitated the IRGC's senior leadership and obliterated the command and control hubs that are directing proxy forces across Iraq, Syria, and Lebanon. American air power has comprehensively degraded Iran's conventional military machine. So from the sky, Operation Epic Fury is flawlessly executing its mission. But here's the catch: economically, the war has the potential to bleed out of control. Iran has fully activated its asymmetric playbook. They realize that they can't win a conventional firefight, and the regime is dug in and has effectively blockaded the Strait of Hormuz. They've traded their air defenses for a stranglehold on global shipping, which has triggered a massive spike in energy prices and is sending shockwaves through Wall Street, London, and Tokyo. Now the allied forces have the military advantage, but Tehran is betting that they can inflict enough economic pain to force the West to blink first. So maybe you're wondering, like I was, with the most powerful military in the world, why doesn't the US just simply sail in, take over the strait, and guarantee the free flow of oil? It sounds like a straightforward display of American naval supremacy. But with closer examination, military planners will tell you that a physical takeover of the Strait of Hormuz isn't just improbable; it is a strategic and economic trap. First, you got to look at the map. The Strait of Hormuz is essentially a closed loop. There's one way in and there's one way out. At its narrowest point, the water's just 21 nautical miles, which is about 24 statute miles. However, because of how shallow the water is and the need for regulated traffic, the actual navigable shipping lanes are about 2 miles wide in either direction. Now when you look at the coastline that is Iran, it's not flat and sandy. This is the Zagros mountain range, and it looks exactly as it sounds. It's a jagged, elevated, incredibly rugged coastline that's heavily indented with caves and coves and hardened military bunkers. In military terms, here Iran holds the ultimate high ground. Because the strait is so impossibly narrow, any ship that's transiting these waters is forced right up against the coastline, which traps it in a geographic kill zone. Because of this geography, the United States Navy loses almost all of its critical tactical advantage, which would be reaction time. We have no reaction time. If there's a threat that's launched from a hidden mountain cave, it doesn't have to travel far. So if an anti-ship cruise missile or a drone comes skimming low over the water, our most advanced naval defense systems have seconds to detect, track, and engage that target. Human operators can't physically react that fast, and even automated systems are going to be pushed to their absolute breaking point when the enemy is launching from the freaking coastline. Picture this: you've got an American destroyer transiting the strait, and suddenly 50 highly maneuverable, heavily armed fast attack boats swarm out of hidden coastal inlets. Simultaneously, midget submarines begin hunting beneath the surface. Then you've got the naval mines that are dumped directly into that narrow two-mile shipping lane. But the most modern and terrifying threat is going to be from the sky. We're talking about swarms of loitering munitions and suicide drones. These drones are cheap; they can be launched by the dozens from the backs of regular commercial trucks that are hidden on the shore. Here's the brutal math: an incoming drone can cost about 20 grand, but the US standard missile fired to intercept that drone costs several million dollars. Furthermore, our ships only carry a finite number of interceptors in their tubes. So if the enemy launches a hundred cheap drones simultaneously and a destroyer only has 90 missiles ready to fire, ten of those drones are going to get through. But let's assume the impossible. Let's assume that the US Navy flawlessly runs the gauntlet, defeats every threat, and secures the water. We still lose. And here's why: because the mission isn't to protect American warships, the mission is to protect commercial oil tankers. These supertankers are massive, incredibly slow-moving behemoths, and they possess zero on-board defenses. They've got no radar jamming equipment, no automated Gatling guns, and no ability to take evasive maneuvers. They are unarmored floating bombs. The US military can't physically attach a billion-dollar destroyer to every single commercial vessel that's moving through the gulf. So all it would take is one cheap drone slipping through the net or one dumb mine striking a hull to blow a hole in a fully loaded supertanker. The result is instant. You got a catastrophic fire, an unprecedented environmental disaster, and a burning wreck that completely blocks a narrow two-mile lane for everyone else for God knows how long. This brings us to the ultimate reality of global trade. The decision of whether the strait stays open doesn't actually get made at the Pentagon or even in Tehran. It's being made right now by executives in business suites in London. Commercial shipping runs entirely on maritime insurance. So if a vessel's hull and cargo aren't insured, it does not legally sail. Period. End of story. If the US military fired the first shot in an attempt to take over the strait, the entire region would instantly be classified as an active war zone and maritime insurance would evaporate overnight. Shipping companies could try to obtain special "war risk" coverage. However, just one look at a defenseless supertanker crawling through a drone-infested two-mile corridor is going to send premiums into the millions of dollars per voyage. It's just not viable. The math doesn't math. Ship owners will simply radio their captains and order them to drop anchor in the Arabian Sea. You can see what a mess this is. By using military force to keep the strait open, the United States would instantly trigger the exact scenario it's trying to prevent, which is a total shutdown of global energy markets and a catastrophic economic crash. But what about air power? Surely the most advanced air force in the world—stealth fighters, strategic bombers, and round-the-clock surveillance drones—could simply bomb the threat into oblivion from above. The answer is still frustratingly no, and Iran has spent decades engineering exactly that frustration. That truck problem I mentioned? That alone is pretty much unsolvable. Iran's coastal drone and missile launchers don't require a military base or a fixed installation. They come from the backs of ordinary commercial trucks that can then go anywhere along hundreds of miles of rugged coastline. Even with 24/7 surveillance overhead, you just can't guarantee that you can locate and destroy every launcher before it fires. You'd have to be perfect every single time, and Iran only needs to get lucky once. For heavier, more powerful weapons, Iran's gone underground, literally. They've constructed vast networks of fortified tunnels and underground complexes that are buried beneath hundreds of feet of solid rock. This is what Iranian commanders openly call "missile cities." Standard aerial bombs can crater the surface, but that accomplishes nothing. Of course, we do possess specialized bunker-buster munitions that are capable of penetrating deep earth, but Iran has built so many of these dispersed underground facilities that destroying them all would be a long, grinding, and inherently imperfect campaign. Here's the deeper problem that air power can't solve: Iran's coastal defense is deliberately decentralized. Missiles, drones, and fast boats are dispersed across hundreds of locations, from mountain roads to fishing villages to civilian infrastructure. There is no single command center to destroy. There's no airbase runway to crater that would collapse the entire system. Local commanders are empowered to act independently. So bombing Tehran doesn't stop a truck on the coast from launching a drone an hour later. And finally, there's the grim arithmetic of attrition. Even if you destroy a launcher, Iran holds a solid stockpile of missiles and drones that they've built up over years of sanctions-era self-sufficiency. You're not just going to destroy hardware; you're in a race to degrade their capacity faster than they can use it. While tankers are trying to transit right now today, this does not work. An air campaign in this instance doesn't avoid the war; it just starts it from a different direction. And still, it ends in the same place. Ultimately, the only way to physically secure that narrow strip of water is to completely eliminate the threat from the shore. This is why so many are saying that to actually take over the Strait of Hormuz, the United States would be forced to launch a full-scale land invasion of Southern Iran to carve out a massive coastal buffer zone. Well, we now know that Iran is a mountainous nation of 92 million people. It's larger in landmass than France, Germany, the UK, and Spain combined. It's almost the size of Alaska and twice the size of Texas. So the justifiable fear is that this could demand a brutal, protracted trillion-dollar ground war that would dwarf previous Middle East conflicts. And nobody, and I mean nobody, wants this. Which brings us to Trump's 15-point peace plan for Iran. This is a quiet parallel effort that's been moving through the diplomatic corridors of Turkey, Egypt, and Pakistan. Right now in Islamabad, Pakistan, American emissary Steve Witkoff has delivered a blueprint for peace to the regime in Tehran. It's a sweeping 15-point proposal, a document that demands profound concessions from the Islamic Republic in exchange for economic survival. Because the fate of global markets and perhaps the lives of thousands are hanging in the balance, it's vital that we understand exactly what's in this document. So here's what the US is asking of Iran. First and most critically: the nuclear question. The proposal demands that Tehran permanently abandon any pursuit of a nuclear weapon. All uranium enrichment on Iranian soil must cease immediately. Furthermore, Iran must surrender its entire stockpile of highly enriched uranium, which is around 450 kilograms, to the International Atomic Energy Agency. The fortified facilities at Natanz, Isfahan, and the deeply buried bunkers at Fordow have to be dismantled. In their place, the IAEA has to be granted full, unrestricted, and permanent access to verify compliance. Second: the dismantling of the proxy network. We know for decades Iran has fought its battles through a decentralized web of regional militias. Well, the American plan demands a complete secession of this strategy. Tehran has to immediately cut off all funding, arming, and operational direction to groups like Hezbollah, Hamas, and the Houthis. Third: the Strait of Hormuz. As mentioned, currently the Iranian military has placed a chokehold on this vital artery of global commerce, demanding extortion payments in Chinese yuan for safe passage. Well, the 15-point plan requires Iran to immediately reopen the strait, restoring it as a free, unrestricted international maritime corridor. And fourth: the ballistic missile program. The document insists that Iran severely curtail the production and range of its ballistic missiles, legally binding the nation to restrict any future use of these weapons strictly to self-defense of its own borders. If, and only if, the Iranian regime agrees to these terms, Washington has outlined a series of significant concessions. The United States agreed that they would lift the crippling international sanctions that have suffocated the Iranian economy. Furthermore, America and its allies would assist Iran in developing a strictly civilian nuclear energy program, allowing for electricity generation. This would happen at the Bushehr plant, provided the nuclear fuel is processed and stored outside of Iranian territory. Then you've got the arguably most controversial proposal, and this is the permanent removal of the diplomatic snap-back mechanism. For those of you who don't know what this is, the snap-back is the West's ultimate veto-proof weapon. It guarantees that if Iran ever cheats on a nuclear agreement, the US can instantly force the United Nations to slam crippling global sanctions back onto Tehran's economy, and neither Russia nor China can veto it. By offering to remove this, Washington is assuring Tehran that sanctions won't automatically be re-imposed on a whim. However, it also means that America is trading away its best diplomatic leverage. So those are the terms that are laid upon the table in Islamabad. It's a stark choice between economic rehabilitation and military degradation. And this is the chasm between war and peace. Is it rapidly narrowing or is it growing rapidly? Well, President Trump says there's been major progress. However, the word from Tehran indicates that the regime has rejected the proposal, labeling it a maximalist demand for total surrender. In turn, they've issued their own demands and they're insisting on absolute sovereignty over the Strait of Hormuz, financial reparations for the damage inflicted by the recent strikes, and a total withdrawal of American and Israeli forces before any ceasefire can begin. And then there's the ultimate plot twist. We're hearing through these back channels that Tehran doesn't want to talk to Steve Witkoff or Jared Kushner anymore. They blame Witkoff's team for the collapse of the nuclear talks right before the war started, citing a massive deficit of trust. Instead—this gets interesting—Iran is explicitly demanding to negotiate with Vice President JD Vance, whom they perceive as being more inclined to wrap this foreign conflict up quickly. Well, with the lines drawn, the American media has gone into overdrive, spinning this 15-point plan into completely different directions. On the left, the establishment media is framing this as a desperate scramble by the administration to save a tanking global economy. They're hammering the hypocrisy of giving up the snap-back mechanism and they're arguing that we're surrendering our ultimate leverage to Tehran just to get them to the table. They point to the Strait of Hormuz blockade as proof that Iran actually holds the geoeconomic power and that our envoys are getting played by these intermediaries. Conversely, on the right, conservative media is treating this as a masterclass in peace through strength. They're focusing on the severe demands that have been placed on Iran, like the total dismantling of their nuclear bunkers. They're framing this not as a negotiation but as terms of a surrender that's forced upon Tehran by the overwhelming success of Operation Epic Fury. To them, the UN snap-back doesn't matter when you have bunker-buster bombs, and they see this as a necessary showdown to stop China from using Iran to destroy the US dollar. So is the left right that we're desperately surrendering our leverage? Or is the right correct that we're forcing a total capitulation? Or is it a combo of both? And what does it mean that Iran is trying to bypass Witkoff to get to the Vice President? And even more to the point, who's even negotiating on behalf of Iran?
