IEA Executive Director: The global energy crisis explained | Podcast | In Good Company

Norges Bank Investment Management(105.0K) ·45:56 ·9.2K views · 2026-04-01
Dr.
TL;DR Dr. Fatih Birol warns that the world is facing the greatest energy security threat in history, surpassing the shocks of 1973, 1979, and 2022 combined. The future global economy and the AI race will be won by those who secure cheap, abundant electricity through a rapid...

TL;DR

Dr. Fatih Birol warns that the world is facing the greatest energy security threat in history, surpassing the shocks of 1973, 1979, and 2022 combined. The future global economy and the AI race will be won by those who secure cheap, abundant electricity through a rapid expansion of nuclear power, batteries, and resilient grids.

The Magnitude of the Current Energy Crisis

  • Unprecedented Scale: The current crisis is more severe than the 1973 and 1979 oil shocks and the 2022 Russian gas crisis put together.
      • Supply Losses: The world has lost 12 million barrels of oil per day, compared to the 5 million lost in each of the 1970s crises.
      • Critical Choke Point: The single most important factor for the global economy is keeping the Strait of Hormuz open.
  • Lagging Impact: Dr. Birol warns that April will be significantly worse than March because the market is still consuming "pre-war" cargoes that were already in transit.
  • Strategic Reserve Release: The IEA released 400 million barrels of oil (the largest in history).
      • Stabilization vs. Cure: This release is described as a "pain reliever" to gain time and reduce price volatility, but it is not a permanent cure for supply disruptions.
  • Vulnerable Nations: While G7 and G20 nations are taking measures, emerging and developing countries in Asia, Africa, and Latin America are at the highest risk of "foreign debt spirals" and energy rationing.

Europe’s Three Historic Strategic Mistakes

Dr. Birol identifies three specific policy failures that have compromised European economic security and competitiveness:

  • Over-reliance on Russia: Depending on a single, unreliable country for a vital commodity like natural gas.
  • The Nuclear Retreat: Turning away from nuclear power was a "historical mistake."
      • Case Study: Birol compares France’s previous plan to reduce nuclear reliance to "selling the Eiffel Tower"—liquidating a vital national asset.
  • Losing the Solar Lead: Europe pioneered solar technology 25 years ago but "dropped the ball," allowing China to dominate manufacturing and the global market.
  • Industrial Impact: European electricity prices are now 2-3 times higher than in the U.S., threatening the survival of energy-intensive legacy industries.

The "Age of Electricity" and Technological Shifts

  • Electrification Trends: Global electricity demand is growing twice as fast as total energy demand, driven by data centers, AI, and electric vehicles (EVs).
  • The Battery "Magic Wand": Birol identifies batteries as the most consequential technology.
      • Market Growth: Five years ago, EVs were 5% of global sales; last year, they reached 25%.
      • Future of Transport: Global transport is moving toward total electrification, with electric trucks expected to be the next major trend.
  • Nuclear Renaissance: A "strong comeback" for nuclear power is underway, encompassing both traditional plants and Small Modular Reactors (SMRs).
  • The Coal Paradox: Despite the green transition, coal is seeing a temporary uptick in China, India, and parts of Europe as a desperate response to high natural gas prices.

Infrastructure Risks and the AI Race

  • The "Achilles Heel" of Energy: The primary bottleneck is not power generation but Grid Capacity.
      • Current grid infrastructure is insufficient to support the massive influx of renewables, leaving many projects idle.
  • Cybersecurity: As the world electrifies, the risk of cyber-attacks on power systems becomes a top-tier systemic threat.
  • Energy as an AI Input: The winner of the AI race will be determined by the availability and price of electricity.
      • Data Center Demand: A single medium-sized data center can consume as much electricity as a town of 100,000 households.
      • Competitive Edge: If a region lacks quick, cheap power, they will fall behind in technological innovation regardless of their software capabilities.

Notable Quotes

"I saw that the decision makers, governments in Europe, but around the world, didn't understand how big the problem is and can be—not just for the energy sector, but for the global economy." — Dr. Fatih Birol
"Turning our back in Europe to nuclear power... was a historical mistake. In France, when the government program was bringing the nuclear share from 75% down to 50%, I said it is something like selling the Eiffel Tower." — Dr. Fatih Birol
"There is no AI without electricity. Full stop... Who will win this race will be determined by two factors: the technology, and the availability and the price of electricity." — Dr. Fatih Birol
"You [Norway] feel so guilty for no reason. You are a country which provides energy security. When Russia invaded Ukraine, Norway was there to support Europe... I wish there were many Norways around the world." — Dr. Fatih Birol
Hi everybody and welcome to In Good Company. I'm Nicolai Tangen, the CEO of the Norwegian Sovereign Wealth Fund. And today, we have a tremendous guest, Dr. Fatih Birol, Executive Director of the International Energy Agency. And wow, what a topical podcast this is going to be. So big welcome, Fatih. Thank you very much, Nicolai. Now, you have warned that markets and politicians are still underestimating the consequences of the crisis in the Middle East. What are they getting wrong? You know, Nicolai, we're an intergovernmental organization. We work for the governments and public institutions. After the war started, since there are many market-related dynamics, I told my colleagues and myself that for three weeks we wouldn't make any public statement in the newspapers or with journalists, even though we received as usual lots and lots of requests. I only made a statement about the oil release of the IEA. This is another story. But I saw that the decision makers and governments in Europe and around the world didn't understand how big the problem is and can be—not for the energy sector, but for the global economy. So it is the reason I decided, I think it was a week ago or so, to come up with some numbers and make people understand that this is a serious issue for all of us. The way to deal with this is first to recognize what a big problem it is. I tried to describe what the problem is, and I think it was, in my view, a good move that gave a big drive to policymakers to discuss the situation more intensely. You've said that it's the greatest global energy security threat in history and bigger than '73 and so on. Just what metrics are you looking at? So, you know, we have many energy crises, unfortunately. But when you look at the last couple of decades, three of them stand out. One of them is the '73 oil crisis, the '79 oil crisis, and the 2022 Russian invasion of Ukraine regarding gas. When you look at the oil crises of '73 and '79, in both of them, we lost each about 5 million barrels per day of oil. So 5 plus 5 is about 10. And we all know that these oil crises led to global recession in many countries and a foreign debt spiral that developing countries fell into. Today, as things stand now, we lost 12 million barrels per day. So that is more than two of those oil crises put together. In terms of natural gas, when Russia cut the gas, we lost around 75 BCM of gas. And now the amount of gas we lost is higher than the Russian gas crisis, which means the current crisis is more than all these three crises put together. Plus, if I may, in addition to this oil and gas, there are many vital commodities very important for the global economy. Petrochemicals, fertilizers, helium, and sulfur are very important for the global supply chains. This comes on top of that. If I may say one more thing, we are following the damages on the energy assets in the region. According to our database, 40 key assets have been damaged. Some of them are light, but some of them are severe and very severe. It will take some time to get back. So it is for the reason of looking at these numbers and metrics, as you said, that I decided to make statements to make people understand that we are heading towards a major disruption, and perhaps the biggest in history up to now. How will this pan out going forward? I think I was very happy that many governments understood how serious it is. And I was very happy to see that the countries are now taking measures—the G7, G20, the Europeans, and the Japanese. I was last week with the Japanese Prime Minister Kishida and the day before with the Prime Minister Albanese in Australia. Asia is hit the worst for the time being, but it will come to Europe and elsewhere. So, countries are now aware of the problem and we are discussing how to tackle the situation best. Of course, Nicolai, I should tell you that the measures we are taking, that the IEA is taking, and all the governments are taking are important. But the single most important solution is opening up the Strait of Hormuz. Now the Strait hasn't been closed totally, right? It's just open for friends. Now, what do you do when these choke points are treated like this? I think it is one of the vulnerabilities of the global energy system, both for oil and gas. We are trying to look at other alternatives. One of them was, as you may know, that on the 11th of March, I announced that we released 400 million barrels of oil to the markets, both crude oil and products. As soon as it was heard, prices went down by $18. But of course, afterwards, there were many statements here and there, and it rebounded. We have suggested many governments take some demand-side measures. It is not easy, but it is very important, such as working from home in some cases, or reducing the speed limits for cars in others. I believe there is a need for additional measures in some cases to support financially, especially the vulnerable parts of the population. But if I tell you one thing, Nicolai, we talk about Europe a lot in the newspapers, and Japan, Australia, or Korea elsewhere, but the biggest problem today I see is the emerging and developing countries. They are hit the worst. These are the countries that are importing oil and gas and their economies are hit, especially starting with Asia. It will come, of course, very soon to Africa, Latin America, and elsewhere. This is my biggest worry. How much time will it take before the effects of this are really filtering through to higher inflation? I don't know when you are releasing this podcast, but the next month is April. In two days, we are fast-tracking this one. Okay. You are really hot, Fatih. So this is coming out straight away. Okay. In April, April will be much worse than March. Why? I will explain to you. Because in March, there were already some cargoes, both oil and gas, that already left the Middle East before the war. They are still coming to the ports, coming to the countries, and they are still bringing oil or LNG and the others. In March, we have already benefited from this. But in April, there is nothing. So it means with even the modest estimates we have now, the loss of oil in April will be twice the loss of oil in March. On top of that, you have the LNG and others. So it will come through the inflation and then I think it will cut the economic growth in many countries. Especially in emerging and developing countries where they don't have hard currencies, they may be in even bigger difficulty. And we may well see, Nicolai, in many countries, the rationing of energy may be coming soon. The 400 million barrels that you released is roughly 20% of reserves, right? What would it take for you to recommend further releases? So, we are assessing the market on a daily, if not hourly basis, 24/7. If we think there is a need for crude or product, we may well intervene. I will make a suggestion to governments in that direction. The biggest problem today is the lack of jet fuel and diesel. These are the main challenges. And we are seeing it already in Asia, but soon it will come to Europe in April or maybe the beginning of May. And therefore, I am assessing the situation with my colleagues. When the time is ripe, I will make the decision to make a suggestion to the government. Is there a point where these drawdowns go from being stabilizing to destabilizing? I think currently it is stabilizing. But as I said, this 400 million barrels is the largest release we ever made in history. It is two times more than, for example, when Russia invaded Ukraine. But I have to be very honest, Nicolai, this is only helping to reduce the pain. It will not be a cure. The cure is opening up the Strait of Hormuz. This is the single most important issue. We are just helping to reduce the pain and gaining some time, maybe in other words, if I may say so. But I don't claim that our stock release will be a solution. What do you think about European governments which subsidize energy bills for their citizens? I think if it is done in a targeted way and in a temporary way, this may well be a solution. You know what is the problem in Europe? I mean, there are many problems in Europe, but one of the problems is that first of all, Europe's reflex was a bit slower than the others. They thought it is an Asia issue, or it is a Middle East issue. No, no, no. Energy markets are global. Come on. Energy markets are global. So the first issue is Europeans and the governments needed to understand that it's a major issue coming to Europe very soon. So we had to shake up the European governments. What is happening in Europe is that Europe is buying a lot of gas from the spot markets. Today, the Asian governments and Asian buyers, since they cannot have the LNG coming from the Middle East, are jumping on the spot markets. It is where Europe normally gets gas. Therefore, the prices of gas are going up in Europe. Since the European electricity prices are based on the marginal cost of natural gas, electricity prices will go up. So we will have in Europe high gas prices and high electricity prices. Therefore, I think it is important that the governments in Europe do support the vulnerable parts of the population. This should be targeted support but a temporary one. What I am afraid of, Nicolai—maybe just making one step on the politics here, which is not my professional occupation, but I have to say this—is that these high energy prices may give a fertile ground for extreme political thinking just before some of the important elections going to happen in Europe. So just a side remark, but I think it is important to register here. You've urged European governments not to ease restrictions on Russian gas, right? Do you think that's realistic if this continues into the next winter? I think for three reasons I told them it is. First one is technically it is almost not possible. For Nord Stream 1, there was a sabotage and it is technically not intact. Nord Stream 2 didn't get the licensing yet from the Germans. The others are too small. Economically, the Russian gas was mainly indexed to oil prices. And with these high oil prices we have now, Russian gas will also be expensive. And the third, and in my view maybe the most important one, is that I am saying this since almost 25 years. I was a chief economist before in this organization. Over-reliance on Russia was a big mistake. We made this mistake and we paid for it. But one time it is a mistake. If we make it a second time, it is not a mistake. LNG was part of the solution last time. Now it's kind of part of the problem. How are the dynamics of the LNG market changing? I think the LNG and general gas industry may suffer a bit of reputation damage. Why I am saying this is that gas is presented, and rightly so, as a reliable, affordable, and flexible option. But now, after 2022 Russia gas and now today, it may cast a long shadow on the gas industry. I think the gas industry has to work hard in order to prove that they are still reliable, affordable, and flexible. I think a few fuels may lose some credit or some image from what happened. The gas industry may suffer. I don't know if it's temporary or not, but I see this happening. Of course, when we look at structural terms, there will be some others who can win from this crisis in the long term. In my personal view, this may well reshape the global energy markets in the next years to come. In the IEA, you don't really publish forecasts any longer. You talk about different scenarios, right? Exactly. If you were to look at the best-case scenario for the Middle East, what does it look like? I think the Middle East countries did suffer and will suffer. When I say Middle East, I make a distinction between countries like Iraq, Lebanon, and Bahrain. They will be hit the most. The other countries, like Saudi Arabia, the Gulf countries, Emirates, and Kuwait, will recover quickly. Let me just tell you about Iraq, for example. In Iraq, 90% of the government revenues come from selling oil. Today, Iraq revenues came to very, very low levels. With these government revenues, they pay the salaries of the people working there and the pensions, and it's about 15 million people. So for Iraq to recover will be much more difficult than it will be for the Saudis or the Emirates. Like the gas industry in general, Middle East countries need to make a lot of effort to regain their reputation as a reliable supplier. This may take some time, but they did some wonders in the past and they may make it again. But I would definitely distinguish the countries with the lower capacity, such as Iraq, vis-a-vis the others. What does the worst-case scenario look like here? I think the worst-case scenario is that the Strait of Hormuz continues to be closed. And this may well lead to major damage to the global economy. This damage might be the worst for the emerging and developing countries, which have nothing to do with creating this problem. They are not a part of this problem one way or another, but they may suffer the most. This is for me the worst case. Remember the 1970s, when many countries fell into the foreign debt spiral? This had many implications—economic, political, and social. I am more worried on that front, but I hope this will never be the case and before too long the Strait of Hormuz starts to run and we can start to recover. But again, Nicolai, we have to also think worldwide. We are taking some short-term measures, but there will be some long-term structural reactions to this crisis in the energy world. Tell us about them. Because we could see changes in the renewables, nuclear, and so on. Could you just lay out what you think will be the changes? I mean, it is too early to give very specific things, but when I look at the 1970s, I see at least three important changes. One was nuclear power. There was a big wave of nuclear build as a response to the oil crisis, about 170 gigawatts. This is about 40% of the nuclear power plants we have today, which were built as a response to the oil crisis in Europe, Japan, Korea, the US, Canada, and elsewhere. This was number one. Number two was in the car industry. Before the 1970s, an average car needed about 20 liters to drive 100 kilometers. But afterwards, fuel efficiency standards came as a response to this crisis, and very soon, it was 10 liters you needed. It was halved. The third one was related to domestic production, like the North Sea in Norway and other countries. The biggest growth in the North Sea came as a response to this oil crisis because of higher prices and because governments gave tax incentives and speedy licensing. At least three things happened. And this time, what I expect is that nuclear is going to make a comeback, and this comeback will be even stronger for both traditional and small modular reactors. Second, I expect, especially in Asia, the electrification of the transportation sector. I also expect that renewables will be growing with batteries even faster. In addition to those, I wouldn't be surprised if in some cases coal makes a big push in China, Indonesia, and India as a response to high natural gas prices. These are areas where we may see a response. Well, let's unpack each of these. As we electrify everything, are we building more vulnerable systems or more resilient systems? It depends on how you do it. If you make an electrification of the energy systems, we will still use oil and gas for many years to come. But I am saying since almost one and a half years that we are entering the age of electricity. Electricity demand is growing two times faster than total energy demand. It is coming with AI, data centers, and electric cars. We need to build grids, resilient grids around the world. But this would mean one of my worries is the increasing number of cyberattacks on the power systems. We have to be careful because the world is becoming a dangerous place and the energy system is unfortunately at the target of many bad actors in the world. What do you think is the biggest systemic risk in the electrified energy system? One thing is the more innocent one: government failure in not having enough grids. Last year the world built a huge amount of renewables, but four times of these renewables were waiting idle because there was not enough grid capacity. They finished the solar panel or the wind, but they don't have any grids. This is one risk I see. The other problem in addition to the grid problem is, as I mentioned, the cyber security. Cyberattacks on the grids are my worry and our numbers show they are increasing. We bought 25% of one of the grid operators in Germany. What would it take to attract more capital into this sector? I think you have to work—and I don't talk about any specific company—but if we want grids to be stronger, there is a need for governments to make it attractive for investors. Currently, unfortunately, governments gave a push to building renewables, but they forgot that we need grids to bring this electricity from these power plants to the households and the industry. It is time for them to put the investment framework in place to make it attractive. They have to facilitate and simplify the licensing and permitting processes. These two things would be needed. I've seen numbers which indicate electricity demand is up 40% by 2035. How much can the infrastructure actually cope with? The issue today, unlike what the world thought in the past, is not building power plants because solar, wind, or gas is not so difficult. The difficult part now is that the world is not coping with building grids. This is the Achilles heel of the age of electricity. You also mentioned coal. What does the coal scenario look like? Coal scenario means in fact China scenario because about 55% of the coal today is used in China. Coal demand was more or less flat in the last few years and we were expecting it to go down. But with this crisis we are in, I wouldn't be surprised if we see an uptick in coal use around the world starting with China. In the United States, we see coal use is rebounding and I wouldn't be surprised if even in European countries, in this urgent situation, countries would make use of their coal reserves. And then on nuclear, Europe has spent two decades building down its nuclear capacity. How big a mistake has that been? Nicolai, for Europe, I think we made three historically strategic mistakes. One of them I mentioned to you: over-reliance on one single country for a very important commodity. The second one is the nuclear. In the end of the 1990s, about one-third of European electricity came from nuclear, and it is going down now to about 15%. We turned our back in Europe to nuclear power. It was a historical mistake for me. Now we are seeing this in every country. In France, when President Macron took office, the government program was bringing the nuclear share from 75% down to 50%. At that time, Le Monde asked me what I thought. I said it is something like selling the Eiffel Tower. It is a national asset of France. Many countries made that mistake. The third mistake is that today, of all power plants installed in the world, 75% was solar. This is mainly manufactured in China. But 25 years ago, it was Europe that started building the solar panels. They dropped the ball and China took it over and now China is dominating the game. These three mistakes cost us a lot in terms of our competitiveness, in terms of our economic security, and I would say foreign policy. If you were to expand on that and include batteries, what do you think? At the IEA, we follow about 600 technologies on a day-to-day basis. If you ask me to choose one to touch with a magic wand and make it economic and widely used tomorrow, I would choose batteries. Because it will change many things, including the dynamics of the transportation sector. Europe is behind China today, but for advanced batteries, there is a chance for Europe to come back. I am in discussion with the European governments and I see that Europe is now looking at this option to be one of the frontrunners. I think this would be a good thing for economic security. The technology advances in batteries are astonishing, right? Amazing. And AI will help to accelerate this. So, electrical vehicles—Europe again had a good start. Now the Western world is retrenching on ambitions here. What are going to be the implications? Five years ago, 5% of all the cars sold in the world were electric. Last year, it was 25%. This will go very quickly. China is the leader, but European car manufacturers may need some flexibility. But it is very clear for me: the transportation sector's future is electric. What happened in the Middle East, in my view, will only accelerate this. If the European companies want to be still leading companies and part of the competition globally, the future is electric. If we make another podcast in two years, we will talk about electric trucks. I follow this very closely and China is making a lot of advancements here. Globally, I think the electric cars will be penetrating the car markets even faster than it was before after this crisis. Now, if we add together everything we spoke about so far, the end result is that electricity prices are higher. European electricity prices are two to three times higher than the US and way higher than China. What are the implications for Europe as an industrial power? I think Europe's competitiveness is at stake. I believe there are some legacy industries in Europe that governments need to support. Some of them may not be able to compete with the others, especially those very energy-intensive ones. Europe should support some of the selected legacy industries but at the same time should have a roadmap for tomorrow's industries and push them strongly. I believe I should also mention this because there is a lot of Europe bashing. I think Europe has very strong economic fundamentals. Being a 450 million single market and a democracy, with the right industrial policies combined with the right energy policies, Europe can go through this. Just any reflection on the link between AI and electricity prices? AI demand is increasing energy prices, but at the same time higher energy prices are very important going forward in terms of the usage of AI. It's a huge input cost in the whole thing, right? Definitely. We have chosen AI as a key topic to focus on four years ago. Why? Very simple. Because there is no AI without electricity. Full stop. One medium-sized data center consumes electricity as much as a town with 100,000 households. And you need electricity 24/7. There is a big race in AI between the US, China, and Europe. In my view, AI is perhaps the most consequential technological innovation of humanity. Who will win this race will be determined by technology and by the availability and the price of electricity. If you don't have a lot of electricity, and if you don't have it cheap and quick, then you may be behind your competitors. I see that Europe has to work a lot on this second part. Technology part, okay, but also in the electricity part, maybe harder than the US and China. Fatih, you guys have got a lot of things right, but you got a couple of things quite wrong too, right? In particular the speed of the clean energy transition. We got a lot of critics. Some people say we are too optimistic when it comes to clean energy. What we are doing is looking at different projections and scenarios. What we have said in the past—I think 10 years ago—was that we were rather conservative in terms of the penetration of solar power. China surprised everybody. It is not the world, but China surprised everybody. Except for that, I think we got solar, wind, nuclear power, oil, and gas just right. We don't pretend to get everything right. We just put the data on the table. If we are wrong, we say we were wrong. I have a motto: Data always wins. Sometimes it takes some time, but at the end of the day, data always wins. We put the data and the facts on the table for the governments and the investors. What would you say about your predictions for fossil fuel production? How right have you been on those? Fossil fuel production? We were right in how the gas demand was. We were completely right on the oil front. We said that oil demand will slow down because of China and the penetration of electric cars. Coal we said would plateau, and it is plateaued. Renewables, we saw that the renewables are growing very strongly. It is the reason we make different scenarios. If you go this way, this is the result. If you want to go this way, these are the results. We have different projections for the policymakers around the world. Now Norway, of course, we made money from oil and gas. Should Norway continue to explore for more oil and gas? It is completely up to the Norwegian government and Norwegian people to decide. But I want to say a few things because I like the Norwegian energy people. I also like the Norwegian football, Bodø/Glimt. When I talk with my Norwegian friends, I say that you feel so guilty for no reason. You are a country which provides energy security. When Russia invaded Ukraine, Norway was there to support Europe. In terms of climate change, Norway has a very ambitious program. In terms of helping the developing countries, Norway is there. Your prime minister supported me on the clean cooking in Africa. I think Norway is doing a good job and you have electric cars. You don't have to feel guilty. You have to be proud of what you do in the global energy security and what you do for the poor around the world. I wish there were many Norways around the world, to be honest with you, but there are not. What are the similarities between Bodø/Glimt and IEA? We are a modest organization with a core budget of 22 million euros. With a very small budget, we made miracles in my view. Why? Because we have excellent teamwork. That is a similarity with Bodø/Glimt. Bodø/Glimt is a Norwegian football team which wrote history in my mind. The difference is that Bodø/Glimt is in the Champions League. We are still the champion in the energy world. So this is a small difference there. Fatih, that's a great place to end. You are still the champion in the energy world. Hopefully that will continue for many years and you are for sure doing a great job. Big thank you for taking the time. Thank you. Take care. Bye-bye.

Prince Harry Showing Confidence on Stage in Washington, D.C.

LMT(86.7K) ·24:14 ·5.8K views · 2026-04-01
Prince Harry calls for a fundamental shift from "privacy by extraction" to "privacy by design," arguing that systemic accountability and human-centered regulation are essential to protect society—particularly children—from the harms of social media and emerging artificial intelligence.
TL;DR Prince Harry calls for a fundamental shift from "privacy by extraction" to "privacy by design," arguing that systemic accountability and human-centered regulation are essential to protect society—particularly children—from the harms of social media and emerging artificial...

TL;DR

Prince Harry calls for a fundamental shift from "privacy by extraction" to "privacy by design," arguing that systemic accountability and human-centered regulation are essential to protect society—particularly children—from the harms of social media and emerging artificial intelligence.

The Normalization of Privacy Exploitation

  • Personal Context: Prince Harry highlighted his seven-year litigation against three UK media organizations over systemic and unlawful privacy invasions, noting that the exploitation of personal lives has become a "normalized" commercial tactic.
  • Systemic Failure: Digital privacy is not merely a personal issue but a foundational one that underpins global trust, safety, and societal stability.
  • The Extraction Model: Current tech systems are not failing by accident; they are succeeding at their intended design: maximizing data extraction for profit.
      • The Duke argued that the "alignment between power, incentives, and responsibility" is what has truly failed.

Rebutting the "Innovation vs. Regulation" Narrative

  • The Safety Precedent: Prince Harry rejected the tech industry's claim that guardrails stifle innovation, citing aviation, medicine, and finance as industries that succeeded specifically because trust and safety were engineered into the system.
      • Scalability: Safety does not limit growth; it makes it scalable and sustainable.
  • The Illusion of Consent: Current digital participation requires a "structural surrender" of personal information.
      • When access to essential digital life depends on non-negotiable terms, consent becomes a formality rather than a safeguard.
  • Closing Legal Gaps: Regulation often fails when it focuses on the "letter" of the law while allowing companies to bypass the "intent" through design workarounds.

The Human Cost of Algorithmic Harm

  • Vulnerable Populations: The briefing highlighted the "irreparable harm" caused by platforms that algorithmically target children with content leading to eating disorders, drug overdoses, bullying, and suicide.
      • Case Studies: Mentioned recent legal victories against Meta and YouTube as evidence that platforms were built to exploit rather than protect.
  • Systemic Addiction: The Duke challenged the narrative that digital harm is a "parenting failure" or a "lack of discipline," pointing to a hundred-billion-dollar industry dedicated to studying and nudging human behavior.
  • Accountability Delays: Some parents have waited over five years to access a deceased child's phone due to platform privacy rules that protect the company's data control rather than the user's family.

The AI Opportunity and Path Forward

  • A Rare Inflection Point: As the world enters the era of Artificial Intelligence, there is a "rare opportunity" to reverse the mistakes of the last 20 years by governing systems *before* they are fully deployed.
      • Privacy by Design: Accountability and safety must be embedded into the architecture of AI from the start, rather than being retrofitted after harm occurs.
  • American Influence: Given the global reach of U.S. tech, the standards set in Washington D.C. define the digital environment for the rest of the world.
  • Human-Centered Leadership: The Duke called for more "EQ" (Emotional Intelligence) and moral leadership to prioritize human outcomes over "extra zeros at the end of the quarter."
"Safety didn't limit innovation; it made it scalable."

— Prince Harry, Duke of Sussex

"When access depends on agreement, and agreement isn't negotiable, consent becomes a formality, not a safeguard... is this consent or is it structural surrender?"

— Prince Harry, Duke of Sussex

"We cannot wait for behavior to magically change when incentives are structured around avoiding accountability and maximizing profit."

— Prince Harry, Duke of Sussex

"For the last 20 years, we've built digital systems first and governed them later. AI gives us a rare opportunity to reverse that sequence."

— Prince Harry, Duke of Sussex

Hello everyone and a very warm welcome to LMT YouTube channel. Leading with humanity in a digital world. Under the soft glow of conference lights in Washington D.C., Prince Harry stepped onto the stage with a quiet gravity. His presence felt less like royalty and more like responsibility. Dressed with understated elegance, he carried the tone of someone who has truly listened—not to abstract numbers, but to real grief, to the voices of families whose lives have been shattered by the unseen consequences of the digital world. Good morning everyone. Good morning everyone. There it is. Thank you very much to IAPP for inviting me and for the work that so many of you are doing on the front lines of digital privacy. At a time when there is so much devastation and suffering happening in the world, it might seem strange to be speaking about privacy. But I see it differently. I see privacy as a foundational issue, one that underpins trust, safety, and the stability of our societies. And getting this right will have global consequences. Now, my connection to privacy and the lack of it begins in a different place than most—from birth. And you may know that I've spent the last seven years in litigation against three media organizations in the UK over their systemic and unlawful invasions of privacy, as well as the cover-up of it dating back to the early 2000s. The worst examples of an industry that too often behaves as though it owns people's privacy and feels it gets to decide what constitutes the public interest. And unfortunately, I have come to learn that this exploitation has become normalized. The breaches of privacy have morphed into commercial tactics across industries, notably the tech industry with innovations like social media platforms and advances in artificial intelligence. As you can perhaps tell, I have nothing to gain from taking on powerful institutions. In many ways, it comes at a personal and reputational cost for me, my wife, and our children. But is it worth it? Absolutely. Because this is about more than one individual. It is about the systems that shape and influence all of our lives. Across my work in business and philanthropy, I've seen how the current model of technology isn't just failing to support progress; it's actively setting it back for communities around the world. Over the last several years, I've had countless conversations with experts like you all, along with parents and young people who have experienced first-hand the loss of privacy while coming of age in our modern, increasingly online world. I've done a deep dive into the tech-fueled world in which my children, all of our children, are growing up in. I've heard harrowing stories of how time spent on the platforms of today's tech giants is leading to grave and irreversible harm. Stories that all but cancelled out any entertainment or other value those technologies were offering. Stories about how the existing so-called solutions calling for bans and blocks and parent toolkits were unrealistic and ineffective, given how interwoven tech has become across every facet of life. Stories about fears that without adequately addressing privacy across social media, we would never be able to reckon with the challenges of the next technological chapter: artificial intelligence. Simultaneously, I am seeing constant headlines about state-sponsored surveillance, doxing of election workers and civil rights activists, and massive privacy breaches featuring social security numbers and sensitive health information. The more I learned, the more I felt my own experience with having my privacy owned was just one small example of an extraction problem that we can miss its gravity if we aren't forced to stare at it. But stare at it we must. The challenge ahead of us all is significant and worthy of our attention. For anyone outside of this room thinking it won't affect them, think again. We are confronting this challenge and we are stronger together. This room of 4,000 people is evidence of that. This room of people with the power to prioritize privacy. You, collectively, can rewire our entire system. The fact that humans are the architects of this technology means that humans are in the position to design a better future. And a world where the right to privacy is not just an ideal, but the norm. So let's take a step back to focus on how we got here. The uncomfortable reality is that many of the privacy-related harms we're now grappling with today are not the result of failure, but the result of success. Systems optimized for extraction are doing exactly what they were designed to do. And this is no longer a debate, no longer opinion; it is fact. So what has failed isn't the technology, but the alignment between power, incentives, and responsibility. Because power, especially at this scale, demands responsibility. And where responsibility is absent, harm becomes predictable and inevitable. Which means the challenge in front of us is not simply better compliance, but better alignment between business models and human outcomes. And to get to that alignment, we have to peel back another layer because business models don't just appear, just like technology doesn't just come to be. These systems didn't emerge on their own. They were designed. And the outcomes we're seeing, good and bad, are a direct result of the choices built into them. That's not what we're told most of the time. We're told, by those that are incentivized to tell us so, that technology needs room to breathe; that innovation can't have guardrails or else it will be held back from its potential. But history shows the opposite. The most transformative industries—aviation, medicine, finance—did not succeed in the absence of safeguards. They succeeded because trust was engineered into the system. Safety didn't limit innovation; it made it scalable. We've regulated aspects of data use, but we haven't changed the fundamental reality. Participation in the digital world still requires surrendering control over your personal information. When access depends on agreement, and agreement isn't negotiable, consent becomes a formality, not a safeguard. And even where laws exist, systems are often designed around the edges, perhaps complying with the letter while bypassing the intent. Regulation without closing those gaps risks legitimizing the very dynamics it was meant to correct. So why should some humans, especially humans with endless resources, be allowed to pass on answering hard questions when their profit is powered by the very humans they refuse to protect? Why should they receive privacy protections while victims of their actions and decisions are exploited, blamed, and harmed? From the outside looking in, I can understand that some questions around privacy are incredibly complex. Questions like: given the sheer amount of personal identifying information of children shared on social media without their consent, how can they easily reclaim their privacy before it is used to train LLMs or worse, used to fulfill the darkest motives? How did we travel so far down the path of the customer experience that we ended up surveilling children and adults in nearly every aspect of their lives? And in a world increasingly shaped by artificial intelligence where technology will enhance our efficiency and eventually reshape how we live entirely, will personal privacy still be considered worthy of protection? And how do we keep profit-seeking companies from using the banner of privacy to refuse basic transparency about their back-end practices and what they know about how their technology affects users? These are daunting but necessary questions. And it is a relief that there are professionals across the world dedicating their careers to answering them, many of whom are in this room today. But it is also a false notion that every question about privacy is complicated. I refuse to accept that. Some questions have simple answers. Some questions should not even have to be asked. Like, should a parent have to worry about their child's data being sold to a predator? Should a person's voice or face or innermost thoughts be irrefutably their own? Should a young girl have to worry that the man across the room is secretly recording and undressing her using AI through the lens of a pair of smart glasses? Should a government or corporate giant be able to exploit personal privacy to compromise people's personal safety or sanity? Should powerful people have more privacy rights than the people who give them that power? The answer to these questions is simply no. Should people feel safe in their own homes with their own devices? Is the right to privacy inseparable from a person's ability to exercise free will over their own life? Should we have agency over our own lives? The answer to these questions is simply yes. And yet none of this is playing out simply. Let's not let anyone who has an interest in keeping it complex allow us to forget those simple questions with simple answers. Too often, companies want to lean on downstream complexities to avoid upstream accountability. And if you look at the terms that govern many of today's platforms, the imbalance becomes even clearer. Users are asked to grant broad, often irrevocable rights over their data and content. Systems infer who we are, not just what we share, but from how we behave. And the rules themselves can change at any time, with continued use treated as approval and acceptance. Companies conflate repeated use with trust of the platform itself. At that point, we have to ask: is this consent or is it structural surrender? Yesterday, we heard from one of the major companies on this stage say that in the age of AI, we will need to go toward "privacy by experimentation" or "privacy by innovation." Slick slogans with a sinister undertone. Sort of like being in 'The Good Place'. What about privacy by design? Safety by design has been the call to action of the parents and young people we work with. You would think it would be exactly what we all want to get to. But what's getting in the way? The loss of extra zeros at the end of the quarter? Just last week, two historic and precedent-setting accountability lawsuits were decided and won against major tech companies Meta and YouTube. And about bloody time. A jury confirmed what parents have felt and experts have said all along: the systems driving our social media platforms have been built to exploit, not protect. And the people at the heart of it have always known it. Finally, some truth and accountability has arrived. My wife and I had the opportunity to spend time with many of the parents in the Los Angeles case as it proceeded in the courtroom each day. Families we've come to know over the years while learning more about how technology has stolen their kids' lives. Every one of those families and more like them has experienced irreparable harm because of the way technology intersected with their child's life. The way technology followed their children's behavior so closely that platforms algorithmically targeted them with the very dangers they were most susceptible to, leading to eating disorders, drug overdoses, bullying, dangerous viral challenges, and in many cases, suicide. Some parents have had to wait more than five years—five years—to gain access to their child's phone and apps because of the platform's own privacy rules. Parents were left searching for answers, blaming themselves until they eventually discovered the harmful content that social media apps were pushing to their sons and daughters at a time when they were most vulnerable, leading to their death. We're told it's a lack of discipline, that it's a parenting failure or a mental health problem, that users should simply opt out. But that framing again intentionally shifts responsibility away from the systems designed to capture and hold our attention in the first place. Their stories are impossible to ignore, and they will almost certainly appear in history books one day. But more questions hang over these stories. Will they actually inspire change? Will companies and governments that are currently pushing to build technology that extracts the maximum possible value from our lives decide on their own that enough is enough? Or will meaningful change only happen if we choose to demand it? And that's a choice that rightfully rests with us. This room knows the answer. We cannot wait for behavior to magically change when incentives are structured around avoiding accountability and maximizing profit. And the system itself is designed to keep people addicted. When powerful people continually show their desire to turn our real world into a social media world; a world where anonymity is used to harm, a world without laws or protection, without consequence, without decency; a world without truth and reality becomes a world of chaos and division. And we have to rewrite the script. We have to be strategic, put rules in place, and Trojan horse in protection if necessary. We have to build systems of governance that actually protect people. And we have to build a culture that demands real, meaningful privacy. Because the truth is that it should not take horrific stories about children taking their own lives for us to care about how our information is used. All of us have experienced some version of the same dynamic. We open an app intending to spend five minutes; 40 minutes later, we're still there, watching content we didn't ask for, wondering why we couldn't just close the screen. And we blame ourselves; we say we lack discipline. But the reality is that we've built systems that begin with engagement and then scale through increasingly precise behavioral prediction and monetization. There is an entire industry worth hundreds of billions of dollars dedicated to keeping us exactly where we are—studying us, predicting us, nudging us towards behaviors that serve someone else's outcome. And increasingly, these systems influence not just what we see, but how we think, feel, and engage with the world, with little transparency into how deliberately those outcomes are engineered and in whose interests they operate. This is not freedom. This is not normal. And this is not okay. Trust is part of what allowed the digital space to scale in the first place. But that trust has been broken. Not suddenly, but steadily, as people realize these systems don't operate the way they were promised. And still we continue, because opting out is no longer realistic. The system no longer relies on trust to function, yet we rely on it for connection. That should concern all of us. So the question isn't whether trust is broken; it's whether we're willing to rebuild it for everyone's sake and what that will take. There is also something important about this moment. We're not just living through another phase of technological change. We are standing at the beginning of the next world-shaping shift. Artificial intelligence will transform nearly every part of our lives. And that transformation gives us something rare: a chance to do things differently. For the last 20 years, we've built digital systems first and governed them later. AI gives us a rare opportunity to reverse that sequence; to embed accountability, privacy, and safety into the architecture from the start, not retrofit it after harm has already occurred. We will not get many chances to correct course. And having this conference here in Washington D.C. is fitting. As a Brit living in the United States, I've been struck by the global reach of American tech. The standards set here increasingly define the digital environment everywhere, even in countries trying to protect their own citizens and their own democracies. And when that influence scales faster than safeguards, the impact is felt far beyond U.S. borders. We can choose data sovereignty instead of data surveillance. We can choose innovation that benefits everyone, not just a handful of companies creating consolidated wealth. We can invest in human-centered technology led by human-centered leadership. We need more EQ, more leadership, and moral leadership that our world so desperately needs, in an industry that we can actually be proud of. These choices and changes require breaking out of groupthink and rejecting a story that we have been told over and over again. We've somewhat regulated data collection, but we have not yet governed the systems that act on that data. It requires anticipating the arguments we're about to hear about advances in technology and AI and any other new product rolling out on the market, and being ready to respond, reject, and demand a different reality. So, as you go about your time together this week, ask yourselves not whether privacy will matter in the age of AI, but whether we will build systems where it is impossible to ignore. And thank you for being the brave leaders that we all need. At the International Association of Privacy Professionals conference, his words did not rush; they lingered. He spoke about a growing crisis—the harms linked to social media and the devastating stories of parents who have lost their children. This was not distant advocacy. This was personal, human. He emphasized that accountability must go beyond empty promises. It must mean systems that answer for their impact, remedies that truly reach those who suffer, and solutions brave enough to confront the root causes, not just the surface. Then a pause, a shift in tone. Amid the weight of it all, a quiet sense of hope emerged. When asked what gives him hope, he didn't point to institutions or policies. He pointed to the people in the room: the researchers, the policymakers, the advocates, the ones willing to challenge what is broken and build something better. "It's you." Not spoken loudly, but deeply felt. He closed with gratitude, simple, genuine, unpolished. Taking on powerful institutions is like moving an oil tanker. And you just have to stick with it. And the longer you stick with it, more you stick with it, and more you come together to achieve that goal, the faster it will happen. And by this point, it's already taken far too long, but I do feel like we're on the cusp of something transformational. A reminder that real change doesn't come from speeches alone. It comes from persistence, from courage, from people who refuse to accept harm as inevitable. And in that room, hope didn't feel distant. It felt human.

"My connection to privacy, and the lack of it, begins from birth": Prince Harry's Full Speech

HELLO!(220.0K) ·21:14 ·182 views · 2026-04-01
Prince Harry calls for a systemic shift in the tech industry, moving away from extractive business models that prioritize profit over privacy and toward a "Safety by Design" framework that protects users—especially children—as we enter the era of Artificial Intelligence.
TL;DR Prince Harry calls for a systemic shift in the tech industry, moving away from extractive business models that prioritize profit over privacy and toward a "Safety by Design" framework that protects users—especially children—as we enter the era of Artificial Intelligence. ...

TL;DR

Prince Harry calls for a systemic shift in the tech industry, moving away from extractive business models that prioritize profit over privacy and toward a "Safety by Design" framework that protects users—especially children—as we enter the era of Artificial Intelligence.

The Normalization of Privacy Exploitation

  • Personal Context: Prince Harry links his lifelong experience with media intrusion to the broader digital privacy crisis, noting he has spent seven years in litigation against three UK media organizations for systemic, unlawful invasions of privacy dating back to the early 2000s.
  • Commercialization of Extraction: He argues that the exploitation of personal data has moved beyond the media into a "normalized" commercial tactic used by tech giants to fuel social media platforms and AI innovations.
  • Structural Surrender: Harry challenges the current definition of digital consent.
      • When participation in modern life requires agreeing to non-negotiable terms, consent becomes a "formality, not a safeguard."
      • Companies often conflate "repeated use" of a platform with "trust," ignoring the fact that users often have no realistic choice but to opt in.

The Human Cost and Algorithmic Harm

  • Impact on Youth: Through his work in philanthropy and business, Harry highlights "harrowing stories" of technology leading to irreversible harms, including eating disorders, drug overdoses, bullying, and suicide.
  • The "Blame Shifting" Narrative: He rejects the industry claim that tech-related harms are the result of "parenting failure" or a "lack of discipline," asserting instead that systems are intentionally designed to capture and hold attention through precise behavioral prediction.
  • Case Study in Accountability:
      • Legal Precedents: He cites recent successful lawsuits against Meta and YouTube as evidence that platforms are built to exploit rather than protect.
      • Access Barriers: He highlights the plight of parents who have had to wait over five years to gain access to a deceased child’s phone due to the platforms’ own restrictive privacy rules, which protected the data but shielded the harmful content that led to the child's death.

Reframing Innovation and Regulation

  • Engineering Trust: Harry disputes the tech industry’s claim that "guardrails" stifle innovation.
      • He compares the tech sector to aviation, medicine, and finance, industries that only became scalable and transformative because safety and trust were engineered into their core systems.
  • Safety by Design: He contrasts "Privacy by Design" with recent industry slogans like "Privacy by Experimentation," which he labels as "slick slogans with a sinister undertone."
  • Upstream Accountability: He calls for companies to stop leaning on "downstream complexities" (the difficulty of managing content) to avoid "upstream accountability" (the responsibility for how the system was designed).

The AI Inflection Point and Global Leadership

  • A Rare Opportunity: As AI begins to reshape society, Harry argues we have a unique chance to reverse the "build first, govern later" approach of the last 20 years.
      • Proactive Architecture: Accountability and safety must be embedded into the architecture of AI from the start, rather than retrofitting them after harm has occurred.
  • Global Influence: Speaking in Washington D.C., he notes that American tech standards define the digital environment globally. He calls for:
      • Data Sovereignty: Choosing user control over data surveillance.
      • Human-Centered Leadership: A move toward "Moral Leadership" and "EQ" in tech to ensure innovation benefits the public rather than just consolidating wealth.
"The fact that humans are the architects of this technology means that humans are in the position to design a better future and a world where the right to privacy is not just an ideal, but the norm."

— Prince Harry, Duke of Sussex

"Safety didn't limit innovation, it made it scalable."

— Prince Harry, Duke of Sussex

"Participation in the digital world still requires surrendering control over your personal information. And where access depends on agreement, and agreement isn't negotiable, consent becomes a formality, not a safeguard."

— Prince Harry, Duke of Sussex

"We cannot wait for behavior to magically change when incentives are structured around avoiding accountability and maximizing profit."

— Prince Harry, Duke of Sussex

Good morning, everyone. Good morning, everyone. There it is. Thank you very much to IAPP for inviting me and for the work that so many of you are doing on the front lines of digital privacy. At a time when there is so much devastation and suffering happening in the world, it might seem strange to be speaking about privacy. But I see it differently. I see privacy as a foundational issue, one that underpins trust, safety, and the stability of our societies. Getting this right will have global consequences. Now, my connection to privacy and the lack of it begins in a different place than most. It begins from birth. You may know that I've spent the last seven years in litigation against three media organizations in the UK over their systemic and unlawful invasions of privacy, as well as the cover-up of it, dating back to the early 2000s. These are the worst examples of an industry that too often behaves as though it owns people's privacy and feels it gets to decide what constitutes the public interest. Unfortunately, I have come to learn that this exploitation has become normalized. The breaches of privacy have morphed into commercial tactics across industries, notably the tech industry, with innovations like social media platforms and advances in artificial intelligence. As you can perhaps tell, I have nothing to gain from taking on powerful institutions. In many ways, it comes at a—comes at a personal and reputational cost for me, my wife, and our children. But is it worth it? Absolutely. Because this is about more than one individual. It is about the systems that shape and influence all of our lives. Across my work in business and philanthropy, I've seen how the current model of technology isn't just failing to support progress; it's actively setting it back for communities around the world. Over the last several years, I've had countless conversations with experts like you all, along with parents and young people who have experienced firsthand the loss of privacy while coming of age in our modern, increasingly online world. I've done a deep dive into the tech-fueled world in which my children, all of our children, are growing up. I've heard harrowing stories of how time spent on the platforms of today's tech giants is leading to grave and irreversible harm. These are stories that all but canceled out any entertainment or other value those technologies were offering. I have heard stories about how the existing so-called solutions—calling for bans and blocks and parent toolkits—were unrealistic and ineffective, given how interwoven tech has become across every facet of life. I've heard stories about fears that without adequately addressing privacy across social media, we would never be able to reckon with the challenges of the next technological chapter: artificial intelligence. Simultaneously, I am seeing constant headlines about state-sponsored surveillance, doxing of election workers and civil rights activists, and massive privacy breaches featuring social security numbers and sensitive health information. The more I learned, the more I felt my own experience with having my privacy owned was just one small example of an extraction problem. We can—that it—that we can miss its gravity if we aren't forced to stare at it. But stare at it we must. The challenge ahead of us all is significant and worthy of our attention. For anyone outside of this room thinking it won't affect them, think again. The good news is that we are confronting this challenge and we are stronger together. This room of 4,000 people is evidence of that. This is a room of people with the power to prioritize privacy. You collectively can rewire our entire system. The fact that humans are the architects of this technology means that humans are in the position to design a better future. We can design a world where the right to privacy is not just an ideal, but the norm. So let's take a step back to focus on how we got here. The uncomfortable reality is that many of the privacy-related harms we're now grappling with today are not the result of failure, but the result of success. Systems optimized for extraction are doing exactly what they were designed to do. This is no longer a debate, no longer opinion; it is fact. So what has failed isn't the technology, but the alignment between power, incentives, and responsibility. Power, especially at this scale, demands responsibility. Where responsibility is absent, harm becomes predictable and inevitable. Which means the challenge in front of us is not simply better compliance, but better alignment between business models and human outcomes. To get to that alignment, we have to peel back another layer. Business models don't just appear, just like technology doesn't just come to be. These systems didn't emerge on their own. They were designed. The outcomes we're seeing, good and bad, are a direct result of the choices built into them. But that's not what we're told most of the time. We're told, by those that are incentivized to tell us so, that technology needs room to breathe. They say that innovation can't have guardrails, or else it will be held back from its potential. But history shows the opposite. The most transformative industries—aviation, medicine, finance—did not succeed in the absence of safeguards. They succeeded because trust was engineered into the system. Safety didn't limit innovation; it made it scalable. Now, we have regulated aspects of data use, but we haven't changed the fundamental reality. Participation in the digital world still requires surrendering control over your personal information. Where access depends on agreement, and agreement isn't negotiable, consent becomes a formality, not a safeguard. Even where laws exist, systems are often designed around the edges. Perhaps companies are complying with the letter while bypassing the intent. But regulation without closing those gaps risks legitimizing the very dynamics it was meant to correct. Why should some humans, especially humans with endless resources, be allowed to pass on answering hard questions? Their profit is powered by the very humans they refuse to protect. Why should they receive privacy protections while victims of their actions and decisions are exploited, blamed, and harmed? From the outside looking in, I can understand that some questions around privacy are incredibly complex. Questions like: given the sheer amount of personal identifying information of children shared on social media without their consent, how can they easily reclaim their privacy before it is used to train LLMs or worse, used to fulfill the darkest motives? How did we travel so far down the path of the custom experience that we ended up surveilling children and adults in nearly every aspect of their lives? In a world increasingly shaped by artificial intelligence, where technology will enhance our efficiency and eventually reshape how we live entirely, will personal privacy still be considered worthy of protection? And how do we keep profit-seeking companies from using the banner of privacy to refuse basic transparency about their back-end practices? How do we learn what they know about how their technology affects users? These are daunting but necessary questions. It is a relief that there are professionals across the world dedicating their careers to answering them, many of whom are in this room today. But it is also a false notion that every question about privacy is complicated. I refuse to accept that. Some questions have simple answers. Some questions should not even have to be asked. Like: should a parent have to worry about their child's data being sold to a predator? Should a person's voice or face or innermost thoughts be irrefutably their own? Should a young girl have to worry that the man across the room is secretly recording and undressing her using AI through the lens of a pair of smart glasses? Should a government or corporate giant be able to exploit personal privacy to compromise people's personal safety or sanity? Should powerful people have more privacy rights than the people who give them that power? The answer to these questions is simply no. So, what about these? Should people feel safe in their own homes with their own devices? Is the right to privacy inseparable from a person's ability to exercise free will over their own life? Should we have agency over our own lives? The answer to these questions is simply yes. And yet none of this is playing out simply. Let's not let anyone who has an interest in keeping it complex allow us to forget those simple questions with simple answers. Too often, companies want to lean on downstream complexities to avoid upstream accountability. If you look at the terms that govern many of today's platforms, the imbalance becomes even clearer. Users are asked to grant broad, often irrevocable rights over their data and content. Systems infer who we are, not just what we share, but from how we behave. The rules themselves can change at any time, with continued use treated as approval and acceptance. Companies conflate repeated use with trust of the platform itself. But at that point we have to ask: is this consent, or is it structural surrender? Yesterday we heard from one of the major companies on this stage say that in the age of AI, we will need to go toward "privacy by experimentation" or "privacy by innovation." Slick slogans with a sinister undertone. It is sort of like being in the "Good Place." What about privacy by design? Safety by design has been the call to action of the parents and young people we work with. You would think it would be exactly what we all want to get to. But what's getting in the way? Is it the loss of extra zeros at the end of the quarter? Just last week, two historic and precedent-setting accountability lawsuits were decided and won against major tech companies Meta and YouTube. And about bloody time. A jury confirmed what parents have felt and experts have said all along: the systems driving our social media platforms have been built to exploit, not protect. The people at the heart of it have always known it. Finally, some truth and accountability has arrived. My wife and I had the opportunity to spend time with many of the parents in the Los Angeles case as it proceeded in the courtroom each day. These are families that we've—that we've come to know over the years while learning more about how technology has stolen their kids' lives. Every one of those families and more like them has experienced irreparable harm because of the way technology intersected with their child's life. The way technology followed their children's behavior so closely meant platforms algorithmically targeted them with the very dangers they were most susceptible to. This led to eating disorders, drug overdoses, bullying, dangerous viral challenges, and in many cases, suicide. Some parents have had to wait more than five years—five years—to gain access to their child's phone and apps because of the platforms' own privacy rules. Parents were left searching for answers, blaming themselves until they eventually discovered the harmful content that social media apps were pushing to their sons and daughters at a time when they were most vulnerable, leading to their death. We're told it's a lack of discipline. We are told that it's a parenting failure or a mental health problem, and that users should simply opt out. But that framing again intentionally shifts responsibility away from the systems designed to capture and hold our attention in the first place. Their stories are impossible to ignore, and they will almost certainly appear in history books one day. But more questions hang over these stories. Will they actually inspire change? Will companies and governments that are currently pushing to build technology that extracts the maximum possible value from our lives decide on their own that enough is enough? Or will meaningful change only happen if we choose to demand it? An actual choice that rightfully rests with us. This room knows the answer. We cannot wait for behavior to magically change when incentives are structured around avoiding accountability and maximizing profit. The system itself is designed to keep people addicted. This happens when powerful people continually show their desire to turn our real world into a social media world—a world where anonymity is used to harm. It is a world without laws or protection, without consequence, without decency. A world without truth and reality becomes a world of chaos and division. We have to rewrite the script. We have to be strategic. We must put—put rules in place. Trojan Horse in protection if necessary. We have to build systems of governance that actually protect people. We have to build a culture that demands real, meaningful privacy. Because the truth is that it should not take horrific stories about children taking their own lives for us to care about how our information is used. All of us have experienced some version of the same dynamic. We open an app intending to spend five minutes. 40 minutes later, we're still there, watching content we didn't ask for and wondering why we couldn't just close the screen. We blame ourselves. We say we lack discipline. But the reality is that we've built systems that begin with engagement and then scale through increasingly precise behavioral prediction and monetization. There is an entire industry worth hundreds of billions of dollars dedicated to keeping us exactly where we are. They are studying us, predicting us, and nudging us towards behaviors that serve someone else's outcome. Increasingly, these systems influence not just what we see, but how we think, feel, and engage with the world. There is little transparency into how deliberately those outcomes are engineered and in whose interest they operate. This is not freedom. This is not normal. And this is not—this is not okay. Trust is part of what allowed the digital space to scale in the first place. But that trust has been broken. It was not broken suddenly, but steadily, as people realize these systems don't operate the way they were promised. And still we continue, because opting out is no longer realistic. The system no longer relies on trust to function. Yet we rely on it for connection. That should concern all of us. So the question isn't whether trust is broken; it's whether we're willing to rebuild it for everyone's sake and what that will take. There is also something important about this moment. We're not just living through another phase of technological change. We are standing at the beginning of the next world-shaping shift. Artificial intelligence will transform nearly every part of our lives. That transformation gives us something rare: a chance to do things differently. For the last 20 years, we've built digital systems first and governed them later. AI gives us a rare opportunity to reverse that sequence. We can embed accountability, privacy, and safety into the architecture from the start, not retrofit it after harm has already occurred. We will not get many chances to correct course. Having this conference here in Washington DC is fitting. As a Brit living in the United States, I've been struck by the global reach of American tech. The standards set here increasingly define the digital environment everywhere. This is true even in countries trying to protect their own citizens and their own democracies. When that influence scales faster than safeguards, the impact is felt far beyond US borders. We can choose data sovereignty instead of data surveillance.
