The Big Interview: Michael O'Leary

Virgin Media News(20.2K) ·44:00 ·54.6K views · 2026-04-01
Michael O'Leary (CEO, Ryanair) Colette Fitzpatrick (Presenter, Virgin Media News)
Michael O'Leary forecasts a 15-20% rise in summer airfares due to jet fuel volatility and warns that government inaction on the Dublin Airport passenger cap threatens Ireland’s tourism and international standing.
TL;DR Michael O'Leary forecasts a 15-20% rise in summer airfares due to jet fuel volatility and warns that government inaction on the Dublin Airport passenger cap threatens Ireland’s tourism and international standing. Rising Airfares: Fares are expected to trend upward...

TL;DR

Michael O'Leary forecasts a 15-20% rise in summer airfares due to jet fuel volatility and warns that government inaction on the Dublin Airport passenger cap threatens Ireland’s tourism and international standing.

Aviation Economics & Summer Outlook

  • Rising Airfares: Fares are expected to trend upward throughout the summer due to geopolitical uncertainty and rising fuel costs.
      • Fuel Volatility: While Ryanair has hedged 80% of its fuel through March 2027, unhedged jet fuel prices rose from $70/barrel in March to $150/barrel in April.
      • Advice to Consumers: O'Leary advises booking immediately, as prices will likely peak as summer progresses and Middle Eastern capacity remains constrained.
  • Capacity Constraints: Several Middle Eastern carriers have grounded aircraft due to regional conflict, limiting global supply and driving up prices on long-haul routes to Asia.
  • Fleet Expansion: Ryanair plans to take delivery of 300 new aircraft over the next decade, aiming to carry 300 million passengers annually.

Infrastructure & Regulatory Disputes

  • Dublin Airport Passenger Cap: O'Leary heavily criticized the Irish government for failing to scrap the 32-million-passenger cap despite having a 20-seat majority.
      • US Relations: He warned that US aviation authorities are "pissed off" with the cap; there is a legitimate risk of retaliatory restrictions on Irish carriers landing in New York.
      • Infrastructure Failure: He labeled the current administration a "do-nothing government," citing failures in housing, electricity costs, and transport infrastructure.
  • Noise Complaints: Dismissed local residents' noise concerns as "NIMBYism" (Not In My Backyard).
      • Noise Levels: Claims research shows aircraft noise typically reaches 53 decibels—well within the "safe" 50-65 decibel range.
      • Resident Conflict: Famously characterized local opposition groups as "self-appointed lunatics" and "NIMBYs" who moved near an airport that has existed since 1940.

Operational Policies & Customer Service

  • Baggage Fees: Confirmed that ground staff receive a commission (approx. €4–€5) for identifying oversized bags at the gate.
      • Strategic Goal: The primary objective is to force passengers to carry less, reducing aircraft weight and fuel consumption.
  • On-Board Alcohol: Advocating for a two-drink limit at airport bars to curb "disruptive behavior" caused by a mix of alcohol and drugs.
      • Safety Concerns: Noted that Ryanair averages one diverted flight per day due to unruly passengers, with Ibiza being the most problematic route.
  • Customer Philosophy: Asserts that Ryanair provides the best service in the industry by focusing exclusively on the two metrics passengers care about: price and punctuality.

Industry Perspectives & Personal Future

  • Publicity Strategy: Admits to using "outrageous" ideas (like charging for toilets) as free PR to drive sales without an advertising budget.
  • Elon Musk & Social Media: Labeled Elon Musk an "idiot" regarding aviation technology and described social media as a "cesspit" that should require identity verification.
  • Succession Planning: O’Leary is currently negotiating a contract extension to 2032.
      • Succession: While he intends to work until age 70, he confirmed that a formal succession plan—including five group airline CEOs—is reviewed annually.
  • Political Views: Expressed admiration for Margaret Thatcher and Seán Lemass, while criticizing "the Greens" and the high number of teachers in the Dáil for a perceived lack of "getting things done."
"If you have a 20-seat majority, you should be capable of getting legislation passed in a month, two months, three months... but it is dither and indecision." — Michael O'Leary
"I think social media is a cesspit... I don't understand why governments around the world allow people to post all this stuff anonymously." — Michael O'Leary
"99.9% of people book Ryanair on what's the lowest fare, and then we're more likely to get you there on time than any other airline." — Michael O'Leary
Good evening and welcome to the Big Interview. Our guest this evening is a man who needs little introduction; it's Ryanair boss Michael O'Leary. Michael, you're very welcome to the studio. Thanks very much for coming out with us here in Ballymount. Thank you, Colette. Pleasure to be here. Airfares, where are they going because of the war in Iran? I mean, they're probably going up this summer, but how much we don't yet know. The sooner the war ends, the more, the sooner we will remove the uncertainty over fuel prices. But Ryanair is—we've hedged 80% of our fuel for the next 12 months to March 2027. We're certainly going to insulate our passengers against egregious fuel price rises. But the 20% we haven't hedged has doubled between March to the month of April. In March, our unhedged fuel, we were paying about $70 a barrel. In April, we're paying about $150 a barrel. So this is the jet fuel as opposed to regular oil? Jet fuel. Yeah. And you know, the longer the war goes on in Iran, if that goes on into May or June, we're probably facing oil prices or jet fuel prices of anything up to $200 a barrel. So globally then, airfares, are they going to go up by what, 15, 20% do you think? I'm not talking about the short haul, about Ryanair. I mean, it is hard to know because, you know, it's not certain. If there's a recession, airfares will come down. If there's higher oil prices, the legacy airlines—the high-fare ones—would be trying to put on fuel surcharges. So the trend is certainly going to be up towards the remainder of this summer. And that's why we would be encouraging people to book early and book now, because I think fares will rise as we move through the summer. So have you seen a bounce, say from the likes of people who would have wanted to go to Dubai in the past and they're now thinking, no, I'll go to Spain, I'll go to Portugal, I'll go to France? Certainly this Easter. I mean, there's been a dramatic switch. I think lots of families who would have been going either to the Middle East or flying over the Middle East to go to Asia have all rebooked. They're going to Italy, Portugal, Spain, Greece. They're all certainly staying much closer to home over the Easter school holidays. It's a little bit early yet; people are not yet changing their summer holiday plans, but that's likely to happen if this war continues. So if you hadn't booked your flight for the summer, would you book it tonight? Would you wait a few weeks? Would you wait til the end of May? I would book it tonight. And I'd book it as soon as possible because I think the risk is that not alone will airfares rise, but even if the war finishes, it's going to take the Middle Eastern carriers a long time to get back in the air. Kuwait or Qatar this week have grounded, sent about 20 or 30 of their aircraft to a kind of a desert facility where you close up airplanes. The first bank designed for AI customers

Reid Hoffman(101.0K) ·57:18 ·9.6K views · 2026-04-01
Sean Neville, co-founder of Circle and architect of USDC, is launching Catena Labs to build the world’s first AI-native bank, shifting financial infrastructure from human-centric models to a future where 100% of economic transactions are executed by autonomous agents.
TL;DR Sean Neville, co-founder of Circle and architect of USDC, is launching Catena Labs to build the world’s first AI-native bank, shifting financial infrastructure from human-centric models to a future where 100% of economic transactions are executed by autonomous agents. The...

TL;DR

Sean Neville, co-founder of Circle and architect of USDC, is launching Catena Labs to build the world’s first AI-native bank, shifting financial infrastructure from human-centric models to a future where 100% of economic transactions are executed by autonomous agents.

From Stablecoins to the Agentic Economy

  • The Circle Legacy: Neville co-founded Circle to make money move like data on the internet. The company has since processed over $10 trillion in volume, proving that "narrow banking"—where assets are fully backed and not lent out—is the most stable model for internet finance.
  • The "Agentic" Shift: While the last decade was about putting dollars on the internet, Neville believes the next decade is about AI becoming the primary customer. He predicts a future where every transaction is executed by AI agents rather than humans navigating websites.
  • Catena Labs Vision: Unlike traditional banks that use AI as a tool, Catena is an "AI-native bank" designed for autonomous agents that may exist for only minutes to execute a specific transaction before disappearing.

Solving the "Know Your Agent" (KYA) Challenge

  • Identity and KYA: Current financial systems rely on Social Security Numbers and human history. Neville proposes a KYA (Know Your Agent) framework to identify machines.
      • Mapping to Entities: In the near term, agents must map back to a "KYC’ed" (Know Your Customer) human or licensed business.
      • Future Autonomy: Neville envisions a future where agents have unique, government-recognized identifiers independent of specific humans, enabling "zero-person" billion-dollar entities.
  • Deterministic vs. Probabilistic Guardrails:
      • The AI Layer: Uses LLMs for "steering" and judgment, which is inherently probabilistic and prone to hallucination.
      • The Blockchain Layer: Uses smart contracts to enforce deterministic rules. For example, a smart contract can programmatically prevent an agent from spending more than $50, a rule the AI cannot bypass.

Rebuilding Financial Infrastructure for AI Customers

  • Eliminating the "Shopping Cart": Neville argues that AI customers don't need user interfaces, shopping carts, or marketing copy. They require high-quality data schemas and APIs designed for machine parsing rather than human reading.
  • The Disappearing Back Office: The first massive disruption in finance will be the "disappearing back office," where AI handles "unsexy" tasks like reconciliation, cash flow management, and compliance.
      • Case Study: Neville currently utilizes 100 AI agents for compliance monitoring, overseen by only two humans.
  • Programmable Money: Stablecoins are the ideal "rails" for AI because they are programmable. Traditional credit card rails fail AI because they lack the transparency, speed, and cost-efficiency required for micro-transactions between machines.

Policy, Regulation, and the "Genius Act"

  • Public-Private Cooperation: Neville emphasizes that for the U.S. Dollar to remain the global reserve, the government must codify how dollars work on blockchain rails.
  • The Genius Act: He highlights this legislation as a critical step in providing "rules of the road" for stablecoin reserves (T-bills and cash), ensuring consumer protection while allowing private-sector innovation.
  • The Privacy Imperative: To avoid a "surveillance state" on-chain, Neville advocates for open standards and payment-specific blockchains (like Circle’s "Arc" or the "Tempo" project) that balance transaction transparency with individual privacy.

Notable Quotes

"I think every single transaction will be executed by AI in the future... it will be the safest way to execute transactions. It’ll also be the fastest way to execute commerce at the lowest costs." — Sean Neville
"Agents don't need shopping carts... I hope it's not just a new sort of search that still has the same underlying mechanics. We don't need user experience... they can understand data effectively." — Sean Neville
"We see a single-person company that can generate a billion dollars... my response to that is always: why do we need that one person?" — Sean Neville
"Blockchains are useful for moving value, but the thing that's particularly exciting is you can program money on them. You can write smart contracts with rules that AI can't possibly circumvent." — Sean Neville
So you said earlier that most economic transactions in the future will be agent to agent and you said this is something we'll want. Why do we really want that for all the people who are listening being like, "That sounds insane"? I think it's not even the most; I think it's all. I think every single transaction will be executed by AI in the future. You know, it sounds a little science fiction, but that's the world I think we will ultimately want to get to if we have the proper guardrails and it's sort of aligned with human ethics and so on. I hope it's not just a chat interface on top of a shopping cart. I hope it's not just a new sort of search that still has the same underlying mechanics. Agents don't need shopping carts. Will we see a single-person company that can generate a billion dollars or $100 billion of value? And my response to that is always: why do we need that one person? Most people think about AI as something that helps us work faster. Sean Neville is building for a different future, one where AI doesn't just assist with finance—it is the customer. Sean co-founded Circle and architected USDC, the $76 billion stablecoin that turned crypto from speculation into actual infrastructure. But even inside Circle, he says, not many people believed in it at first. Now he's doing it again. Catena Labs is building the first AI-native bank. Not a bank that uses AI tools, but a bank designed from the ground up assuming your customer might be an autonomous agent that exists for minutes, executes a transaction, and disappears. This raises questions we've never had to answer before. How do you do KYC on something that doesn't have a Social Security number? Who's liable when an agent misbehaves? And why do credit cards fail where stablecoins succeed when AI is the one spending the money? Sean has 100 AI agents doing compliance monitoring right now, overseen by just two humans. He's navigating regulators who either want to build frameworks for AI finance or believe nothing good can possibly come from letting machines touch money. This conversation is about building at the collision point of two platform shifts and why some infrastructure has to be rebuilt from scratch. Welcome to Possible, Sean Neville. Great to have you here; welcome to Possible. So you helped start a company called Circle, which helps process I think now $10 trillion in volume using stablecoins and is valued at close to 13 billion. Let's rewind to 2013. You and Jeremy co-founded Circle with this bold vision of making money move like the internet. What was the moment or idea that convinced you this needed to exist, and what made you guys go, "We're the right people to build it"? Well, so this is actually the third company that Jeremy and I worked on together. And so our previous companies were at the advent of the web where, you know, we saw open standards on the internet enable people to publish their opinions, you know, express themselves without gatekeepers. Fundamentally, when we look at Circle, that's really the seminal idea: it should be possible for everyone to plug into the global economy without gatekeepers sort of chopping off your money along the way for the privilege of access. And so the vision just generally speaking was how do we make money work on the internet the way content and data work on the internet? The internet has new business models but it wants data transmission to be free, and we view money as just data. So transmission of money should be free or very, very close to free so that we can unlock new kinds of businesses for everybody. And so that was generally the idea. Why us? You know, we had a lot of experience in building web companies at that point and working on sort of open standard approaches to it, but we were also helpfully naive about doing that in a regulated industry. The regulatory pattern for the internet is quite different than obviously financial services. But also not only did we not have a background in say payments—traditional payments—but really any. We were builders in any regulated like healthcare or aviation or, you know, pick your regulated industry. So I think it was actually helpful that we were so naive that we thought this was a thing that we could do on the policy side in addition to the tech side. What was the regulatory thing that was kind of the most interesting, like a surprise or a learning or something you'd say to other entrepreneurs? Because I, by the way, have also had similar kind of things both in financial systems and in biology and pharma, where I go ooh, I'm like, I go from being a software guy into it. I'm curious which one is the ugh or the aha moment in discovery in regulatory? Yeah, it was both ugh and aha at the same time. And I think, you know, we embraced the opportunity to educate policy makers and we felt like even if it took a decade or more to encode some of these ideas in public policy, we were up for that and that's what we felt needed to happen. A lot of the early economic philosophy, particularly from Jeremy, my co-founder, and that I embraced as well, was just this notion of what's been referred to as a narrow bank. This is this idea that's been around since the '20s or '30s, which is a really simple, almost boringly simple idea. Which is just, you know, if you're a bank and I give you my money and I come back 90 days later, my money is still there and you can give it back to me. Because the sort of like payment facility and custody facility is separate from the credit facility of a bank, and you're not allowed to, you know, take my $10 and lend it out 100 times. And that's a thing that hasn't actually been allowed in the government and stablecoins are effectively that. They're narrow banking. Yes, exactly. And one of the cleverest things I think we did in the regulatory side in PayPal which was, hey, if we could get the FDIC to say that our deposits are FDIC insured, you essentially get all the regulators to go, "Okay fine, like we're good." And so what we did is we essentially went to them and said, well you have this pass-through broker insurance where it allows it, so we're not putting it in banks on behalf of the people, but it's FDIC insured still. And the FDIC agreed with us. So then our regulatory conversation got a lot simpler because anytime that a regulator asked us, "Well, but you realize they're protected by the FDIC?" and they're like, "Oh, never mind. We don't need to talk to you anymore." Right, and so that was part of it. So Sean, for our listeners who might not be as familiar with a stablecoin, could you explain to us what it is? Yeah, I don't love the word stablecoin. I think of it as dollars and money on the internet and, you know, I don't know if the word stablecoin survives, but the origin of it is that we have other kinds of money that can move on these open rails, these open blockchain rails. Bitcoin for instance is one and there are many others. But the problem with those coins is that they change in value, so that if I'm sending you money, how much money you receive might be quite different than the money that I sent and neither one of us can know ahead of time. And so the idea of a stablecoin is that I send you $10 and you get $10. And it's stable in terms of its value. So before we get to the really exciting stuff you're doing now, OpenClaw went from zero to I think over 145,000 GitHub stars in two weeks, with 1.5 million agents transacting on Multibook. Some people are calling it the early singularity; I'm not one of those. Others are calling it a dumpster fire; I'm closer to that. Where do you land? Maybe both in a way. So I think the positive I would say is that very quickly many more people have suddenly experienced this idea of a layer of abstraction above say Claude Code or chat or co-work even. You know, these new sort of what they had thought of as how you interact with AI, sort of a layer above that that can use all of those things under the covers. And so the appetite is there and the interest and enthusiasm for those kinds of actors is there. More on the dumpster fire side is that you know, there are just tremendous security implications. I think the top OpenClaw skills have malware problems; people are using them without being able to sort of clear the trust chasm. When it comes to those agents being financial actors, that's a real problem because people can get hurt and it could go sideways in an ugly way. Which is many of the things we want to address and we want to make it safe for agents to be economic actors. I mean ultimately we think the only economic actor anyone will trust will be agents, but it's not mults or lobsters or whatever we sort of call them today. So I think the experiment is great, but there's also a lot of reason to be worried about the form it's taken, particularly the form it's taken right now. So actually that leads perfectly into my next question, sort of talking about how do you make economic agents trustworthy on the internet? You stepped back from Circle, the company you co-founded, and started a new company. Can you tell us about it? Yeah, so in many ways it's a nice trajectory from Circle and I have to, you know, I am still on the board so I'll bite my tongue on some things on the Circle side. But we got excited about creating dollars on the internet to unlock new business opportunities. But one of the other problems with the economy is that people don't have equal access to it. And one of the things that's exciting about AI is that if we get it right, then the optimistic view is it will be able to provide individuals and businesses everywhere around the world with access they've never seen before. Then the economics will work and the intelligence will work and it will leverage things like digital dollars and other currencies on the internet. But it'll do more than that. It'll allow people to take the next step into stepping into the global economy in ways they just haven't been able to before. And so it's a nice continuation but it's also the thing I've been most excited about in my life to work on. This is just an incredible era to be building. So walk us through a concrete example. An AI agent tries to pay for something today using existing rails. What breaks? Where does a credit card fail where a stablecoin or other rails succeed? Yeah, so I'll give a kind of a retail commerce example even though most of the flows that we're focused on now are actually more in the enterprise and business oriented. But the commerce one is almost easier to I think illustrate. So it starts with maybe an analogy to how you might buy something today. If you're purchasing something on Amazon.com today, we don't have to really worry that we're not on Amazon. Because SSL and HTTPS and all that stuff, we don't even think... we used to have locks in the browser that would at least give... now we don't even need that, we just trust that the technology handles that we're talking to the legit Amazon. And that kind of layer doesn't exist with agents today. If they're chatbots on a website it exists. But with these new surfaces, how do I know that it's Amazon that my agent is talking to? So it's the fundamental identity issue mapped back to an institution that I want to have some trust in. And then once I clear that hurdle and I can be sure that my agent is talking to Reid's agent, how do I put some controls around what those agents are allowed to do? And a simple example would be spending rules. So just set up something like, "Allow my agent to buy my goods on Amazon up to $50, but notify me for anything above that and don't let it spend on Etsy without notifying me." Simple spend rules. And they can get much more complicated, but just first is identification, and then there's rules, and then there's the auditability of looking back and seeing what actually happened. And that's particularly important in cases where something went wrong and understanding where the liability lies. And so these are all the sort of "Who are you? What are you allowed to do? And can you look back and understand what was done?" Those are the sort of initial fundamental problems that need to be solved if agents are the ones that are—whether they're paying other agents or paying humans or just participating in automation with finance. It's really all of these things. Loosely we refer to these things as KYA, which is Know Your Agent, and being aware of who you're interacting with and if it's my agent, how do I make sure it's trustworthy to your agent? These sorts of things. And so this is kind of a foundational element before we even get to payments that I think we need to address, that we've been focused on. Hopefully the solution is not a proprietary vendor solution but something that is more of a de facto or formal standard that we can all build on, similar to what we saw unlock e-commerce. So what are some of the things that go into this new architecture? Obviously agent identity and certification is one part of it. I'm curious to get a little bit more depth. What are the different kinds of ways that you conceptualize the way that the network works when the customers or the engagement services are AI, not humans? So there needs to be a way, and this is like nascent, doesn't really exist today. There isn't sort of a DNS of agents to just resolve to discover agents out there. Although there are things that are emerging related to MCP server marketplaces for instance, or now we have OpenClaw skills marketplaces. But there isn't that mechanism yet. So one of the things that needs to happen is when agents begin to talk to each other, whether it's through APIs or MCP servers or through these new discovery mechanisms, that first some verification can happen that maps back to who is the operator of that agent. It could, if it's a one-to-one scenario, you have your agent and I just need to be sure it's actually your agent. But it could be much more complex in that we're all using someone else's agent to perform some function, and then we need to be sure the operator of that agent is who we think. In the regulated financial situation, it often needs to map back to a licensed entity that's allowed to execute money transmission or lending or whatever it may be. And so we think of just the foundational primitives as—a lot of them are related to identity and what are the protocols, meaning just how do you exchange these credentials back and forth in order for agents to do that in a trustworthy way. So Sean, at Circle you were designing APIs for human customers; at Catena, as you just said, you are designing for AI customers. And so can you give us another concrete example of what's different? What are API design choices that you're making now because you're specifically building for these AI agents? Yeah, I mean I think first of all I think API design is totally different now. As a human developer, do you ever really need to read docs anymore to integrate an API, whether you're building something in finance or not? So a lot of even the API docs are generated for agentic workflows or even just directly for LLMs to be able to parse effectively. Many times this is sort of even past the era of vibe coders, but principal architects leveraging large language models to build software don't even look at the underlying APIs anymore because it's more productive—so long as you can trust the AI, it's more productive not to do so. So then you end up designing APIs in a different fashion. There's a really simple version of this that used to happen prior to some of the latest advancements, which was just how do you get a large language model to use a tool? Which is really like, if you put in these words, how do you get that large language model to recognize, "Okay, now I need to make an API call"? And so there are these function calling tools and mechanisms. And even in that mode you could see in order to tweak the large language model to understand it should use an API, you kind of had to do some prompt engineering and coax it a certain way. But the real way to make it more reliable is actually changing your API so that—particularly not so much the signatures, to get a little technical, but not so much the function signatures, but the input and output parameters. So that it could understand the schemas and what it was meant to deliver. You would end up changing it in a way that was tuned to large language models, not so much human developers that would be looking at like a JSON schema to read. And that is only becoming more exacerbated. So I think that's generally speaking the case. To the Circle question, I think when we build APIs in sort of help businesses leverage stablecoins with APIs, we're optimizing to make them productive and make them happy, make developers happy. With AI there's a little more of a governance criterion in place, which is: how do we make this AI trustworthy and how do we help this API be reliably and safely executed by this machine? And there it's a slight difference because trust is still the biggest hurdle to clear with how can we trust these things with anything, let alone money. So looking forward a little bit, where do you think the first large-scale adoption of AI transacting on behalf of consumers or enterprises will be? Are there any obvious industries that might be disrupted or transformed? So I think ultimately it will hit everything but obviously not all at once. I do have very strong conviction that, as I said, I think the only actors that we'll trust with our money will be agents and agentic actors, and it'll be the only competitive way to earn a return and so on. But we're certainly not there yet. And so the harder question is well, what does it hit first? And many of the scenarios that have been outlined I would say are more consumer oriented, but where we see flows happening today are much more in the enterprise. And I wouldn't necessarily call them agents even; they're sort of automated workflows that increasingly have large language models in the loop. In the world of finance, they're not the sexy use cases of "get me some edge" and predicting the stock market; it's more like the disappearing back office. It's cash flow, basic liquidity management, compliance, reconciliation, these sorts of things. And so I think what happens sooner rather than later doesn't really look like a trading floor full of agents; it looks like a back office that's gone in the enterprise. So I was trying to explain to my 10-year-old the other day how people used to buy airline tickets and I couldn't remember. I was like, "God, what did we do? Did we call someone?" before there's the internet. When you think of the consumer side, how do you think consumers will act differently? Obviously we went from like we can't trust the internet for anything, to oh we can buy books, oh I guess we can buy clothes, now we can buy cars, buy whatever. Is there going to be like a similar trajectory for maybe stablecoins? Yeah, buy stablecoins. Like just for the sort of normie person who does online banking or puts their credit card into Amazon, what are they going to expect from this AI agent future? Well, I hope it's not just a chat interface on top of a shopping cart. I hope it's not just a new sort of search that still has the same underlying mechanics. You know, agents don't need shopping carts and we don't need user experience sort of like "here's what your tax is" and like, they can understand a lot of that data effectively. So I think the exciting element for consumers will really be expecting a new kind of interface that does not look like e-commerce, that optimizes you to not drop off in a shopping cart experience across a number of pages. And what does that experience look like? Well there's a lot of different experiments there and so I'm not going to predict exactly what it looks like. I think it will look very, if we get it right again, I think it will look very, very different than today. But it also won't be the case that an agent just magically handles all of your payments because there is some kind of shopping that is just pleasurable and requires sort of an introduction of taste. And then there are other commerce transactions that actually I never want to have to buy a train ticket again. I just want it to handle that for me. But I think there are all of these little pieces to resolve. I think the user experience and the consumer experience is the hardest nut to crack. So getting back to the trust piece, obviously we have agent-native banking needs like a new compliance strategy; that's going to be hugely important. And so can you tell us a bit how Catena handles compliance monitoring and like what do humans do versus what do AI agents handle? Yeah, so I'd say first of all, being a regulated financial institution, we're subject to the regulations that exist. So we can hope to encode into policy additional things related to AI safety, particularly AI safety related to financial flows, which I think ultimately will become very important. But in the meantime there are regulations that exist. So we're not starting from a blank slate. And so we police for money laundering, for instance. Maybe put it in two buckets: there's sort of real-time risk monitoring for things, and we've built out these systems obviously at Circle. When you take a fiat money and take a credit card and you want to turn that into stablecoins, you need to make sure that the risk is managed effectively and that's often handled in real time. And some compliance pieces are also handled in real time—sort of screening, hey this is a counterparty that we're just not allowed as a US company to send money to. And it's just like a hard line; you can't do that. OFAC screening. Exactly. And so we can't facilitate money transmission to North Korea, for instance. And that's just a hard line as a US company. There are other things though that are more complicated on the compliance side that are usually not handled in real time. And a lot of that relates to looking at transaction activity to understand money laundering schemes. The traditional way to handle that is to have a bunch of humans who use tools—they sort of sign into like a SaaS-like dashboard and they do transaction monitoring and they open cases and gather evidence and file suspicious activity reports, which is like a very sort of secretive process. And a lot of that can be automated very, very effectively. And it doesn't eliminate the need for a human compliance and risk expert, but the tasks that that human does are very different when they're not signing into all these dashboards to do the work. They're more orchestrating agents that can very effectively do that kind of work. And how close are you to that team of agents doing this work today? Well, we do that today and it'll only get better is what I say. And we'll quantify along the way—is it getting better because these things are very measurable in terms of false positives. So we do that today. I think there's still an open question though about how you structure the team to orchestrate them effectively, and the mechanisms to orchestrate them are changing so quickly that the answer today probably isn't the answer tomorrow. And I think that's true across many disciplines. We're talking about compliance and risk, but it's also true in every other discipline when we're building this kind of bank. We're building an AI-native bank from the ground up and so it touches every operational aspect of the institution. So speaking about identity, obviously agents don't have Social Security numbers, they don't have credit histories. Like when you think of traditional banking it's KYC (Know Your Customer) and AML (anti-money laundering). How do you know your customer when your customer is an agent? So in the near term, our version of KYA is mapping back to one of those entities that has been KYCed and KYBed. Because an agent can't apply for a bank account; it can't get a credit card directly. And so in the future I'd hope... that's what I was going to say. And in the future I think that that will change. I think we will want it to change. Oh, okay. And I think we collectively will want it to change because the value that we stand to gain will be great enough to force those sort of policy changes where there will be unique identifiers that are recognized by governments for agents. Even if the agent isn't identified with a specific human person? Yes. I think we will want to get there because what we'll want to see ultimately is sort of really autonomous operations that many of us can kind of own and participate in and generate value from. And so at that point, the agent's identity really is separate from any one of us. But in the near term, it's more like if you're interacting with our AI bank, then it needs to map back to us as the licensed financial—and I'm using "bank" loosely here—licensed financial institution because an agent simply can't be a licensed financial institution. It can't go out and get money transmission licenses or apply for a charter and so on. So there's a progression to get there. We have the technology to very reliably map back to the licensed entity. So the KYB business that's onboarded or the KYCed individual, we can use cryptography to do that in a way that means you don't really have to trust any business or company to see the tie. And so we can see back to like, say, this merchant has these credentials that were issued by Visa to this business, even though we're only interacting with that merchant's agent as an example. You mentioned in the future that we might not want one agent to track directly to one human. It might be an agent that's acting on behalf of many people and they sort of have some ownership. Is there a concrete example that you can give where we might want that sort of shared ownership of an agent? Yeah, so I'll answer a different question first and then maybe that'll help answer that question. So there's been a lot of talk about: will we see a single-person company that can generate a billion dollars or $100 billion of value? And my response to that is always: why do we need that one person? You're like, "Forget the one, let's do zero." I mean really. So I think what we want is a different kind of relationship between the humans and the companies, the entities. And I think it looks a little different than classic equity, but there is a role for humans that are collectively orchestrating data and behaviors in an autonomous workflow, or agent, for them to generate value for themselves mapped to the value that they're adding to the agent. And in that situation, you want multiple people contributing, not just one sort of orchestrating mastermind running a company that has a bunch of co-pilot workers. So it sounds a little science fiction but that's the world I think we will ultimately want to get to if we have the proper guardrails and it's sort of aligned with human ethics—all these important issues. And then the identity piece becomes interesting because it's bidirectional between humans and agents. It's not only "Can my agent trust Reid's agent?" It's more "Can Reid's agent trust me as a human being when it begins to interact with these workflows?" And so then we get to this whole other level of what this could look like. This is more 10-15 years potentially, although now 10-15 years maybe that's three years the rate that it's accelerating. But even 10 isn't like that far in the future. And I think also to your point, like everyone's always asking could we trust agents? Like, there's a lot of non-trustworthy humans around. And so if we could have a situation where we trust agents, that might be a better future. Yes, and the time is always the hardest thing for me to predict on this. And we're in this world and we see this constantly, but then if I interact with others who are not in this world, you could see this might take a little bit longer than we think to fully develop. This is the same thing that we went through with the internet and certainly with stablecoins. We're 13 years into Circle's tenure and a lot of... we're only just now realizing some of the use cases we thought surely will be satisfied by 2014 and they still haven't been. And so it may take longer than we expect, although right now this world to me seems very different than anything else I've done. In May 2025, you released the Agent Commerce Kit—I do think the acronym's a little odd, ACK—but the month before Google's AP2 or OpenAI's ACP and then OpenClaw happened. All of a sudden the future that you're working towards with agent identity and payment roles suddenly is an interesting shape and much closer. What do you think of this kickoff of this open source hobby project accelerating everything, and what are the ways that we need to navigate? What are the things we need to be doing in society given this acceleration of this? I do think that the solution for agentic identity and verifications and all the things we've been talking about should be something that is an open standard that everyone can build on. I'm a very big believer in open standards and the power of open networks as opposed to, say, proprietary solutions offered by one vendor. And I think that there has been fragmentation on the AI side when it comes to these kinds of topics today for a lot of reasons. I think one of the reasons is that even large incumbents aren't sure where the value is going to accrue, especially now that tech is not a moat for anybody. One person or Alibaba, it just... it's sort of no longer... we're even maybe past the castle moats metaphor and we're in the era of like gunpowder and ballistas and so on. So I think it's for that reason and many others the approaches to things like agentic identity are quite fragmented—even more fragmented now than they were a year ago. And so it feels like we haven't made the progress toward a standard that I had hoped for. But there are many ways for standards to emerge. MCP has kind of become a de facto standard. It didn't emerge through the W3C as a working group, but competitors to Anthropic have embraced it. These projects that achieve a level of virality show real appetite and interest that can shine a spotlight on a use case that causes others to act. Agents suddenly showed up as customers a lot sooner than we expected them to, so that's a positive way to look at it. For ACK and for some of these other protocols, I fundamentally look at it as, ideally, a set of standards that we can all rely on so that as you're building agents and others are building agents, they're all leveraging the same open standard that no one can sort of unilaterally change. And also so that our agents don't have to try to integrate 20 different approaches to the same thing and we can build just much more valuable competitive offerings on top of that base. So when people are worried about AI, they talk about runaway agents. And sometimes that's sort of more bioterrorism and geopolitics, but often it's an agent buying 10,000 burritos or making a million-dollar PolyMarket bet when you wanted them to put $10 on red. And so you talked about sort of guardrails that's like you can tell them, "Don't spend more than $50" or "These are the rules." Talk more about your guardrails and specifically whether they're sort of rules or judgment. Like, are we giving these agents values about what to do or are we giving them hard and fast rules to make sure that they still have their autonomy but they're aligned with humans? Yeah, so it's a really good question. I think of it in two layers and this is how we've approached what we build out today. First layer is very much a—you could just call it the AI layer. And a simple example of a guardrail there, it would be easy to circumvent, but it's just kind of a prompt suggestion. But the problem with guardrails without policy enforcement is it's just that; they just become suggestions. So you can flesh out many things, many layers of guardrails. You can have... we're talking about an agent but usually there are many sort of LLMs involved in workflows. And you can have LLMs check the output of other LLMs—did they have just ones dedicated to guardrails before any activity happens? And so there is a sophisticated tuning of that architecture and orchestration. But when it comes to things like moving money, those are deterministic outcomes and they should not be completely non-deterministic. If I'm moving a million dollars, it either needs to move or it needs to not move. And it can't be sort of worse than failed; it can't be, "Oh, yes it did move," and it didn't move or it moved to a different place due to hallucinations or whatever. And so the second layer is really the underlying deterministic layer which is a place where stablecoins actually excel because we can programmatically enforce rules both at the wallet sort of cryptographic key level and on-chain in smart contracts. We can write rules that AI can't possibly circumvent. And so you need both operating in concert and so that's sort of how we approach enforcement of these guardrails at a couple of different levels. And then I think there'll be iterations on both of these things, but it's one of those places where blockchains are useful for moving value, but the thing that's particularly exciting is you can program money on them. And you can write these so-called smart contracts that have rules in them that then are not controlled inside sort of a bank's ledger, but publicly on these rails using cryptography. And so that turns out to be really helpful for AI flows and AI actors who are executing these money flows, as opposed to just having an API and then trying to deal with the inputs and outputs. So another thing people are really worried about of course is external threats. Like, are the AI agents going to become so good at whatever it is—scams, fraud, etc.? Are there ways that you think you are better positioned to ward off external threats than traditional banks or how do you guys look at that? Yeah, it's a problem now. I think a lot of people are approaching through this sort of proof of personhood, which is very useful and will become incredibly more useful. But we really think more of the other side which is sort of proof of agency. Which is more along the lines of proof that this agent belongs to the operator that it says it does and that has these policies that we can be very sure cannot be bypassed and how do we enforce these restrictions. And I think that we have a time window here to get that right, but the window won't be open forever before people are burned. And so some of the sort of not doomsday but dire scenarios you mentioned I think are likely to happen without these guardrails in place, frankly. In some of your other comments you've mentioned that agents are surprisingly bad at marketing content generation but great at compliance. What other counterintuitive things have you learned about what AI is actually good at and actually still TBD in financial services? Some of the things that it used to not be so great at, it's actually not bad at now. And there's always the thing of, I hear people say, "Well I put this in Claude and it told me this," or ChatGPT or whatever and it's like, "Well, that's what it told you based on what you put in and what you had." It's sort of like saying, "Well I put this in Photoshop and this is the image that came out." It's like, well if I use Photoshop it's quite different than if a real photographer uses it. Maybe you're the problem here. Right, and so it's like some of it's garbage in, garbage out. But it's also we have very sophisticated workflows now in terms of managing memory and context and so on when it comes to making these things good at software engineering that have made them so good at developing software that the profession is completely different than it was a year ago. And I think that'll happen to maybe not every knowledge worker domain all at once, but it'll happen to many of them. And it'll be a similar progression. So a lot of the things like marketing content and just in terms of generating copy, there's a certain tone and it's not just the em-dashes or whatever the clichés are; it's the phrasing of the short-short-long, short-short. It's the kind of like freshman in high school sort of, "This is a good essay" kind of a thing. But that's going away with the right context and the right data provided and examples provided and models that get taught by each other to be better at those things. So I think the other answer to the question is a lot of the stuff that it's good at—it's the non-sexy stuff. It's just really good at parsing a tremendous amount of information, synthesizing it, and turning it around to make you more productive. Well it's so clear that you are so excited about this sort of next world and you're building this whole fleet of financial tools for AI agents. What made you decide that this was the thing to do, this was the first thing you needed to get out into the world? Yeah, I mean I think if we don't get this piece out there, it's like an unlock. If we don't get this piece out then we can't unlock these new opportunities. I mean the sort of pattern through crypto and stablecoins and Circle that I think is relevant to AI is there's kind of a toy phase where people are speculating with some random stuff and it seems easy to dismiss, but gets some attention. But then there's this like really hard middle ground of infrastructure development and regulators start plugging in and this middle ground can last a long time of doing really hard work to unlock new kinds of opportunities to the later phase which is these world-changing applications. The likes of which we can't possibly imagine when we're in these early stages. And it was similar to when I was actually young in the early days of the web; I couldn't possibly have predicted all of the things that the internet and the web would be used for, but we just had a sense: this is world-changing. That's the excitement: to build the unlock. We're in the infrastructure phase and it's often accompanied, if it's truly valuable, by a speculative element as well. So there's hype and there's speculative cycles, but that doesn't mean it's not real and important. And the last thing I would say is I think that there's a time window here to get this right and the window is closing. And so there's urgency here to execute. Why do you think the window is closing? Because the advancements now are AI augmented, so they're just exponential in terms of the capabilities. We're talking about OpenClaw which again I think is exciting—I don't want to poo-poo it—but there are also some real concerns. And so what's next will be augmented by OpenClaw agents building the next thing. Making sure that we're capable of working on all of these things to make AI powerful for people with the kind of guardrails and the alignment that we've talked about—this is the time to do that. But there will come a time when, if we don't get it right, it may be too late. Crypto has taught us immutability has costs: no chargebacks. Traditional banking has fraud protection and appeals. Where should we be thinking about agent commerce in this? Yeah, so I think one of the big hurdles that people have now, even with agent commerce just the sort of consumer shopping behaviors is: if a merchant tricks my agent into buying something, who's liable? The merchant wants to know this, the consumer wants to know this, and these are questions that are answered in, say, the Visa world or the Mastercard world and they're not answered yet in agentic commerce. So it's very difficult to establish liability paths. And this comes back to agentic identity and understanding who it is, what are the rules it has to follow, and then who is ultimately responsible if an audit shows that an agent bypassed its guardrails. And so these are things that have to be defined. There are lots of different opinions. I'd say some are very traditional, which is more along the lines of AI is just a surface on the underlying rails. We believe new rails need to be created and that smart contracts and blockchain technology help us get a step forward in creating this. So you said earlier that most economic transactions in the future will be agent to agent and you said this is something we'll want. We'll want these autonomous agents doing that. Can you make the affirmative case—why do we really want that for all the people who are listening being like, "That sounds insane"? Yeah, I think it's not even the most; I think it's all. I think every single transaction will be executed by AI in the future. And I think we'll want that to be the case because it will be the safest way to execute transactions. It'll be safer than trying to trust a web form and a purchase through the whole chain than it is today. And that's not the case today—we have a ways to get there—but I think that ultimately will be the case. It'll also be the fastest way to execute commerce at the lowest costs. I mean, thanks to things like stablecoins, we can move a trillion dollars around the world for fractions of a penny and nearly instantly. Connecting those kinds of flows effectively does require a similar level of intelligence because now the bottlenecks in terms of speed are not the machine or the blockchain; it's all the layers on top of it. And then when it comes to earning a return on our assets, I think it will be the only competitive way to earn a return. Today things like private banking or certain opportunities are accessible to very few people. And the promise of AI is that you have the same level of access that all of these high-end businesses or ultra-high net worth individuals have, and the only way that you get there is by trusting these agents. They will be the only way that you get a competitive return on your underlying assets and so that's the optimistic vision, certainly the thing that we're marching toward. So one of the things that you know, I know, technologists know, is that these AI systems are inherently these interesting probabilistic systems. But when you get to, for example, a statement of like all commerce transactions flowing through agents because it's higher reliability, how do you square the probabilistic nature of these things where they can go strange very quickly with the necessity of reliability within the financial system? What's your thinking about like what is already present or what needs to be created? What's the thought on that impedance? Yeah, so I think I'll start at the user experience level, which is I think a lot of the tasks that we'll be executing will involve some level of orchestrating these agents doing a variety of things for us and steering and tuning. And then past the user experience is the underlying infrastructure to take that steering—I would say that's more at the AI layer, not just at large language models but with all of the integrations and data that they'll touch. And then when it comes to deterministic outcomes when they're really doing specific things in the world—moving a car or executing a financial transaction—then those things need to be absolutely deterministic and not probabilistic. And so that's the sort of second layer. But I think that the really interesting thing is that rather than clicking a button in an interface and then checking the readout that the button led to what we expected to happen and ultimately committing, it will be more like we're doing less on those surfaces but we're doing a lot of steering as needed to orchestrate the agents in the way that we want them to behave. Which is what happens in software engineering today. Software engineering has gravitated toward you don't really use IDEs anymore or look at source code so much because you have a new level of interface which still requires a great deal of attention. Some would say it even requires more, but you're kind of multitasking across a set of orchestrating surfaces either on a command line or otherwise to build software. Going up a level, what are the things that we should be doing with our kind of government financial infrastructure to bring all of our companies, industries, invention, society forward? What are some things... if you just said, "Hey guys, if you started paying attention at least to a few things, do these"? Yeah, I'll say on the policy side, there's a policy component to it. Obviously we're leaning into being a regulated financial institution so we're confronting the existing policy regime. But when it came to building US Dollar Coin and stablecoins, our belief was then that this is something—in order for the dollar to work on the internet, the United States government has to say that this is how dollars can work on the internet. And there needs to be public-private sector cooperation and joint innovation. The way that we usually do that in the West is that there's public policy and there's private sector innovation. We thought it might take 10 years; it ended up taking about seven years to get the Genius Act passed for stablecoins. Where the government says you have a dollar on blockchain rails, it needs to be backed by these T-bills and cash and overnight repos and so on. It's very specific things. Here are the consumer protections, here's how you can't return yield because it's not a mutual fund, those sort of things. And when it comes to regulation of AI in finance, I do think that ultimately we'll have policy requirements around any AI actors that are permitted to touch things like dollars. And that's where public-private sector again needs to cooperate because the US dollar is backed by the United States government; it's not a private sector technology. And so how these things move through agents, similarly to how they move through blockchains, will have to be encoded ultimately in public policy. The way that that's happening today is there are several different sort of workstreams in DC and otherwise to explore this. Some come out of the AI safety world—AI safety guardrails in general and sort of clearing evals and the like. Others are very much out of finance. And so there are many different... I would categorize my experience so far as they're fairly nascent. It's an important investment toward a long-term result that we'll need to have in place. So if we go back to March 2023 and Silicon Valley Bank, the whole point of a stablecoin is that it is stable and that it is pegged in this case to the US dollar. USDC briefly de-pegged. Can you take us through what broke, what held—what did this teach us about stablecoins in that moment? So in terms of the stability of a stablecoin, we did not have the Genius Act in place then. So there are many different approaches to how you provide stability. There were no clear reserve... so you have this token on chains, but it's backed by reserves. There were no clear guidelines from the government as to how those reserves should be structured. And when we were building USDC over the years, we've had different approaches to how we manage our underlying reserves, as has everyone else. Since then, even before the Genius Act, we've moved into this mode that's very conservative. As long as you have trust in the US dollar and the US government, it's a very conservative reserve backing which is how we're able to ensure stability. But it is the case that stablecoins today—this is sort of the next problem with stablecoins—it is the case that they trade against one another in the marketplace. We first lacked regulatory clarity to say, "This is how we achieve stability and this is how the government will enforce protections for everyone." Now the problem is if you launch a stablecoin and you launch a stablecoin and I launch a stablecoin, we can do that because we have regulatory guidelines, but none of our stablecoins will trade as a dollar. We have this... it's sort of a singleness of money problem. If Amazon issues a coin and Walmart issues a coin, well, they're they're going to have a market against one another until we solve this interoperability and this sort of clearing house issue. And that hasn't been clarified by the Genius Act or anything else yet. And in order to clarify that, do you think we need additional government regulation? There's some technical approaches to it, so I think we'll see some solutions that people can rely on—and by people I really mean those businesses who just want to look at this as a dollar. They don't want USDC; what they want is a dollar that moves more efficiently and is more accessible in their markets. And if they have 20 of those, they want to view all of those collectively as just dollars. And so there are some technical approaches to how to how to achieve that, but we may also see it addressed in subsequent legislation that comes out in this space. So many crypto maximalists—you kind of worry that Circle, other similarly centralized stablecoins can lead to a government having full control over individual citizens' ability to transact, monitor all transactions. Do you see this as a risk of heading to a CBDC surveillance world? Is there anything we should be doing to navigate in or out of that? So just on CBDC, just to clarify: Central Bank Digital Currency. There's this there's this idea that maybe the federal government should issue its own stablecoin and issue it directly to individuals. And that's been an approach that has been looked at say in China. Here, generally the way we do things is that we have private sector innovation but with public policy. So we have the government saying how to do it and then we have companies such as Circle and others that are able to actually do that thing. So that's the kind of differ difference between stablecoin issuers in in the US versus the sort of CBDC approach. But the the crux of the question really I think is related to privacy. And it is important to have an open mechanism for value exchange that also has privacy protections. And a simple example of that is if you you're paid on-chain, I shouldn't be able to see your paycheck, and so there needs to be some privacy protections. Another example is for very large transfers internationally there's counterparty info exchange—there's this thing called the travel rule even at relatively low volumes—and so that should be private. You don't need to know my address or Social Security number if I'm part of these transactions. And so there is an there is an imperative to maintain privacy and balance transparency on these open rails with privacy. Yeah, I'll say how we're going about it because I do think it's it's similar at Circle but it's different from other tech companies. "We're Not Writing Code by Hand Anymore. That's Over." | Owen Jennings & David Haber - The a16z Show

a16z(260.0K) ·27:17 ·7.0K views · 2026-04-01
> "We’re not writing code by hand anymore.
TL;DR: Block (formerly Square) recently executed a 40%+ reduction in force (RIF) driven by a paradigm shift where AI agents and "squads of three" now outperform traditional teams of 14, effectively decoupling company output from human headcount. The "December Paradigm Shift":...

TL;DR: Block (formerly Square) recently executed a 40%+ reduction in force (RIF) driven by a paradigm shift where AI agents and "squads of three" now outperform traditional teams of 14, effectively decoupling company output from human headcount.

The Catalyst: Breaking the Correlation Between Headcount and Output

  • The "December Paradigm Shift": Owen Jennings (Business Lead, Block) identifies the first week of December as a turning point where frontier models (Opus 4-6, Codex 5-3) became capable of handling complex, existing codebases rather than just greenfield projects.
  • Decoupling Growth from Hiring: Historically, output scaled with headcount. Jennings argues this correlation is now broken; one or two engineers using advanced AI tools can be 10x to 100x more productive than a full department.
  • Strategic RIF Execution: The 40% staff reduction was not driven by financial distress or "ZIRP" bloat, but by a functional redesign.
      • Development-Heavy Cuts: Reductions were concentrated in engineering, product, and design (EPD), where AI gains are highest.
      • Protected Areas: Compliance, regulatory, and sales teams saw minimal cuts due to the high risk and nuanced nature of those roles.

Internal Infrastructure: The Agentic Operating System

  • Goose (Agent Harness): A model-agnostic substrate developed by Block that allows the company to swap between 120+ different models (OpenAI, Anthropic, open-source) depending on the task. It serves as the foundation for both internal tools and customer-facing products.
  • Builder Bot: An internal autonomous tool that builds features and merges Pull Requests (PRs).
      • Efficiency: Builder Bot frequently completes 85% to 100% of a feature’s code, with humans acting as "final-mile" editors.
  • G2 (Agentic OS): An internal-only system that allows any employee to automate deterministic workflows.
  • Shift in Workflow: Engineering has moved from a linear PR-review-merge cycle to a parallelized system where a single developer might oversee 14 different agents building PRs simultaneously.

Organizational Redesign: From Hierarchies to Squads

  • The "Small Squad" Model: Block moved from a functional hierarchy (e.g., 14-person teams with eight server engineers and four client engineers) to "squads" of one to six people.
  • Flattening the Org: On the development side, Block cut management layers by 50% to 60%, resulting in only two or three layers between the executive team and the "builders."
  • The Demise of Meetings: To facilitate rapid building, Block reduced internal meetings by 70% to 80%, allowing teams to focus on output over coordination.
  • Role Fluidity: In the new structure, the lines between roles are blurring. Most designers and Product Managers (PMs) at Block are now expected to ship PRs directly to GitHub.

Product Innovation: Generative UI and Proactive Intelligence

  • From Static to Generative UI: Jennings predicts the end of rigid app interfaces. Instead of every user seeing the same layout, "Generative UI" creates custom interfaces, charts, and visualizations on the fly based on individual user data.
  • Money Bot (Cash App): Built on the Goose harness, this serves as a "CFO in your pocket" for users, proactively suggesting financial moves and generating custom data visualizations that do not exist in the hardcoded source.
  • Manager Bot (Square): Allows merchants to build custom business applications via natural language. For example, a restaurant owner can ask the bot to create a custom employee scheduling app that integrates with WhatsApp.
  • QA Challenges: Moving to non-deterministic, generative outputs for tens of millions of users creates significant Quality Assurance (QA) hurdles that the company is currently solving through "proactive intelligence" filters.

New Moats in the AI Era

  • The "Vibecoding" Risk: While simple apps can now be "vibecoded" (built entirely by AI) in a week, Jennings argues that distribution, network effects, and hardware remain defensive moats.
  • Deep Context as a Moat: The ultimate defense is a company’s unique understanding of complex signals—in Block’s case, the interplay between buyers and sellers.
  • Iteration Speed: Defensibility now depends on how many times a company can run the "feedback loop" between their proprietary data and their AI builders. Companies that can iterate thousands of times a day via agents will outpace those relying on human-speed development.

***

But yeah, I mean Cash App went from... when I joined Cash App in 2016, we had just started to figure out how to monetize and had our first dollars of gross profit. Overall we've been growing at a healthy clip over the past decade, but Cash App and Afterpay have definitely been growing more quickly. But increasingly we're trying to think about things from an ecosystem perspective. And that's maybe where Goose as a platform comes in, which is we built Goose internally. The way to think about Goose is... it's a nod to Top Gun or whatever, the co-pilot thing. But the way to think about Goose is it's an agent harness and it's model agnostic. So I can run Goose on an Anthropic model, on an OpenAI model, or on an open-source model. There's probably like 120 models that we have. And depending on what I'm trying to do, I'll kind of swap out the models. And then that was useful for a human to use, but we've built the agentic layer on top. So now a lot of the automations at Block are actually routing through the Goose agent harness. And we've been able to leverage this across the products that we're building. So Moneybot, which we like to think of as a CFO in your pocket, is essentially a proactive chatbot that can take actions on your behalf within Cash App. That's built on top of Goose. Managerbot, which is roughly a similar thing on the Square side, is also built on top of Goose. So it's a lot of this foundational work on agentic systems and then the triggers and the underlying data and events that you need to power them. That's working across the entirety of the company. On the product side, I think the biggest shift has really been like we're going from a world where for the past 10 or 15 years, everyone's used to a static UI, a rigid UI. You tap through the UI. Everyone has the same Uber or Lyft or Cash App; it looks the same. That's going to fundamentally change in the next six months. Generative UI is here. We're seeing it with Moneybot, we're seeing it with Managerbot. As the models get better... what is that gonna look like in practice, I'm curious. I think, in the simplest terms, your Cash App should look really different from mine. And the reason why is... okay, well I get my paycheck into Cash App and I'm super into Bitcoin. You could probably achieve that just through personalization; that's not that interesting. What we're actually seeing—and Anthropic had some releases this week that are incredible—is like I can go into Moneybot and say, "How have I been spending my money?" And it'll show me a bunch of charts and visualizations where it is actually, on the fly, generating that visualization. It's not actually in the code itself. So that's really cool. It's also potentially a nightmare from a QA perspective. We need to figure out how you're going to QA all of these non-deterministic outputs for tens of millions of customers. But a great example on the Square side is with Managerbot. Maybe charts aren't that impressive to you, but let's say you own a multi-location quick-serve restaurant. You say, "Hey, could you build me an app where I can manage scheduling for these two locations and automatically fire off text via WhatsApp or Signal or whatever to my employees?" It's actually going to create that app for you. And the way that app looks and feels is not in the source code of the actual application that we push to the App Store. And so I think it gives folks way more control, it's way more personalized, and ultimately I think it'll lead to higher engagement. I think it'll lead to better product discovery. And really I think the key thing is I don't think that if we ask customers to prompt these tools themselves they're going to necessarily know the right prompts and come up with the right answers. So we've invested massively on the proactive intelligence side where what we've found, especially as it relates to money, is we need to be prompting our customers with things that we think make sense for them. And that's where we're creating a lot of the value. Yeah, I mean I think we're all incredibly bullish on the impact of AI in the way that all these businesses run and the products you can create. How does that flow back to your stock price? You know, the business has... the stock has been roughly flat for six or seven years. But the business has grown a lot. To your point, the gross profit per employee's grown massively. Like how do you sort of reconcile that dimension? Yeah. I think... so I think, you know, markets are cyclical and there's all sorts of things that are happening. I remember in 2021 when our stock price was like, I don't know, 260 bucks and I was like "That was a little bit irrational." You can take a kind of longer-term mature view and say, you know, markets are voting machines in the near term but they're weighing machines in the long term. Just focus on building. David and Jonathan earlier talked a bit about kind of defensibility. How do you think about your own moats at Block?
