U.S. Supreme Court Oral Argument on Trump Restricting Birthright Citizenship

C-SPAN(1.8M) ·125:26 ·148.2K views · 2026-04-01
The Supreme Court heard arguments on whether the 14th Amendment’s Citizenship Clause permits the executive branch to deny birthright citizenship to children of undocumented immigrants and temporary visitors based on a "domicile" and "allegiance" requirement.
TL;DR The Supreme Court heard arguments on whether the 14th Amendment’s Citizenship Clause permits the executive branch to deny birthright citizenship to children of undocumented immigrants and temporary visitors based on a "domicile" and "allegiance" requirement. The case...

TL;DR

The Supreme Court heard arguments on whether the 14th Amendment’s Citizenship Clause permits the executive branch to deny birthright citizenship to children of undocumented immigrants and temporary visitors based on a "domicile" and "allegiance" requirement. The case hinges on whether the phrase "subject to the jurisdiction thereof" refers to simple obedience to U.S. laws (*jus soli*) or a deeper political allegiance and lawful residency (*jus sanguinis*).

The Core Legal Dispute: Jurisdiction and Domicile

  • The Government’s "Domicile" Theory: General Sauer argued that the 14th Amendment presupposes a legal domicile—lawful presence combined with the intent to remain—to establish the requisite "allegiance" for citizenship.
      • Allegiance Requirement: The government contends that "jurisdiction" in the 1860s meant more than being subject to police power; it required a total political allegiance, which illegal aliens and temporary "sojourners" cannot legally establish.
      • Exclusion of Classes: Under this theory, children of birth tourists and those in the country illegally would not be citizens because their parents lack the legal capacity to form a domicile.
  • The "Universal Rule" Counter-Argument: Ms. Wang, representing the respondents, argued that the 14th Amendment codified the English common law rule of *jus soli*, where birth on the soil confers citizenship regardless of parental status, with very narrow exceptions.
      • Limited Exceptions: These exceptions traditionally only include children of foreign diplomats, invading enemy armies, and (historically) tribal Indians, all of whom were viewed as being "under a fiction of extraterritoriality."

Interpretation of Precedent: *Wong Kim Ark* (1898)

  • The Role of Domicile: A central point of contention was the 1898 case *United States v. Wong Kim Ark*.
      • General Sauer's View: Pointed out that the term "domicile" appears over 20 times in the *Wong Kim Ark* opinion, suggesting the Court's ruling was limited specifically to the children of *domiciled* aliens.
      • Ms. Wang's View: Argued that while domicile was a stipulated fact in that case, it was not the *holding*. She asserted the controlling rule of decision was the common law, which ignores domicile in favor of the location of birth.
  • Judicial Skepticism: Justice Kagan and Justice Sotomayor questioned why the Court should adopt a "revisionist" history that contradicts a century of understanding. Justice Kagan noted that most of the government’s brief focused on temporary visitors, while the immediate policy target is undocumented persons.

Originalist Context and the 1866 Civil Rights Act

  • Textual Shifts: Justice Kavanaugh and Justice Alito explored the differences between the Civil Rights Act of 1866 (which used the phrase "not subject to any foreign power") and the 14th Amendment ("subject to the jurisdiction thereof").
      • Foreign Power Clause: Justice Alito suggested that a child born to foreign nationals remains "subject to a foreign power" (such as potential military conscription in their parents' home country), which might exclude them under the 1866 Act's logic.
  • The Intent of the Framers: General Sauer cited Senator Lyman Trumbull, a primary framer, who stated that jurisdiction meant "not owing allegiance to anybody else." Conversely, Ms. Wang cited contemporaneous treatises and members of Congress who believed the amendment was intended to be a broad, bright-line rule.

Practical Consequences and Policy Implications

  • Birth Tourism and "Pull Factors": General Sauer argued that unrestricted birthright citizenship has created a "sprawling industry" of birth tourism (specifically mentioning hotspots in China and Russia) and acts as a magnet for illegal immigration.
  • Implementation Challenges: Justice Jackson expressed concern over the "logistics of the delivery room," asking how hospitals would determine a parent's subjective "intent to stay" or immigration status at the moment of birth.
      • Government Response: General Sauer claimed the process would be "transparent" for most, using existing DHS databases to verify status, similar to how Social Security numbers are currently generated.
  • The Risk of Statelessness: Justice Sotomayor and Ms. Wang warned that the government's theory could lead to "thousands of American babies" losing citizenship and could potentially be applied retroactively, despite the government’s request for prospective-only relief.

Notable Quotes

General Sauer: "Unrestricted birthright citizenship... demeans the priceless and profound gift of American citizenship. It operates as a powerful pull factor for illegal immigration and rewards illegal aliens who not only violate the immigration laws but also jump in front of those who follow the rules."
Justice Kagan: "The text of the clause I think does not support you. I think you're sort of looking for some more technical esoteric meaning... you're using some pretty obscure sources to get to this concept."
Ms. Wang: "Everyone born here is a citizen alike. That rule was enshrined in the 14th Amendment to put it out of the reach of any government official to destroy... The 14th Amendment's fixed bright-line rule has contributed to the growth and thriving of our nation."
Justice Alito: "What we're dealing with here is something that was basically unknown at the time when the 14th Amendment was adopted, which is illegal immigration. So how do we deal with that situation when we have a general rule?"
Allegiance to the United States. For aliens, lawful domicile is the status that creates the requisite allegiance, and the text of the clause presupposes domicile. For decades following the clause's adoption, commentators recognized that the children of temporary visitors are not citizens, and illegal aliens lack the legal capacity to establish domicile here. Unrestricted birthright citizenship contradicts the practice of the overwhelming majority of modern nations. It demeans the priceless and profound gift of American citizenship. It operates as a powerful pull factor for illegal immigration and rewards illegal aliens who not only violate the immigration laws but also jump in front of those who follow the rules. It has spawned a sprawling industry of birth tourism as uncounted thousands of foreigners from potentially hostile nations have flocked to give birth in the United States in recent decades, creating a whole generation of American citizens abroad with no meaningful ties to the United States. It's very clear in this court, in all of its early cases interpreting the 14th Amendment, that the one pervading purpose, the main object of the citizenship clause, is to overrule Dred Scott and establish the citizenship of the freed slaves. So there's a constitutional guarantee that applies to both federal or national and state citizenship. And the key point we make there is that that word "reside," if you look at, for example, section 1473 of Justice Story's commentaries, was understood to mean domicile. General Sauer, I want to zoom out a little bit and think about jus soli and jus sanguinis. So as I understand it at the time of the 14th Amendment those were the two dominant approaches—you know, jus soli the English common law roughly following the soil, jus sanguinis roughly citizenship following the parents. Now jus soli was very generous on the soil, the English common law, and so it extended citizenship to those born there who may not have been born of parent citizens. But jus sanguinis, you know, if parents who are citizens had a child abroad then that child's citizenship followed the parents. So one thing that's puzzling me about your argument, when I think about the ratification of the 14th Amendment, in many ways it would have made sense for them—and you acknowledge the jus sanguinis in citing Vattel—it would have made sense in some ways for them to say, okay, we're going to follow if they wanted to accomplish what you're saying they wanted to accomplish, you could say well we're going to follow jus sanguinis because we're going to make it all ride on parentage. So I guess I am looking at your position in this case and it boils down to requiring us to do at least these two things. One is believe that the framers were not importing the common law rule and understanding of birthright citizenship. And the second is to believe that what they were doing was departing from that common law rule in the way that you suggest. That is, in the—they were seeking to have this turn on domicile. I think you have a number of hurdles to accomplish those two things. One of which I think is that when we look at this court's case law, I know no one I think has mentioned Schooner's Exchange, but it appears that that was an 1812 case in which it seems as though the court had already accepted at the time of the ratification of the 14th Amendment that the allegiance that you're talking about was the English common law rule. That in other words, allegiance meant that you are covered by the laws of the jurisdiction, that you can rely on that jurisdiction's protection. That's what allegiance meant. Now you're saying today, no, no, allegiance meant something about loyalty or that kind of idea. But if the Supreme Court had prior to the 14th Amendment established that allegiance meant the common law definition, I think your first hurdle is to help us understand why we would believe that when the common law—when the 14th Amendment was ratified, the framers weren't just incorporating what we had previously said it meant. TL;DR JPMorgan Chase CEO Jamie Dimon warns that geopolitical risks are at their highest level since World War II, while domestic success is threatened by a "softening" of American grit and a corporate leadership class too "chicken shit" to engage in honest policy debate. ...

TL;DR

JPMorgan Chase CEO Jamie Dimon warns that geopolitical risks are at their highest level since World War II, while domestic success is threatened by a "softening" of American grit and a corporate leadership class too "chicken shit" to engage in honest policy debate.

Geopolitical Instability and Global Risks

  • Unprecedented Geopolitics: Dimon views the wars in Ukraine and the Middle East, coupled with tensions with China, as a period of extreme risk not seen in decades.
      • The Iran Threat: He criticizes the West for tolerating Iranian proxy wars for 45 years and highlights the threat to the Straits of Hormuz and global oil prices.
      • China Strategy: While acknowledging China’s technological gains, Dimon argues the U.S. holds a superior hand due to food, water, and energy independence, whereas China faces a "complex neighborhood" and lower per-capita GDP.
  • The "War of Choice" Debate: Dimon pushes back against the idea that confronting Iran is a war of choice, citing decades of American casualties and Iran’s pursuit of ballistic missiles and nuclear capabilities.

The AI Revolution: Cyber Fears vs. 3-Day Work Weeks

  • Cybersecurity as Top Threat: Dimon’s "single greatest fear" regarding AI is its application in cyber warfare. He warns that while AI can defend, it is a potent weapon for catastrophic attacks that could shut down critical infrastructure.
  • Labor Market Displacement: He acknowledges that AI will cause significant job displacement in the short term.
      • Optimistic Long-term View: Dimon predicts future generations may work only 3.5 or 4 days a week, live longer, and see cancer cured through AI-driven breakthroughs.
      • Corporate Responsibility: He advocates for "doubling down" on re-skilling, re-training, and re-locating workers rather than simply cutting headcounts.
  • Guardrails vs. Extinction: While some tech leaders fear "P-doom" (human extinction), Dimon remains skeptical of such catastrophic outcomes, favoring the development of human-led guardrails.

Corporate Leadership and Domestic "Softness"

  • "Chicken Shit" CEOs: Dimon criticizes modern executives for being too afraid to speak the truth on policy or politics, leading to a vacuum where only politicians attempt to solve societal problems.
      • Business Engagement: He argues that business leaders must be more involved in policy-making to prevent the rise of a "permanent political leadership class" similar to Europe’s.
  • National Grit: He expresses concern that America has become "a little soft" and has failed to teach the virtues of the U.S. system in schools.
      • Core Values: He identifies the biggest domestic risk as a loss of "heart, soul, and grit," noting that military strength is entirely predicated on economic strength.
  • The Epstein Scandal: Addressing the banking industry's ties to Jeffrey Epstein, Dimon attributes the oversight to an "inside club" mentality where people prioritize connections over character, while also questioning why the government failed to act sooner.

Political Outlook and Policy Solutions

  • On Donald Trump: Dimon acknowledges that Trump "raised some wonderful issues" regarding trade and immigration that were previously ignored. He supports border security and merit-based immigration but remains cautious about government micro-management of business deals.
  • Political Aspirations: Despite frequent requests to run for office, Dimon labels a presidential run as "quixotic" for him, citing his age (70) and his preference for the platform he holds at JPMorgan.
  • Economic Fixes: He proposes specific "common sense" reforms:
      • Tax Reform: Doubling the Earned Income Tax Credit (EITC) to help lower-income workers.
      • Education: Judging schools and community colleges based on job placement outcomes and starting salaries.
      • Regulatory Reform: Fixing the "joke" of the permitting process that prevents infrastructure from being built.

TL;DR

One month into Operation Epic Fury, the U.S. has systematically degraded Iran’s military infrastructure with over 11,000 strikes, including the destruction of the bulk of the Iranian Navy, to compel a diplomatic resolution that permanently ends Iran’s nuclear ambitions.

Operational Status and Battlefield Dynamics

  • Strike Statistics: U.S. forces have struck more than 11,000 targets in the first 30 days of the operation.
      • Dynamic Targeting: In a recent 24-hour window, pilots conducted 200 "dynamic strikes"—engaging mobile or newly identified targets in real-time based on updated intelligence rather than pre-planned packages.
      • Air Superiority: The U.S. has transitioned to overland B-52 missions, signaling total air dominance as enemy air defenses are suppressed.
  • Iranian Attrition: Secretary Hegseth reported that Iranian missile and drone launch volumes have reached their lowest point since the conflict began.
      • Command and Control: Recent strikes destroyed major command bunkers, leaving leadership without power, water, oxygen, or reliable communications.
      • Morale: Intelligence suggests widespread desertions and key personnel shortages within the Iranian military as strikes intensify.

Naval and Strategic Objectives

  • Maritime Dominance: The Joint Force has destroyed more than 150 Iranian ships, including all Jamaran-class frigates.
      • Strait of Hormuz: The U.S. is prioritizing the neutralization of mine-laying capabilities to ensure the waterway remains open.
  • The "Arsenal of Freedom": General Caine and Secretary Hegseth highlighted a sharp contrast between the two nations' industrial capacities.
      • U.S. Capacity: A major push is underway to revitalize the domestic defense industrial base, which Caine credited as the "beginning of American combat power."
      • Iranian Degradation: Iran’s ability to reconstitute its defense industrial base is currently assessed as "negligible" due to the scale of U.S. strikes on manufacturing nodes and R&D labs.
  • Allied Participation: Hegseth signaled that the U.S. expects international partners, specifically the Royal Navy, to take a larger role in securing the Strait of Hormuz, noting that the U.S. uses the waterway "dramatically less than most."

Diplomatic Strategy and Terms of Engagement

  • Negotiation Status: Active negotiations are ongoing with what the administration describes as a "new regime."
      • "Negotiating with Bombs": While the U.S. prefers a diplomatic "deal," Hegseth emphasized that military pressure will increase in intensity until Iran relinquishes its nuclear material and ambitions.
  • Nuclear Non-Proliferation: The primary objective of the administration remains the absolute prevention of Iran obtaining a nuclear weapon.
  • Alliance Accountability: Secretary Hegseth critiqued some NATO allies for "roadblocks or hesitations" regarding basing and overflight rights, suggesting that future U.S. commitments to collective defense may be viewed through the lens of ally support during this conflict.

Troop Readiness and Internal Morale

  • Operational Tempo: Hegseth noted a distinct shift in troop morale compared to previous conflicts in Iraq and Afghanistan. Soldiers are operating at "wartime speed," focused on "finishing the job" rather than counting days until the next rotation.
  • Force Protection: The Pentagon has prioritized the rapid fielding of hardened bunkers and layered air defenses (Patriots, THAAD, and electronic warfare) to protect forward-deployed personnel.
  • Tactical Ingenuity: Leadership highlighted several instances of junior-level innovation, including an Army officer who developed a new method for neutralizing maneuvering enemy missiles and intel analysts who are refining target packages faster than the enemy can adapt.

Notable Quotes

"I met a junior airman as the sun was going down... who, when asked what they needed, she simply looked up at me with a sly smile on her face and said, 'More bombs, sir. And bigger bombs.' We will happily oblige her." — Secretary Pete Hegseth
"We would much prefer to get a deal... but I didn't mean it flippantly when I said in the meantime we'll negotiate with bombs. Our job is to ensure that we compel Iran to realize that this new regime... is in a better place if they make that deal." — Secretary Pete Hegseth
"To our adversaries out there, I remind you to beware of the American workforce. We continue to press forward in our military objectives... the distance from that factory floor and that assembly line to the front line is incredibly short." — General Dan Caine

I had a chance to bear witness and I witnessed the best of America. I witnessed warriors, a brotherhood of men and women warriors all, active duty, Guard, and Reserve, united in their love for each other, their shared purpose, and their commitment to the mission. I witnessed sheer competency. I watched a Private First Class confidently calling out an enemy missile trajectory to a room full of officers. Everyone focused, the room was locked in. Two enemy missiles successfully shot down. I spoke to Air Force and Navy pilots on the flight line who every day both deliver bombs deep into Iran but also shoot down drones defending their base. Many had just returned from the skies of Iran and Tehran. I put on a headset and spoke to one crew in the cockpit, locked and loaded each and every day. I witnessed ingenuity, American ingenuity. I met the young Army officer who figured out how to neutralize maneuvering enemy missiles, saving countless lives. His commander confirmed that the whoops and cheers erupted in the Tactical Operations Center when his new approach was first successful. I met the Air Force Intel analyst who refines target packages faster than the enemy can adapt. I actually gave him my card and told him to keep me posted on the ground truth. I did the same with his boss, a Colonel with a heart the size of Texas and a beautiful deployment mustache to match. I witnessed lethality. I met a junior airman as the sun was going down and a chill was setting on the tarmac who, when asked what they needed, simply looked up at me with a sly smile on her face and said, "More bombs, sir. And bigger bombs." We will happily oblige her. I met the Army targeting team who found and sunk the pride and joy of the Iranian Navy, their fighting position plastered with images of sunken enemy ships. And I witnessed urgency. Right when we landed, another C-17 landed just minutes after us, and within 30 seconds of the aircraft coming to a full stop, a team on the ground pulled up and the cargo was being uploaded. Wartime speed. To a man and to a woman, on the ground, in the air, on the flight line, and in the TOC, I heard, "We want everything faster. Higher op-tempo. Wartime speed." The feeling was the exact opposite of the rotational units year after year in the wars in Iraq and Afghanistan that we're so familiar with. In those wars, it was always about the next rotation, never knowing when the mission would end or exactly what the mission was year after year. Not with Epic Fury. I witnessed urgency to finish the job, urgency to achieve mission success, not looking at the next rotation, only moving as fast as possible to win. I got a chance to troop the line, to witness firsthand what we already know is true. I spoke to all ranks and all services, none of whom knew we were coming. It was not rehearsed or scripted; sometimes we just wandered. What I witnessed was motivation. It was sheer mission focus. It was the American warrior unleashed. It was the kind of warfighting American spirit that comes with a clear mission against a determined enemy. A crew chief we flew with summed it up nicely. He said, "It's been a busy few weeks, sir. Tough stuff. But I'm so honored to be called up. This fight is long overdue. We need to address it for our kids. We cannot pass the buck. Please thank the President from us." I heard that time and time again. I asked each young American, "What do you need?" and nobody said better equipment, nobody said more comfortable living conditions, nobody said send me home. Well, of course eventually we want all those things, they do too. But what those Americans said to me, young and old, officer and NCO, male and female, black and white, was "Let's finish the mission. Get us even more bombs, bigger bombs, more targets. Let us finish this." In fact, Admiral Cooper noted this morning that the three Air Force captains shot down by Kuwaiti friendly fire early in the fight weeks ago, they never left the theater. All dropped bombs over Tehran last night. These men and women live the Iranian threat every day, incoming missiles and drones, and know what the world would look like if Iran had the most dangerous weapon in the world, a nuclear weapon. As President Trump has said time and time again for years and in this administration, Iran cannot have a nuclear bomb and they won't. These troops, they want to finish this fight for their kids and their grandkids. This is about history. This is about legacy. Success matters. And because of this President and these Americans, we are closer than ever before to winning. President Trump is doing what no other president had the guts to do. Previous presidents were all talk; he's all action. On the battlefield, because of the latitude the President has given us, American firepower is only increasing. Iran's is decreasing. We have more and more options and they have less. Just one month in, only one month, we set the terms. The upcoming days will be decisive. Iran knows that, and there's almost nothing they can militarily do about it. Yes, they will still shoot some missiles, but we will shoot them down. Of note, the last 24 hours saw the lowest number of enemy missiles and drones fired by Iran. They will go underground, but we will find them. We recently destroyed another one of their command bunkers, leaders forced to flee. No water, no power, no oxygen, no command and control. Their faith in their caves is diminishing. The latest intel is clear out of CENTCOM: our strikes are damaging the morale of the Iranian military, leading to widespread desertions, key personnel shortages, and causing frustration amongst senior leaders. Just last night, we had 200 dynamic strikes alone. A dynamic strike is a strike where a pilot leaves and during their flight they get a new target set based on real-time intel given to them. A new launcher, a new location, a new troop formation. A dynamic target is one that changes while you're in the air because of improved intelligence. There were 200 dynamic strikes alone in addition to the pre-planned targets. The video the President posted last night of Esfahan, an ammo depot struck by US bombers, was significant. You see, you don't get to see many of those videos because as a reminder, Iran has still shut off the internet to 99.9% of its population. But if Iran is wise, they will cut a deal. President Trump doesn't bluff and he does not back down. You can ask Khamenei about that. The new Iranian regime should know that by now. This new regime, because regime change has occurred, should be wiser than the last. President Trump will make a deal; he is willing. And the terms of the deal are known to them. If Iran is not willing, then the United States War Department will continue with even more intensity. Standing here this morning in this briefing room, in my mind's eye I'm actually looking out at the groups I met this weekend. I'm looking at the pilots, the logisticians, the intel analysts, the targeters, the sustainers, the flight crews, the air defenders, the base security, and those maintainers we walked up to at sunset with the chill in the air on the flight line. This includes factories, warehouses, nuclear weapons research and development labs, and the associated infrastructure required for Iran to reconstitute its combat capability. Today I want to talk a little bit about a different front line, a line that doesn't have bunkers or guard posts but is just as critical to our nation's security: our national assembly lines. Today I want to recognize a group of Americans who live at the beginning of our nation's combat power: the Americans who actually make our weapons both inside our defense industrial base but even more broadly inside our national industrial base. In every military option, we could not and cannot do our jobs without the men and women across our country who show up every day around the clock to a factory floor, a workshop, or a laboratory to build the weapons and capabilities we need to project American combat power at the time and place of our choosing. These great Americans, and I've had a chance to spend some time with them when I was in the private sector, are the core of American combat power. They're the machinists running high-tech CNC machines, cutting raw blocks of metal into incredibly precise parts. They're the assembly workers painstakingly taking a kit of parts and turning that into a complex guidance system or precision munition or a rocket motor or building a jet or submarine. Or they're the quality assurance technicians who ensure that when a warfighter pulls a trigger, the weapon works every time. This can be and is tough and gritty work. It's not a quiet office and a desk with paper, and there's nothing wrong with that, but this is exactly the way this group of Americans likes it. I know this, I've seen it myself, and I remain deeply honored by that gift. It's often loud and dangerous work. A single misplaced wire, a microscopic flaw in a weld, or an incorrectly calibrated sensor could mean the difference between mission success or mission failure. The difference is measured in the lives of our sons and daughters who we put on a field of battle. Eli Lilly CEO: Our pill supply can 'reach the planet'

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·22:25 ·1.8K views · 2026-04-01
Eli Lilly has announced the approval of **Foundayo** (orforglipron), a once-daily oral GLP-1 pill that leverages small-molecule manufacturing to achieve the massive scale necessary to address the global obesity epidemic.
TL;DR Eli Lilly has announced the approval of Foundayo (orforglipron), a once-daily oral GLP-1 pill that leverages small-molecule manufacturing to achieve the massive scale necessary to address the global obesity epidemic. The treatment offers a needle-free, more affordable...

TL;DR

Eli Lilly has announced the approval of Foundayo (orforglipron), a once-daily oral GLP-1 pill that leverages small-molecule manufacturing to achieve the massive scale necessary to address the global obesity epidemic. The treatment offers a needle-free, more affordable alternative to injectables, with a specific focus on expanding international markets and U.S. Medicare coverage.

Foundayo: Product Profile and Patient Accessibility

  • Administration and Use: Foundayo is a once-daily oral GLP-1 agonist that does not require specific food restrictions, making it easier to integrate into daily medication routines (polypharmacy).
  • Strategic Use Cases:
      • New Patients: Attracting those who view injectables as an "escalation" in treatment rather than a routine care step.
      • Maintenance: Clinical data suggests patients who reach weight goals on injectables can successfully transition to Foundayo for long-term weight maintenance.
  • Patient Choice: CEO Dave Ricks emphasizes that Lilly is indifferent to whether patients choose injectables or pills, provided they remain within the Lilly ecosystem.
  • Dosing Variety: The medication will be available in six different dosage levels to allow for slow, steady progress rather than just maximum weight loss.

Manufacturing and Global Scalability

  • Small Molecule Advantage: Unlike peptide-based injectables, Foundayo is produced via organic chemistry. This allows for significantly higher production scale and lower manufacturing costs.
  • Global Reach: While 1 in 10 Americans currently use a GLP-1, only 0.5% of the eligible population in China has tried one. Lilly intends to seek 40 international approvals in the next year.
  • Supply Chain Stability: Ricks asserts that Lilly has "as much scale as we need" to supply the global market, potentially avoiding the shortages that have plagued injectable versions like Zepbound and Wegovy.

Pricing and Insurance Coverage

  • Cash Pricing:
      • Entry Dose: Available for as low as $149 per month.
      • High Dose: Priced at slightly over $300 per month.
  • Commercial Insurance: Most patients with commercial coverage will pay no more than $25 per month via manufacturer coupons.
  • Medicare Expansion: Under a recent agreement, Medicare Part D coverage for obesity medications will begin this summer, capping out-of-pocket costs for seniors at $50 per month.
  • Employer ROI: Lilly argues that businesses should see a Return on Investment (ROI) within 18 to 30 months due to reduced cardiovascular events and a 90% reduction in the development of type 2 diabetes among employees.

Policy, Competition, and Financial Outlook

  • MFN Policy: Lilly opposes codifying the "Most Favored Nation" (MFN) pricing deal into law, arguing that a rigid legislative mandate would stifle R&D and damage the domestic drug industry.
  • Market Persistence: While competitor data shows only 33% of patients remain on Wegovy after a year, Ricks claims persistence for Zepbound is higher and expects the oral format to improve long-term adherence.
  • Future Pipeline: Lilly is developing Retatrutide (a triple-acting agent) for more aggressive weight loss, alongside 6-7 other incretins currently in the pipeline.
  • Stock Performance: Despite recent market volatility, Ricks remains confident in Lilly's valuation, citing aggressive sales growth goals and the massive untapped opportunity in global obesity management.
"We essentially have as much scale as we need to supply the world with an oral GLP-1 inhibitor... This is really a way we can affect human health at a planet level because obesity isn't just a U.S. problem, it's a global problem." — Dave Ricks
"We don't care what people take as long as they're taking a Lilly product. We want people to be on the medicine that's right for them... If it has Lilly on the box, that's the goal we have." — Dave Ricks
"Generic makers will find out that supply is going to be a gap as well. There's only so much peptide production capacity... on the planet. Most of the expansion in the last three or four years has been inside Lilly and Novo." — Dave Ricks
So we're announcing the approval of Foundayo, which is the trade name for what used to be known as orforglipron. This is our once-daily oral pill that's a GLP-1 agonist. You don't have to worry about food effects when you take it, and it does what a GLP-1 agonist does: it helps you lose weight. Later we'll get an indication for diabetes and potentially other things as well that are under review. It is a simple new option, and of course, price points are a bit lower. We have a lot of manufacturing capacity, so we don't have to worry about a shortage or anything like that, and we'll be taking this around the world. The U.S. is just the first of potentially 40 approvals in the next year or so. This is really a way we can affect human health at a planet level because obesity isn't just a U.S. problem; it's a global problem. Right, and there's a lot to unpack there. But I want to start just for you as a company and obviously as the CEO of Lilly: how important is this milestone for you? It's a big moment. I think, you know, we've obviously been working in this category of medicines for a while. We launched the first GLP-1 medication 20 years ago and have been improving on it ever since. Here is an option that's not more effective—that's been more the pathway us and our competitor have been working on—but it's more accessible. It's easier to fit into your daily routine. We've got great data on maintenance. A lot of people reach their goals on an injectable and then, you know, want to go to something less invasive than injecting themselves once a week. Some people don't mind that, but some people do. So here's an oral option. Some people have been held back by that barrier and now they can have an oral option. Or maybe some people couldn't afford it or didn't have insurance coverage. We hope to expand insurance coverage, and of course, it will be more affordable even for those paying out of pocket. Later this year, the government will expand insurance coverage, including Foundayo, to all seniors. So that's a big step up. I think this year will be a story not just of better efficacy in this category, but here now with Foundayo, more accessibility—easier to access, easier to take. So let's talk about price. You obviously announced some of those prices when you signed that MFN deal with the administration. So, you know, walk us through those cash prices and then also the price that you are going to be charging to insurance companies. Yeah, so this will be available as low as $149 a month for the entry dose. We announced that back in November; the exact same thing we said then is what we'll do now. The highest dose is a little over $300. There are six different dosage forms, so depending on what you need to reach your health goals, there will be different price points. Those are the out-of-pocket prices. Now, many Americans do have insurance coverage, and as I said, seniors will have insurance coverage starting in the summertime. For most Americans with commercial insurance coverage, we have a coupon you can apply to that so it'll be no more than $25 a month with insurance, and then for the seniors in Part D, it'll be $50. Those are pretty affordable price points and I think particularly for seniors, Angelica, you know who are on fixed incomes, the out-of-pocket pricing has—I get more responses from them that that's out of reach. So I think the senior program that the Trump administration is implementing is pretty important, not just to help people control their weight but actually to affect the health status of our seniors. I'm excited to have that happen, and Foundayo will probably be a key tool because it so easily fits into your daily routine. You know, we all know our parents who have a pill box—Monday, Tuesday, Wednesday—and the other medications they take. Foundayo will just go right in that and be simple to integrate in their life and help them lose weight and control their chronic disease. So yeah, a lot of accessibility benefits happening this year. Yeah, and I just want to ask on, you know, the top end of what that exact amount is. When I was looking the other day, it looked like you guys are actually going to be a little bit more expensive than the Wegovy pill, so I just want to make sure I have those numbers right. I think it might be, but remember we have like six different dose levels. I think they have four, so there are some differences there. Most people on GLP-1 agonists—Foundayo I would expect this to be true and it's certainly true for semaglutide, both oral and injectable—don't get to the top dose. Many people find these medicines to be effective at the moderate doses. The entry dose is really a titration step; that's the 0.8 milligrams. But people, you know, on our 5.5 dose or even a little higher, had a lot of success in our clinical trials. I think what we see consumers doing is not seeking the maximum weight loss as fast as possible, but seeking slow and steady progress. Those are more affordable price points and I think again the oral convenience here will really, you know, help people do that quite simply as they go about their day. So you guys have obviously talked a lot about how it's important to have a small molecule versus the peptide as you think about the cost, the scale. So what can you share with us about why that is so important here? Does it, you know, lower the cost for you guys, make it easier to scale? Talk through some of that. Yeah, there're two distinct benefits of this being a small molecule. Organic chemistry makes this medicine. It's a much smaller molecule than a protein or peptide, which is how the other GLP-1s work, including the oral form of semaglutide. What that does is it does allow for scalability and that will allow us to launch this globally in the first instance. Today you can get the oral semaglutide in the US, but you really can't get it elsewhere at the highest dose. This will be marketed around the world as soon as we have regulatory approvals. We essentially have as much scale as we need to supply the world with an oral GLP-1 inhibitor. The other benefit is like other medicines we're used to taking, whether it be a statin or a blood pressure medication or a diabetes medication: we just sort of take the pill in the morning and we don't have to think about other things. It doesn't have food effects; it can be just part of your regular routine. You can actually take it any time of day; if you take your medicines at night, that's okay too. And you know that I think for people living their daily lives with polypharmacy, which is a reality for many Americans with chronic disease, this will fit in perfectly to that and it's just simple. So I think we find consumers really like that when we test that idea in our clinical trials. Yeah, and you talked about, you know, blanketing the world with this and this really being a pill that will reach, you know, all over. So what do you see as the breakdown? Will it be a mostly U.S. product? Will it be more ex-U.S.? How do you view that mix? Yeah, I think there're like three big expansion opportunities here. One is reaching the ex-U.S. markets. And you may know just last year we really just started launching Tirzepatide or Mounjaro Zepbound in most of these places. It's off to a great start as we reported in our Q4 results. But we know that still there're many markets that are underserved because of a combination of the price point and the supply. Those things interact because if you can't supply a market you're not going to lower the price. Here we can reach more people with a single-acting GLP-1 inhibitor. It's not as good as Tirzepatide, and we don't expect it to be in terms of maximizing weight loss, but it is better in terms of simplicity. It's a simple oral pill and you take it in your daily routine. We can reach the planet. The second use case is there are people who have reported—and we see in the real world maybe with the competitor launch even—who've been on the sidelines. For them, GLP-1 medicines weren't for them because they were injectable. And that wasn't necessarily needle phobia per se, but just it felt like an escalation in their health treatments. And they've jumped into the market. I think we're impressed with the start Novo's had and we expect the oral segment to be pretty big in the United States, in Europe, in Japan where the injectables have been available for a while, as sort of a new segment. We don't see a lot of cannibalization, Angelica. When we look at Novo's data, about eight out of ten people trying that have never tried a GLP-1 inhibitor. So that's people coming off the sidelines, and I think that's a very important use case. We'll expand reach here. And then you know I think the other big use case is maintenance. We'll probably get to that later, but Lilly demonstrated some excellent data last year that after reaching health goals with injectables, patients could safely and easily transition to Foundayo and maintain their health status. That's a big question mark that's been in the media—you've covered this—of discontinuation rates but also, "Do I have to take an injectable for my life?" And here an answer appears, which is no; you could switch to Foundayo and maintain most or all of your success on an oral therapy. So that's a big use case as well. All three of those will add up to what we think will be a pretty successful launch, and as I said, we've got supply to meet the need of all those. Yeah, and just on the discontinuation rate, you know, I don't know if you saw today, Novo had this subscription model that it's introducing and there was a stat that they shared that, you know, 33% of people stay on Wegovy after a year. So that's obviously, you know, a lot of people who are going off of the injectables. What are you seeing with Zepbound? Yeah, a bit higher actually. And it's important to frame this because if we think about most chronic meds, that number Novo's giving is about the same as we see with medicines like insulin or metformin in diabetes or beta blockers in hypertension. Discontinuation is a reality of chronic disease. It's not a good thing. We should try to work those numbers up. Persistence is a problem because if you don't take the medications you don't get the benefit. We see with Zepbound and Mounjaro higher rates than that. We haven't published those yet so I won't give the numbers out. But if you normalize for the supply shortage problems that occurred in 2024 and if you look at only people who are insured with a good copay, the persistence rates are pretty strong for us. We would expect a similar thing for Foundayo, or maybe a little better, because I think the simplicity of a once-daily oral pill that you don't have to think about food effects or other things—you just take it in your normal routine with the other chronic meds you take—I think will be conducive to good persistence in this category. Persistence means better long-term health outcomes. So that's great for the health system, it's great for the individuals taking it. And are you surprised by how many people have been willing to do these weekly shots, or not even the pen but also the vials? Is that, you know, what you would have expected maybe as you were going down the path of developing Foundayo? Well, I think we originally thought that would be a bigger barrier. And let's be honest, I think we were all surprised by the success outside of diabetes of our medicines and our competitor. I mean that's why we had supply shortages. Those products are difficult to make at scale. They are injectable, so it's not really a regular thing that Americans have experienced before. But maybe what we've learned is that, you know, weight management is a very concerning condition for consumers. People want to control their weight; they know it's good for their health, they know it prevents chronic disease, and they've tried a lot of other things. In our data, you know, the average patient who takes Zepbound today in the U.S. has a BMI of about 37, so they are obese and overweight. They've tried almost 30 different interventions as adults—diets or, you know, intensive regimens—that they've sought to control their weight, and here now with Zepbound they can do it for the first time. They're willing to inject themselves once a week. But what we're learning, I think, with the oral introduction from the competitor is people may prefer an oral. So two things can be true: that people would be willing to inject themselves to have success in losing weight for the first time in their life, and they might prefer an oral. So we're offering both. They're both from Lilly—Foundayo and Zepbound—and people will now have more choice after today's approval. Yeah, last week I was at an event where your CMO David Hyman was and he said, you know, we don't really care what people take as long as they're taking a Lilly product. Is that, you know, how you feel about all this? Yeah, exactly. And people, investors ask, "Well, you know, do you worry about switching or cannibalization?" We don't. We want people to be on the medicine that's right for them where they can meet their health goals. If it has Lilly on the box, that's the goal we have. And you know, later this year we'll have new data on an even more powerful triple-acting agent, Retatrutide, in the primary obesity studies, and that will present yet another option when that's approved for people who need to lose even more weight. By the way, we have like six or seven other incretins coming through the pipeline. The future will be many more choices and that's a great thing. We hope Lilly is the one presenting those choices, but I think choice is what consumers want and this is a big category with tens of millions of people so it's expected that there'll be options. How big do you think this category is? There's been a lot of debate about that. Well today in the U.S. probably one in ten, one in nine people who are candidates for these medications either with diabetes or obesity are using them. That's pretty low by any standard. We know the frictions: that there's a lot of underinsurance here or non-insurance, and that out-of-pocket costs are out of reach for many people. You know, Foundayo's introduction can, I think, push the barriers of both of those. The government program we agreed to to expand access for seniors is critical because people on fixed income have the hardest time affording these medications, but they also have the most comorbidity of obesity. So that's I think a really important breakthrough for the country. And then we talked about the international opportunity. If one out of ten Americans is on one of these medications, far fewer are internationally. I just got back from China and we estimate 0.5% of those people with obesity or overweight in China are using a GLP-1. That penetration has to go up and I do think it's a very important class of medications to prevent chronic disease. You take a market like China, India, Brazil—we can't possibly make enough injectables to treat a lot higher ratio of those people and so Foundayo will be an important introduction in those markets along with other options that will come eventually. Mhm, but you already have those generics there, so do you think that there's that much pricing power there? I mean, generics will serve part of the market, but I think they're going to find out that supply is going to be a gap as well. There's only so much peptide production capacity and parenteral production capacity on the planet. You'll have to expand it. Actually, most of the expansion in the last three or four years has been inside Lilly and Novo, so not from generic makers. So let's see. Clearly in India and you know Canada, those are the two. I think China will come next year. You'll see introduction of generics and the price points will come down, but if there're shortages at a low price point, I'm not sure that's solving much of a problem. And I want to talk, you know, obviously this MFN deal, access to Medicare that you made. Part of the goal I feel like a lot of drugmakers have talked about is sort of, you know, making this deal, not getting it written into law. But now you have the White House itself pushing to codify MFN. So do you support that effort? We don't. I think we support the effort to negotiate the deals we did because they were tailored to each company's portfolio. They, I think, have a good definition of what MFN is, which is a comparison to other wealthy countries, and by the way, that's like our own policy anyway—is to price similarly in these countries. So we were happy to sign up for that and, of course, we also expanded access to obesity care as part of our deal. I think whenever you—even if you start with the best intentions, and I'm sure the administration is, we speak to them all the time about this topic—when you throw it into the congressional process, what goes in is not what's going to come out. I think we see a lot of people who would rather tear apart the industry we have to reduce prices today and not worry about whether we have any new medicines tomorrow, not worry about whether America will have a robust drug industry and we'll be able to do research in this country. And I worry about those things. So I don't think that's a great idea, and we've been pretty clear with the administration and congressional leaders about that. It's a torturous path to make a law. I think the probability's not that high that this occurs, but you know, we're vocal about it and thank you for asking. If it does happen, will you regret making that deal? I don't regret making the deal we did, no. But I don't actually think one thing leads to another necessarily and, as I said, I would worry that if anything that would pass Congress, it probably looks nothing like our deal. And so that's, you know, that's the caution we give to the administration but also leadership on the Hill. I think they're listening to that but, you know, of course we'll use the tools we have to combat bad policy and we think it would be bad policy. Mhm. And the other thing I want to talk about is the stock. Obviously, you know, it's been on such a tear over the last few years, but this year the stock has been down and there're some questions about, you know, can Lilly really go up from here? So I wonder what your investment... your message to investors is about, you know, the stock price and what you can do to actually get it back up. Yeah, I actually don't get that question from our long investors. I know there's a lot of volatility in the market. I think the general market's down anyway this year and Lilly's become such a large part of everyone's portfolio, we're not immune to that. You know, at the end of the day I think my job is to make the company more valuable, not worry about the valuation today, and that's what I'm focused on. How do we do that? We drive sales growth, and we have aggressive goals this year to do that. We'll report earnings in a few weeks, I'm sure I'll talk to you on that day or some of your colleagues at CNBC. Drive profitability, so do that successfully and reinvest in the future so that our multiple, which is a projection of future growth, is strong. I stand here today and I've never felt better about all three of those things. So, you know, I think we're in good shape. We've got to move the pipeline, we've got to execute as we have been, but we're executing against I think the most significant opportunity that's ever presented itself to the pharmaceutical industry, which is managing overweight and obesity at a global scale. I think Lilly's built for that task, we're up to that task, and we're in the early innings of this story. So people can write what they want or, you know, trade against us; that's fine. Our job is just to deliver. Yeah, and one of the questions people have asked is about, you know, you talked about this Medicare coverage starting later this summer. So what should people expect from that? Do you anticipate that it'll be like the floodgates open and you have all of these seniors signing up? Will it be a little bit of a slower launch? How do you see that all shaking out? Well, I think it'll be pretty robust, frankly. And I think as we projected our guide for this year, we built that in—that the second half would have a little bit of an acceleration to it although we conceded to price discounts which are actually in flight now. So there was a little bit of a step down as we started the year. You know, we see strong demand continue, and I think that'll only accelerate when you put 40 million plus seniors into accessing the medicine for $50 a month. I think you're going to see good response to that. And that's great because, as I said earlier, our seniors are the least able to afford the out-of-pocket solutions we have now, which are a great change. Remember, Lilly innovated that step to really sort of give consumers the same price that PBMs and middlemen were getting directly on Lilly Direct. But now, you know, $50 really makes that achievable for so many on fixed incomes, and we're excited to be a part of that and I think you'll see robust growth associated with that. I also hope we start to see, as we enter the benefit design cycle for next year, more self-employed or employer-sponsored self-insured programs adopt the same policy. That's our advocacy right now; we're spending a lot of time on that topic talking to businesses to expand access, and don't be the lowest common denominator. Usually business leads in healthcare funding; they shouldn't lag the Medicare program. I also though I wonder, you know, you have all these cash programs for patients and I wonder does that disentivize insurers from covering it if they can just say, "Look, you guys can get it for a couple hundred dollars a month, we don't need to pay it?" That's a topic, actually. And Angelica, I think we have to acknowledge that this is such a big category—I mean 70% of Americans are overweight or obese, so adults. So that's a big group and so it's understandable that an employer or insurer would say, "Whoa, I'm not sure I can manage that." In reality, when we have broad coverage in employers, we see, you know, good programming implemented where people try diet and exercise and then they can progress to the medication. Once they're on the medication, they can titrate down their dose, maybe switch to Foundayo after success and reduce cost to the program. Not anything like 70% of adults are going on these medications, so the budget impact is just less. We're also seeing actually, though, the real savings occur, and there're some important studies last year and early this year demonstrating that around 18 to 30 months, businesses should expect ROI, meaning that the cost of the coverage is eclipsed by the savings from healthcare. And that makes good sense. We've got studies that show reduction in cardiovascular risk, 90% reduction in diabetes development which is, you know, a 4x driver of cost when an employee moves from non-diabetic to diabetic. So, you know, this is bearing out and I think there's no doubt in two or three years when we do an interview like this, we'll have much higher insurance coverage. That said, will it ever be universal? I don't think so, because it is a big category. I think some people hold the view that preventive medicines should not be insured. That's a dialogue maybe we should have more publicly, and one of the most profound benefits of a medicine like Foundayo is going to be prevention of very long-term effects. Let's have that debate, but right now we're focused on executing against the Part D expansion and really pointing to the largest employers in the country and saying that's a model you should follow.

Episode 51: Fabrice Grinda - Founding Partner of FJ Labs

Generating Alpha Podcast(1.7K) ·35:55 ·65 views · 2026-04-01
Fabrice Grinda’s career demonstrates that "ruthless prioritization of life design" and a quantitative, high-velocity approach to marketplace investing can yield world-class results, having built several multi-million dollar companies and invested in over 1,100 startups.
TL;DR Fabrice Grinda’s career demonstrates that "ruthless prioritization of life design" and a quantitative, high-velocity approach to marketplace investing can yield world-class results, having built several multi-million dollar companies and invested in over 1,100 startups. ...

TL;DR

Fabrice Grinda’s career demonstrates that "ruthless prioritization of life design" and a quantitative, high-velocity approach to marketplace investing can yield world-class results, having built several multi-million dollar companies and invested in over 1,100 startups.

The Evolution of a Serial Entrepreneur

  • Early Arbitrage: While at Princeton, Grinda launched Princeton International Computers, exploiting the price gap between US retail and European wholesale hardware to fund his education.
  • Aucland (The European eBay): Launched at age 23, this venture taught Grinda the "chicken-and-egg" dynamics of market design. He targeted Europe because eBay had not yet internationalized, proving that successful business models are often portable across borders.
  • Zingy and the Grit of Survival: Following the dot-com crash, Grinda built a mobile media company that scaled from $1M to $200M in revenue in four years.
      • Operational Hardship: He missed payroll 27 times and lived on $2 a day while maxing out credit cards before achieving a successful exit.
  • OLX and Global Scale: Grinda founded OLX to solve the "safety and moderation" failures of Craigslist.
      • Serendipity in Expansion: OLX launched in 100 countries simultaneously to see where "lightning would strike," eventually finding massive product-market fit in Brazil, Portugal, India, and Pakistan.

The FJ Labs Investment Framework

  • High-Velocity Decision Making: FJ Labs famously makes investment decisions in under 60 minutes over two calls. Grinda argues that more due diligence does not necessarily lead to better decision-making.
  • Anti-Bureaucracy: Grinda avoids leading rounds or taking board seats, viewing them as "admin overhead" that distracts from high-level strategic thinking.
  • Marketplace Specialization: The fund focuses almost exclusively on marketplaces because the unit economics and network effects are mathematically predictable.
      • Key Metrics: They look for a 25% or higher "sell-through rate" (liquidity) and decreasing Customer Acquisition Costs (CAC) as the business scales.
  • Portfolio Construction: Grinda follows the "power law" of venture capital, aiming for 400-500 companies per fund to ensure enough exposure to "unicorns."

Life Design and the "Renaissance Man" Philosophy

  • Optimizing for Fun: Grinda believes that people motivated by "fun" will always outwork and outlast those motivated by "obligation" over a 30-year horizon.
  • The 2012 Reset: At the height of his success, Grinda gave away all his possessions and spent years couchsurfing and living in Airbnbs to "re-examine life from first principles."
      • Current Setup: He now uses a "tri-location" model (New York for intellect, Turks and Caicos for beach/sport, Revelstoke for mountains) to prevent burnout.
  • The Polymath Approach: Despite the common advice to be "single-mindedly focused," Grinda attributes his success to being a "Renaissance man," reading 100 books a year and engaging in diverse sports and philanthropic efforts.

Marketplace Evaluation Metrics

  • Liquidity Thresholds: A service marketplace should ideally represent 25% or more of the supply side's total revenue to be considered "sticky."
  • Seed Stage Benchmarks:
      • For standard marketplaces: ~$150k monthly Gross Merchandise Volume (GMV).
      • For B2B/Low-Take-Rate marketplaces: $750k to $1M monthly GMV.
  • The Role of Technology: Grinda views technology as inherently "deflationary" and the primary tool for solving global crises like inequality and climate change.
"In order to succeed you need to be willing to fail. The costs of failure are actually very low... You may lose one or two years in your rat race alternative, but who cares about the rat race anyway?" — Fabrice Grinda
"I’m not optimizing life for impact; I’m optimizing life for having fun and playing the game of life well. It’s a combination of doing things I love, meeting interesting people, being an amazing father, and building." — Fabrice Grinda
"People who have fun will beat people who do things out of obligation every single time." — Fabrice Grinda
This week on Generating Alpha, I sat down with Fabrice Grinda, serial entrepreneur, prolific angel investor, and founding partner of FJ Labs, one of the most active venture funds in the world. Fabrice grew up in France, studied at École Polytechnique and Princeton, and launched his first startup in 1998 at the age of 23. He went on to build and sell multiple companies, most notably OLX, the classified ads platform that reached 300 million users across 40 countries before pivoting full-time to investing. FJ Labs has now backed over 1,100 startups across six continents, including Alibaba, Coupang, Delivery Hero, and Rappi, with a portfolio valued at several billion dollars. Beyond his track record as an operator and an investor, Fabrice is one of the most unconventional figures in tech and finance, known for his ruthless prioritization of life design, his quantitative approach to evaluating marketplaces, and his willingness to share his frameworks with total transparency. In our conversation, we talked about growing up in France and catching the entrepreneurship bug, what he learned building and losing companies in the early internet era, why he became so obsessed with marketplaces, the specific metrics he uses to evaluate marketplace businesses, and how FJ Labs decides whether to invest or not in under 60 minutes. We also discussed the difference between being an operator and an investor, how he thinks about life design and optimizing for happiness, and much more. If you enjoy this episode, please follow the podcast and rate it five stars on Spotify, subscribe on YouTube, and share it to anyone who you think might find it valuable. I really enjoyed recording this episode with Fabrice and I hope you guys enjoy listening. Thank you. Thanks Fabrice for coming on, I really appreciate it. Thank you for having me. Well, I want to start where I always do, the beginning. If I'm correct, you grew up in Nice, but at the age of 22 you were already running a startup out of Princeton. Tell me about young Fabrice, what your childhood looked like, and where you think your drive came from. I'm not sure where ambition comes from, but like I was five and I was already like, you know, wanted to make a ripple in the fabric of the universe. I was ambitious, right? My role models growing up were like Augustus and actually Genghis Khan and Alexander Hamilton, and I thought long and hard about the role I wanted to play in the world. And yeah, actually I considered going down the political path but then pretty rapidly, I mean early teens, realized that that felt corrupt, corrupting, and limited to your nationality. For instance, in Nice, you're not going to become president of the U.S. And at the age of 10, I fell in love with computers. Right, I got my first PC and it was love at first click. I knew that immediately we were meant to be together, and it was going to be the form of my both artistic expression and to have impact in the world. At that time, my role models were Bill Gates and Steve Jobs. Clearly I could see that their vision, and Bill Gates at the time used to say, you know, a computer in every home, was democratizing and providing access to information. Information technology was a means to touch billions of people absent borders and limitations. And so, it just so happened my hobby became a ginormous industry that could actually influence the world. And if I'm correct, you went off to Princeton and eventually graduated with a degree in economics. But while you were a student you were running this company called Princeton International Computers, which I think exported hardware to Europe. Tell me a bit about that, and then ultimately after that, instead of going to Goldman or McKinsey like your average Ivy League student, you went to McKinsey for a bit and then went off to start your own company. Tell me about that experience in college and why you went off to the races in the startup world so quickly. Well, as a computer nerd I was already building computers from spare parts, you know at like age 10 to whatever, 17. And so I was always like taking them apart, building them, etc. I built a BBS for people to connect, so kind of the ancestry of the internet. When I got to the U.S., I realized that retail prices in the U.S. were lower than wholesale prices in Europe. And so, it wasn't all that hard to imagine. Also at the time, companies like Intel were not really global companies. They would release in the U.S. first, and things would get to Europe like six months or 12 months later. So there was an opportunity for grey market exports where you could buy in the U.S. from wholesale and sell to retailers in Europe who were basically my customers. And so it worked for high-value, low-weight, low-volume items like hard drives, motherboards, processors, and memory, that you could basically FedEx from the U.S. to Europe. And I did that, you know, with no capital because I could get paid upfront and I would pay my wholesalers like at Net 30 basis. And so I had a cashflow profitable business from day one and cleared whatever, 50k a year in net profits, net of paying for college. So that was pretty good, I left college with no debt. But I also knew I wanted to be a tech founder. And the thing is, I was—it was pretty obvious there was a tech bubble, it was starting to inflate. I mean Netscape went public in 95, Amazon had already come to the fore. And so I graduated in 96. I wanted to be a tech founder, but I'm like, you know what, I'm 21, I'm shy, I'm introverted, I'm like really Sheldon Cooper-esque. And I worry that if I build a company I'm going to fail. If I join a startup they're not going to take me seriously. So why don't I go to McKinsey? It's like business school except they pay you, where I could learn oral and written communication skills, I could learn how to work in teams and public speaking, and then I can go build my startup. In hindsight, I'm not sure that was the best choice. Partly, you learn just as much on your own as you learn at another place. Building a company I probably would have failed actually, but it would have been an amazing learning experience. If I joined a startup, probably the same thing, I would have learned a lot. So I think all three were probably viable, and after two years at McKinsey I'd learned what I needed to learn and I was ready to go and build startups, which frankly I knew I wanted to do even before entering Princeton. And tell me about that first startup. If I'm correct it was called Aucland. It was kind of an eBay competitor in France. Walk me through that kind of founding moment, how it developed, and ultimately what you learned from that. So I was looking for ideas and the problem is, back in 98 when you're starting to build tech companies, it took a lot more capital. You needed like Oracle database licenses and Microsoft web servers, you needed to build your own data center. There was definitely no cloud, but there was not even a Rackspace, like a data center. So it was much more capital intensive. And most of the ideas that were interesting to people, like for E*TRADE you need a banking license, Amazon you needed inventory and supply chain management. All these things were not appropriate for a 23-year-old with no money. And so, but I'd studied market design in college, I was an economics major and loved the way, you know, markets work writ large. And so when I randomly encountered the site of eBay, I realized that while eBay has a chicken-and-egg problem of do you get supply or demand first, it was a problem I was particularly well-suited to address. This was given my understanding of market dynamics and liquidity and elasticity of supply and demand, and thinking through how to build supply and matching it with demand, etc. And it didn't require all these other specialized skillsets that these other ideas required. And so, another love at first click moment, I saw eBay and I'm like, oh, it brings transparency and liquidity in otherwise opaque and fragmented markets of like these garage sales that, you know, as a seller you're unlikely to find a buyer, as a buyer unlikely to find what you're looking for. In every other category and vertical, I know how to do this. Now, at the time they were really a U.S. company, they had not yet internationalized, and so I'm like, you know, maybe the easiest thing to do, lacking other better ideas for my skillset, is why don't I take this idea and bring it to Europe. And yeah, that thesis worked. The ideas that worked in one country work in other countries, and I had the skillset to build what turned out to be one of the top three auction sites in Europe. And after that, you started a company which is a mobile media company in a totally different kind of sector, scaled it to ultimately 200 million in revenue and I think in four years. That business had nothing to do with the business you had before. What was the insight there and why do you think you were able to replicate kind of the success across the sectors? Yeah, so people like to pigeonhole you in oh, you're a mobile media founder, you're an auction founder, whatever. Like the reality is the skillsets are transferable. You know, the ability to think through business models and plan and to write a deck and raise capital and hire a team and do business development deals, they're all the same. And it doesn't matter what you're building, you can figure it out. I mean Elon is a great example of that, right, if you look at the breadth of companies that he's building. And the reason I chose that was frankly much more tactical, meaning the bubble had burst. I'd gone from zero to hero back to zero again. The company I'd built was acquired by another company whose stock fell from 10 billion market cap to 30 million, you know, a 99% decrease. I'd lost everything. So I felt that I'd missed the biggest opportunity of my life to be—I was at the right time, at the right place, with the right skills, at a moment where you were valued, which was not the case before, and I'd failed to capture on that opportunity. So I felt long and hard like, do I go back to McKinsey, do I go to business school, do I go to whatever, Goldman, do I go into PE, and I'm like, you know what, I didn't do any of this for the money. I liked creating something out of nothing, I want to be a tech founder. Now it seems that this tech thing is going to be a tiny niche, there's no money in it. Doesn't matter, I'd rather be there than not. But in a world where there's no third-party capital, VC is dead, I need to find an idea that I can make profitable pretty quickly. And so in a way I did a reverse arbitrage. I saw that ringtones were popular and successful and profitable, more importantly, in Europe and Asia, and in the U.S. market it was completely backwards. There was no SMS, there's no text messaging, but I thought it was bound to happen. I'm like, you know what, why don't I bring the idea of ringtones and then of course mobile games, wallpapers, etc., to the U.S. market. And I kind of timed it right. I mean, the first two years were a slog. I borrowed 100,000 on my credit cards, I missed payroll 27 times, I lived in New York for 18 months on like $2 a day, sleeping on the couch of the office, couldn't even afford coffee. It was like four cups of ramen noodles per day, that's it. But I managed to grab a victory from the jaws of defeat to your point. I grew revenues from 1 million in 02, to 5 in 03, to 50 in 04, to 200 in 05, profitable, sold the company and did very well. Now, the thing is, I didn't love the idea I was selling, right? Like I want to make a positive impact in the lives of people. Ringtones provide social currency to teenagers, it's okay, but it wasn't like my core mission. But it's okay, I was willing to compromise on the idea in order to be a tech founder. Now, turns out I was wrong, the internet wasn't dead, it wasn't this niche little thing, it was actually bigger than I thought. And so once I sold the company—and I did stay after I sold it for 18 months because it was interesting to scale it to be part of a publicly traded company—I'm like, you know what, now I have the capital to fulfill my dreams and ambition, let's go build what I really want to build. Tell me about that moment where, like, if you kind of maxed out your credit lines, you missed payroll, if I'm correct you said 26 or 27 times, you were living on like a few dollars a day. Tell me about that moment, like what were you thinking through, how did you get out of it? The—so first of all, there's a saying in French which is "You can't shave an egg." You have—I had nothing to lose, you know. I had nothing to begin with, so you know, declaring bankruptcy when you have zero doesn't cost you anything, it's still zero. And I find that most people in the world are more risk averse than they should be. And they're like, oh what if I go bankrupt, what if I fail, but the reality is, people in our position, we have really nothing to lose. What's the worst that can really happen? Oh, I go live at my parents' for a few months, or on my friends' couches. You know, I'm not going to be in the streets. And I have the skillset that I can get a job. I can go and work in BD or go back to McKinsey or go to Goldman or whatever. Like, that's the backup, right? It's not as though we're going to be under a bridge. The problem is people's egos are too tied to never having failed and they're not willing to fail. But in order to succeed you need to be willing to fail, and I see it in the fundraising basis. In order to get one VC to say yes you have to get like 99 noes, and it might not even be 99, it might be 299 or whatever. And most people are not willing to put themselves out there and face failure, but like the costs of failure are very low. The worst that can happen is maybe you lost one or two years in your rat race alternative, but who cares about the rat race anyway? Like you should live the life that you want to live. Thank you, and back to the episode. And I'm super interested in that company that you built after the kind of the ringtone company, which you said was like the real thing you're passionate about. And if I'm correct, funny little anecdote, I think in 2006 you reached out to the founders of Craigslist and offered to run it for free or to buy it. Ultimately they didn't let you, so you started OLX. What was Craigslist leaving on the table? Why did you want to take it over and have that lead to you starting OLX? Well the observation was like Craigslist was providing a valuable public service to humanity, right? They're mostly free and they were letting people find roommates and apartments to rent and cars and jobs and buying and selling and creating massive liquidity. So it's doing an amazing job for a lot of people, but because of the stance they take where they're not moderating, they're letting the community moderate, it's full of scams, it's full of phishing, it's full of prostitution. It's like a dangerous place frankly for women to be. And people forget women are the primary decision makers in all household purchases. The—there's a level of serendipity in these marketplaces and these sites. You never know when you hit lightning in a bottle. I mean we were like massive. I never even set foot in the country at that time just because somehow the right users started using it and we hit like the zeitgeist. After we had the for-sale we did cars, after cars real estate, etc., and then we went back to more countries. And so we ended up winning in about 30 countries. If you're a service marketplace, you represent 25% or more of the revenues of the supply, whatever category you're in. What does retention look like? It's fantastic. If you have network effects kicking in, typically your customer acquisition costs decrease as you scale.
