Apple built a hardware empire in its first 50 years. The next 50 could be defined by AI.

CNBC Television(3.4M) ·15:45 ·9.7K views · 2026-04-01
As Apple marks its 50th anniversary, the company is pivoting from its legacy as a hardware-first pioneer to an AI-driven services powerhouse, leveraging a "late mover" strategy by partnering with rivals like Google and OpenAI to revitalize Siri and secure its dominance for the next half-century.
TL;DR As Apple marks its 50th anniversary, the company is pivoting from its legacy as a hardware-first pioneer to an AI-driven services powerhouse, leveraging a "late mover" strategy by partnering with rivals like Google and OpenAI to revitalize Siri and secure its dominance for...

TL;DR

As Apple marks its 50th anniversary, the company is pivoting from its legacy as a hardware-first pioneer to an AI-driven services powerhouse, leveraging a "late mover" strategy by partnering with rivals like Google and OpenAI to revitalize Siri and secure its dominance for the next half-century.

The Siri Paradox: From Pioneer to Laggard

  • Lost Lead: Despite launching Siri in 2011 as the first mainstream voice assistant, Apple "blew a five-year lead" by failing to evolve the platform beyond basic tasks while competitors like Google and OpenAI moved toward Large Language Models (LLMs).
  • Original Vision: Siri co-founder Dag Kittlaus notes that Steve Jobs believed the original team had "cracked the code" on AI. However, the technology of 2011 couldn't support the full vision, and development stalled following Jobs' death.
  • Technical Barriers Removed: Siri’s creators argue that modern LLMs finally provide the "brain" required to fulfill their original 2010 goals, but Apple must now play catch-up to integrate these capabilities.

Strategic "Coopertition" with Rivals

  • The Google Gemini Partnership: Apple is reportedly paying Google $1 billion annually to use Gemini and Google’s cloud technology to power a revamped Siri. This follows a previous, though complicated, integration attempt with OpenAI.
  • Existing Financial Ties: The relationship is a "frenemy" dynamic; Google already pays Apple over $20 billion annually to remain the default search engine on iOS devices.
  • Late Mover Advantage: Analysts suggest Apple is intentionally letting competitors spend "hundreds of billions" on infrastructure and R&D, allowing Apple to observe market failures (like the "Netscape effect") before perfecting and implementing the tech.
  • Dangerous Dependency: Some experts warn that relying on Google for a core value proposition creates a "dangerous dependency" that Apple must eventually engineer its way out of to maintain control.

Privacy as a Competitive Moat

  • Structural Obstacle vs. Advantage: While Apple's strict privacy standards have historically slowed AI development (which requires massive data harvesting), they are now being positioned as a unique selling point.
  • The Walled Garden: Apple aims to create a "trusted assistant" that operates within a private, on-device ecosystem—a "walled garden" approach that provides security and customization that data-hungry rivals like Google cannot easily replicate.
  • Vertical Integration: By designing its own silicon and controlling the entire tech stack, Apple can run AI more efficiently on-device rather than relying exclusively on the cloud.

Financial Pivot: The Rise of Services

  • Services Growth: Under CEO Tim Cook, services have become Apple’s most profitable segment. In 2025, services revenue reached a record $109 billion (up 14% from 2024), accounting for roughly 26% of total business.
  • Hardware as a Gateway: New products like the MacBook Neo are designed to reach lower price points, specifically to onboard more users into the Apple ecosystem and increase the recurring services subscriber base.
  • Future Devices: While there is speculation about screenless AI pins (like the Sam Altman/Jony Ive project) or smart glasses, experts believe the handheld touchscreen device remains the central hub, with foldable iPhones expected to be the next major hardware iteration.

Cultural Evolution and Risks

  • Ship to Perfection vs. Rapid Iteration: Apple’s traditional culture of shipping "perfect" hardware is at odds with the AI era's requirement for shipping imperfect, rapidly iterating software.
  • The "Yahoo" Risk: Industry veterans warn that if Apple fails to solve the AI digital assistant problem, it risks becoming like Yahoo—a pioneer that failed to evolve at the necessary pace to remain a market leader.

Apple's first 50 years were defined by iconic hardware, products that changed how people worked, communicated, and lived. Very few companies have reached this 50-year mark, let alone set the gold standard across multiple eras of technology, from personal computing to the smartphone. But the real question now, in the age of AI, is can Apple hold its dominance or will it go to someone else? What is the weather like today? Here's the forecast for today. It is that easy. In 2011, Apple looked early. Siri was the first mainstream voice assistant, debuting years before Google Assistant and Amazon Alexa. But as AI became the defining technology of a generation, Apple failed to turn that early advantage into lasting leadership. They basically blew a five-year lead. It was too soon. The technology wasn't right. And so LLMs are bringing that now. Meanwhile, Google built Gemini, OpenAI built ChatGPT, Anthropic built Claude, and Siri, despite reaching more than a billion users, never evolved into the platform that its creators envisioned. That's the hardware company mindset in action. We would kill to have the technology back then that exists now. So there are no further technical barriers to any part of the Siri vision that we had from the old days. In the 20-plus years that I've been investing in and analyzing this company, this is the biggest delay that they've had by orders of magnitude. Now Apple is making its biggest bet yet, turning to a longtime rival to power a revamped Siri. They've made it clear they don't want to build the brain. They want to leverage the brain. Apple has, I think, recognized that they need partners for AI. I don't think you could find any AI hyperscaler who has more data that would be particularly useful for the kinds of things Apple is interested in than Alphabet-Google. Apple today needs to catch up and they're working very hard to do that. They are at a fork in the road when it comes to their products being meaningful over the long term. Because if they don't solve powering an AI digital assistant, somebody else will, and that could cause a very big company today to be much smaller in the future. Apple has to underpromise and overdeliver and stop trying to say everything's AI-ready. Apple is "buy it today and it works." It's not a promise of the future; it's a promise of today. Apple's rise was built on great hardware. It revolutionized the tech world, bringing the personal computer to the masses. I had wanted a computer my whole life—the huge mainframes, switches and lights all over the front panels—and I had built one of those of my own design. So then I sat down and I wanted to make things more human. And then Steve Jobs came into town. I told him, "You got to come and see this, see the excitement." So the question hanging over the company at 50 is really whether the culture that produced the iPhone can adapt to an era where the product is never truly finished. Tim did a tremendous job of taking it to the next level. I think in the world of AI, it's going to need a new thought leadership. The fact that Apple's lived through a couple of transitions, and the first one, which was the most meaningful, was moving away from centralized computing to personal computing. And that even became even more fine-grained with mobile computing where we actually have things in our pockets, not just in our desktops. Having survived those transitions—or actually enabled them—Apple is probably wary of centralization once again when it comes to AI. Apple got a head start with Siri and let it slip. Siri wasn't created by Apple. The technology was built by Stanford Research Institute cohorts Dag Kittlaus, Adam Cheyer, and Tom Gruber. In early 2010, Siri launched as an app in the App Store and caught the attention of Steve Jobs. Steve Jobs tried it a few weeks later, called us up unannounced. We went to his house and he said he wanted to buy the company. We weren't really ready to sell. He called me 34 days in a row. He's calling me at midnight. Steve Jobs had been tracking AI for a decade before Siri. What did he see in your demo specifically that made him think that you'd cracked the code here? Apparently, a bunch of people ran up and showed it to him and his comment was, "This is the first time that I saw somebody crack AI." He had never seen anything like it before. Siri launched with the iPhone 4S in 2011, but Jobs didn't live to fully realize his vision for what the voice assistant could be. Steve literally was fighting to make sure that he was there to see the Siri launch. He died the next day. Siri was just too young and it didn't get that attention that it needed to be able to be the vision and the execution that Steve thought it should have been. Now Siri got faster and more reliable over the years, but the scope of what it could actually do for users barely expanded. In 2018, Apple recruited John Giannandrea, one of Google's top AI leaders, to accelerate its efforts. But progress remained slow. They lost a little bit when Steve died. They worked to make Siri better technically, but I also think that the number of things, for example, that Siri could do for you really never went anywhere. Here's what's coming up in the NBA for today. Investors have been disappointed for the broader part of two and a half years. They missed AI and have missed it even before that. Apple's scale gives it a tremendous edge. It already has widespread reach with its devices. ChatGPT has 800 million users, but Siri has a billion plus even today. So the users are already there. It recently released the MacBook Neo, bringing more people into the Apple ecosystem at a lower price point. OpenAI President Greg Brockman: AI Self-Improvement, The Superapp Bet, Path To AGI, Scaling Compute

Alex Kantrowitz(52.1K) ·73:24 ·20.6K views · 2026-04-01
OpenAI is pivoting its strategy toward a "Superapp" that integrates reasoning, coding, and browsing, while President Greg Brockman predicts the arrival of "jagged" AGI—capable of any intellectual computer task—within the next few years.
TL;DR OpenAI is pivoting its strategy toward a "Superapp" that integrates reasoning, coding, and browsing, while President Greg Brockman predicts the arrival of "jagged" AGI—capable of any intellectual computer task—within the next few years. OpenAI is shifting focus from broad...

TL;DR

OpenAI is pivoting its strategy toward a "Superapp" that integrates reasoning, coding, and browsing, while President Greg Brockman predicts the arrival of "jagged" AGI—capable of any intellectual computer task—within the next few years.

The Strategic Pivot: The OpenAI "Superapp"

OpenAI is shifting focus from broad research demonstrations to real-world utility, prioritizing a unified "Superapp" over divergent projects like video generation.

  • Product Unification: The goal is to combine ChatGPT, Codex (coding), and web browsing into a single endpoint.
      • Personal Assistant: The app is envisioned as a "personal AGI" that knows user context, has memory, and is aligned with specific goals.
      • Codex for Everyone: Originally for engineers, Codex is being repurposed as a general tool for "computer use," allowing non-programmers to automate software, Excel, and Word tasks.
  • Deprioritizing Sora: While Sora (video) is impressive, OpenAI is focusing compute on the GPT reasoning branch.
      • Branching Challenges: Brockman noted that maintaining multiple distinct "tech trees" (like diffusion for video vs. transformers for reasoning) is unsustainable in a compute-constrained environment.
      • Robotics Link: Sora research continues primarily in the context of robotics rather than as a standalone consumer video tool for now.

Technical Roadmap: "Spud" and the Path to AGI

OpenAI is preparing to release a new series of models that represent years of accumulated research breakthroughs.

  • The "Spud" Model: A new base model (pre-train) is imminent, incorporating roughly two years of research.
      • Qualitative Shift: Brockman expects it to eliminate the "friction" of today’s models, solving much harder, open-ended problems with longer time horizons.
      • Raising the Ceiling: The model has already shown the ability to solve unsolved physics problems in 12 hours that previously stumped human researchers.
  • Defining AGI: Brockman believes we are "70-80% there."
      • Jagged Capability: He expects AGI within "the next couple of years." It will be "jagged," meaning superhuman at complex tasks (coding, science) while still potentially struggling with some basic human intuition.
      • Autonomous Researchers: By Fall 2025, OpenAI expects to use AI "researchers" to run experiments autonomously in silicon, effectively using the AI to build the next version of itself.

The Compute War: Scaling and Infrastructure

Compute is no longer viewed as a cost at OpenAI, but as the primary driver of revenue and capability.

  • Compute as a Revenue Center: Brockman compares buying GPUs to hiring salespeople; as long as the product is scalable, more compute directly equals more value and revenue.
  • Investment Strategy: Brockman admitted telling his team to buy "all of it" regarding compute. Even with massive investment, OpenAI remains "compute-strapped" because demand for reasoning models consistently outstrips supply.
  • Infrastructure Realities: Data center construction requires 18-24 month lead times. Brockman argues that those who didn't commit to massive infrastructure builds by late last year will be unable to compete.

The Future of Work: The "CEO of Agents"

The paradigm of work is shifting from "doing" to "delegating" as AI agents become more autonomous.

  • Management Paradigm: Users will act as the "CEO of a fleet of hundreds of thousands of agents."
      • Accountability: Brockman warns against "abdicating responsibility." Humans must remain the accountable party, providing vision and feedback even if they don't perform the mechanical tasks.
      • Losing the "Pulse": There is a risk of losing one's "finger on the pulse" of a problem when delegating to AI, which requires users to build trust through oversight rather than blind reliance.
  • Economic "Takeoff": Brockman describes the current moment as the "takeoff phase," where AI moves from a "sideshow" to the main driver of economic growth and national security.

Notable Quotes

"The scariest moment at OpenAI was actually after we launched ChatGPT... I remember being at the holiday party and just feeling this vibe of 'we won.' I have never felt that. I was like, no, we are the underdog and we always have been." — Greg Brockman
"It's extremely clear that we are going to have AGI within the next couple of years in a way that it's still going to be jagged, but the floor of task will just be almost for any intellectual task of how you use your computer, the AI will be able to do that." — Greg Brockman
"I remember talking with my team... I said 'how much compute should we buy?' I said 'all of it.' ...No matter how much we try to build, I know we're not going to be able to keep up with the demand." — Greg Brockman
"AI is going to free up so much time to increase human connection... being a human is about being here, being present, connecting with other humans." — Greg Brockman

And so giving people leverage, agency, making it so that if you have a vision something you want to accomplish that you can have a fleet of agents that will go do it for you, but if you think about how the world works, that at the end of the day there's an accountable party, right? If you're trying to build a website and your agent messes it up and your user is affected, it's not really the agent's fault, it's your fault. And so you need to care and I think for people to use these tools right, you need to realize that human agency, human accountability, that's a core part of the system, how the human uses the AI. That's something that is deeply fundamental. And so I think the important thing is that as a user of these agents and we do this within OpenAI, you cannot abdicate responsibility, you cannot just say the AI is just going to do stuff. Of course, but you said feel your you're losing your pulse on the problem itself, that's different than accountability layer to it. Well, to me they actually are linked together because the point is that if you if you're a CEO and you're too far from the details, right, if you're running this company, you're running this this team, and that you've lost your finger on the pulse, that is something that's not going to lead to great results. And so the point that I was trying to make there is that not that it's a desirable thing for humans to not have to know about what's going on. There's some details that because you you can trust like if you are working with a team like a general contractor to build a house, there's a bunch of details there that you probably don't need to worry about because you can trust that that they'll be taken care of. But at the end of the day if there are details that are wrong, you should care, you should be aware. And so this is I think an important nuance of you cannot just blindly say I'm okay with losing my finger on the pulse. That we need to lean in and say I need to keep it there to really understand the strengths and weaknesses and that as you disengage from some of these details, these lower-level mechanical things, you should do it because you have built trust with the system that it will do a good job. One last question about the models. You talked a little bit about the evolutions that the models have gone through. Pre-training and fine-tuning, reinforcement learning that gets it more equipped to solve problems step by step and go out in the on the internet and do things. And now we're in this moment where there the models have learned through that process to use tools and correct me if I'm wrong on this one. What what is next in that progression? Well, I think that the world that we're in is one of this increasing capability and depth of what the machine can do. And some of this is about we've got this tool use, but now we also need to actually build really great tools. You think about something like computer use, an AI that can actually use a desktop, then it is really able to do anything that you can do. But we also have to build a little bit for the machine to think about how in the enterprise credentialing works, how do audit trails and observability work. So there's a lot of technology to build to catch up with what the core model capability is. And I think the overall direction of travel includes things like a really great speech interface, so you can just talk to your computer naturally and just as naturally as this conversation and it understands you, it does what you need, has good advice, it's able to surface that I've been working on this thing, I have a problem, here's it. You wake up in the morning and it says here's your daily report of how much progress your agents made overnight. Maybe it's running a business for you, which I think is going to be a huge application of this technology; the democratization of entrepreneurship is absolutely coming. And it will say here's these problems, there's this customer that's upset, you know that they want to talk to a real human, like you should go talk to them—all of that's going to happen. And then I think that the raising of the ceiling of ambition, of challenges humanity can solve, that is also a next step for this technology and we're seeing the leading edges of it. But the thing that I am just very excited to see is almost if you remember AlphaGo Move 37, right? This move that no human ever would have come up with, it changed humanity's understanding of the game. That is going to happen in every single domain. It will happen in science, in math, in physics, in chemistry. It's going to happen in material science, it's going to happen in biology, it's going to happen in healthcare, drug discovery, but it may also even happen in literature, in poetry, in a bunch of other fields that are going to unlock human creative understanding and ideation in ways we can't imagine right now. Why do you think that hasn't happened yet given how strong you say the models are? Well, I think that there is an overhang of what the models are capable of and how people are using them. So the application—well yeah, it's almost our understanding of what is in these models. That's something that I think is still emerging. So I think that even with no further progress there's still a massive shift that will happen. The economy being powered by compute and AI is still going to happen. But I think there's also something where what we've gotten very good at is training models on tasks that can be measured. And so what we started with was math problems and programming problems where you have a perfect verifier. And a lot of what the progress has been in bringing us to more open-ended problems has been expanding the space of what can be created. And the AI itself can really help with that if the AI is smart and understands things, you give it a rubric for how well a task goes. And of course for things like creative writing, like is this a good poem, that's a much harder thing to grade and so we've had less ability to teach the AI for it to experience and try things out. But all of that is changing in something that we have line of sight for. No, it's interesting reflecting on that. Peter Thiel has mentioned pretty much what you said, that if you're a math person you're probably in deeper trouble in terms of these models coming for what you do than if you're a words person. And you were a member of math club back in the day, are you not concerned about that? Well, I think that it's much easier to see what we lose than what we gain, right? Because we have a deep understanding of, "I used to do things this way, I used to do this math competition, now the AI can do the math competition." But it was never really about the math competition, right? That's not really the thing that drives humanity. And if you think about the way that we do work right now of there's a box and we type behind a box, we weren't doing that 100 years ago. That's not natural. That's not this digital world that we all got kind of sucked into. That's not really what being a human's about. Being a human's about being here, being present, connecting with other humans. And I think that what we're going to see is that AI is going to free up so much time to increase human connection, to build more bonds across people. And that's something I'm extremely excited about. Okay, and then as we shift—well, as you shift really—to these more agentic use cases, there's been discussion about whether the bigger training runs really need to happen and, you know, especially if you like get the model good enough, then you could sort of let it go out in the world and then you can effectively get much of the uplift in areas that aren't the pre-training, which is what these big data centers are needed for. So you were—worked or you work on scaling here, lead lead that process—what do you think about that argument? Well, I think it misses something very important for how the technology development goes. Because it is absolutely the case that every single step of the model production pipeline multiplies. And so you want to improve all of them. And the thing that we see as we improve the pre-training, it makes all the other steps much easier. And it makes sense, because it's a model is able to learn faster, it's a model that is—because it already is like more capable to start—when it's trying out different ideas and learning from its own mistakes, that process just is faster and makes fewer mistakes. And so I think that the big shift has been from thinking of it as just—you're just training this cerebral system on its own and just make it bigger and bigger—to it's also about trying things out, it's also about understanding how people are using it in the real world and connecting that back into your training, but it doesn't remove the value and the importance of continuing that research. And the thing that I think is also shifted is we used to really just focus on the raw pre-training capability but not think as much about the inference ability. And that's been a big change over the past 24 months, to realize that it's a balance between—you can have this model that has all those great properties in the base, but then you really need it to be able to be inferencable. Because you need reinforcement learning, you need to serve it to the world, and I think that means that you don't necessarily go as big as you possibly could because you also really think about there's going to be all this downstream use and you really want the thing that has the best intelligence times that cost and to optimize those two things together. Do you still need the Nvidia GPU if things move mostly to inference? We absolutely do. Why? Well, because there's multiple reasons, but one is that even as the balance of how much inference versus training changes, that you cannot get massive scale training through any other way besides this concentration of compute on one problem. And so I think that the thing that I think will happen is there's some amount of the deployment footprint goes up quite a lot, but that sometimes there will be—you have a particular massive pre-training run and you really want to concentrate a bunch of compute in there. I also think that the Nvidia team is just incredible and does really really amazing work and so yeah, we partner very closely with them. Isn't there going to be a time where people just say we've pre-trained enough, the models are smart enough? I think that that's a little bit like once humanity has solved all problems in front of us, then maybe we can say that, right? But I think that the ceiling of what we want to accomplish, I think that there's just so much ambition that maybe we've over the past 50 years or so just sort of backed off from, right? You think about even problems that seem very clear like: can we have healthcare for everyone that is not just that—it's actually preventative, not just targeting when people have a problem but really think about the lifestyle and how to really help people early detect potential diseases before they happen. Like that's a problem that I think we can actually achieve through more intelligent models. And there's probably some level where you can totally solve that problem and then you say, "Well, do I need a model that's two times smarter?" But there's other problems that are going to demand that. Let's talk about the math about building these data centers. You raised 110 billion earlier this year. What's the math behind that? Does that money go right into data centers? How do you think about how you're going to return that money to investors? Talk about those calculations. Yeah so I think it's as simple as the massive expense we see in front of us is compute. But you can think of compute not as a cost center but as a revenue center. Think of it a little bit like hiring salespeople. Right, how many salespeople do you want to hire? As long as you can sell your product, as long as you have a scalable way to sell that product, then the more salespeople you have the more revenue you will make. And I think the world that we're in is we have continually found we cannot build compute fast enough to keep up with demand. And I see this very concretely, right? Right now we have to make very painful decisions about what we're launching, about where the compute goes, and I think we're going to experience this more broadly within the economy. As we shift to this AI powered economy, the question will be: what problems are going to get that massive compute? How do you scale so everyone can have a personal agent running for them? How can everyone be using systems like Codex? Like there just isn't enough compute in the world to be able to do that. And so we're trying to get ahead of that problem. But it is a new category, right? So you're doing it with real confidence. I mean, in sums of money the world has never seen put towards a project like this. When you're building a new category how do you do it with certainty that it's going to work out? Well, I think there's several components that go into it. So the first is there is historical precedent at this point. From the moment we launched ChatGPT, I remember talking with my team having this exact conversation where I said, "How much compute should we buy?" and I said, "All of it." I said, "No no no, really how much compute should we buy?" and I said, "No matter how much we try to build, I know we're not going to be able to keep up with the demand." And that has been true and that has been true every year since then. And the challenge is that these compute purchases—you have to lock them in 18 months, sometimes 24 months, sometimes longer in advance of them actually being delivered—which means you really need to project forward. And I think that the world that we're moving towards is one where today most of our revenue has come from consumer subscriptions and that will always be very important, but there's other revenue streams we have emerging as well. But the opportunity that clearly is emerging now is knowledge work. And we're seeing this very concretely across every single enterprise realizing this technology it actually really works, and to be competitive they need to adopt it. The willingness to pay and the revenue growth that you're seeing in this industry is very clear, right? It's very clearly happening right now and you just project that forward. And we look—like one thing we get to see that maybe the world doesn't is the line of sight to how these models will improve. And all of this together says that the economy, which is a massive thing—right, the economy is just so large it's almost incomprehensible—all of the growth, like the highest order bit on how this economy grows from here, will be about AI, how well you can leverage AI, and the computational power you have available to power it. You said consumer subscriptions are your biggest source of revenue right now. Is the projection that that will flip and that business will be the biggest source? I think—well, I think that it is very, like very clear how quickly the enterprise—it's not just enterprise because I think enterprise is also changing what it means—but really people using it for productive knowledge work for those kinds of things. Far more people say data centers are mostly bad than good for the environment, home energy costs, and quality of life of those nearby. So it's really negligible water use. And yet, there's a lot of misinformation that these data centers consume a lot. And so similar on—on power—we have a commitment that we are going to pay our own way to not drive up energy prices for people. That's something that as an industry now that people are making these commitments because it is very important that we improve local communities. When we build data centers we really try to go into those local communities, understand what's happening on the ground, and how we can help. There's tax revenue that are associated with these data centers and I think there's jobs that they create. There's a lot of benefits that come from them. And so I think that one thing where it is about how we show up, and that's a responsibility that we take very seriously. Okay but also like if their power costs are not going to go up you have to bring in power which means potentially more pollution. Is that not a concern? Well, I think that—I think that there's much more nuance in terms of not driving up energy costs. If you look at how the grid works today, there's actually a lot of just stranded power. Power that is there that is not being utilized, and you need to upgrade the transmission systems. And again that's something where putting that on us rather than putting it on the rate payers is very important, right? There's lots of places where they have clean power that is actually being underutilized and just being kind of thrown away. And so I think that there's a lot of benefit that comes from having real reasons for that grid, which is aging and obsolete in many places, to upgrade—and that's something actually has real benefits to the community. Like we've seen for example in North Dakota that people's rates have gone down because a data center has shown up and has helped with improving the utilities for everyone. All right, last one for you on the politics. You gave 25 million dollars to MAGA Inc, which is a PAC that's pro-Trump. You spoke with Wired about it and you said, "Anything I can do to support this technology benefiting everyone is a thing I will do." And you know, if that makes you a one-issue voter or one-issue political support—I'll share the one thing I always wonder when it comes to just this one-issue camp. Like shouldn't a stronger country no matter what be the North Star of any political activity? And if that's the case, then is that part of the donation? So the way I'd look at this: so my wife and I made that donation; we've donated to bipartisan Super PACs as well. I think this technology is one where it's coming quickly, within the next couple years really going to transform everything and be the underpinning of the economy. And it's not popular, and we really want to support politicians who really lean into this technology, really engage with it. And so I think that certainly this technology is about uplifting us as a country. I—you know—I am a one-issue donor. This is something where I feel I have a unique contribution to make. But it's really about just expressing support for this technology as something that we should be leaning into as a country. What would you tell someone who's—who's scared of AI? I mean if you have a moment here where you can speak directly to them: they might think it's going to take my job, it's going to pollute my community, it will change the world too fast. What's your message to them? Number one thing is try the tools, because to really understand what it can do for you is something that only by experiencing the AIs that exist now will that really hit home. And we see so much opportunity and potential and empowerment coming from this technology today. You talked a little bit about what you can build now, right? People who have never built a website before can build a website. If you want to start a small business and you're thinking about all the back-end processing and how to actually manage it and all those things, the AI can help you with that right now. And so I think that in your life, thinking about how it can help you with your health, how it can help your loved ones, how it can help you make money, how it can help you save money—these are all going to be on the table. And I think that it is much easier to see what's going to change than it is to see what you're going to gain. But I think that it is worth giving it a fair shot of really trying to understand both sides of the equation. That's the one thing that doesn't get talked about in the polling data by the way. It's the people that have seen it used but haven't tried it themselves, or the people that have never tried AI, are much more negative. And then you get to the power users, or even the people that use it casually, and they're generally pretty positive about the technology. Yeah, for myself, we've been thinking about this technology for a long time. What I see playing out in front of us is more amazing, more beneficial, and going to really have a much more positive impact than we ever imagined. So last one for you. How would you advise someone to prepare themselves for the future—and it has to be more than just get in the tools? I do think that the number one thing is about understanding the technology. One thing we've seen is the people who get the most out of the technology approach it with a curiosity, trying to really try it in your workflows, really being able to get over that initial hump of, "You have a blank box, what do I do with a blank box?" To really develop that sense of agency, that sense of, "I can be the manager, I can set the direction, I can delegate, I can provide oversight"—and to really develop that skill, because that is something that's going to be fundamental. We're building this technology for humans to help humans foster more human connection, for humans to be able to spend more time doing what they want. And so the question is, well, what do you want? And really trying to crystallize that and trying to realize that with the help of this technology is going to be the most important thing. Greg, thanks so much for coming on the show. Vice President JD Vance Swears-In Colin McDonald

The White House(3.0M) ·12:54 ·26.5K views · 2026-04-01
Vice President JD Vance swore in Colin McDonald as Assistant Attorney General to lead a newly established Department of Justice (DOJ) division dedicated to a "zero-tolerance" approach toward taxpayer fraud, aiming to restore social trust and fiscal deterrence.
TL;DR Vice President JD Vance swore in Colin McDonald as Assistant Attorney General to lead a newly established Department of Justice (DOJ) division dedicated to a "zero-tolerance" approach toward taxpayer fraud, aiming to restore social trust and fiscal deterrence. Strategic...

TL;DR

Vice President JD Vance swore in Colin McDonald as Assistant Attorney General to lead a newly established Department of Justice (DOJ) division dedicated to a "zero-tolerance" approach toward taxpayer fraud, aiming to restore social trust and fiscal deterrence.

The Anti-Fraud Task Force and Mission

  • Strategic Context: The administration identifies a "fraud crisis" where trillions in taxpayer dollars are at risk due to a breakdown in social trust and government oversight.
      • Leadership: The effort is spearheaded by a dedicated Anti-Fraud Task Force led by Vice President JD Vance.
      • Policy Shift: Speakers criticized previous administrations and "blue states" for allowing billions to reach fraudsters, both domestic and foreign.
  • Preserving the Safety Net: The mission is framed as essential to protecting the social safety net for those who have a legal right to participate in and benefit from these programs.

Establishment of a New DOJ Division

  • New Organizational Structure: JD Vance announced the creation of a specific division within the DOJ focused holistically on eliminating fraud against taxpayers.
      • Departmental Support: The office was established with the cooperation of Attorney General Pam Bondi and Deputy Attorney General Todd Blanche.
  • Appointment of Colin McDonald: McDonald, a career prosecutor from California, was selected to lead the division as Assistant Attorney General.
      • Professional Background: McDonald previously prosecuted cases involving fraud, political corruption, and organized crime, and served under the Deputy Attorney General for the past year.

Enforcement Priorities and Deterrence

  • Zero-Tolerance Policy: A primary shift in strategy involves investigating all fraud regardless of the dollar amount.
      • Ending Thresholds: Vance noted that the DOJ previously "turned a blind eye" to fraud under certain dollar amounts; the new division will prosecute both high-level and low-level offenses.
  • Restoring Deterrence: The division’s goal is to ensure potential fraudsters fear the consequences of their actions, specifically the threat of federal prosecution and jail time.
      • Application Scrutiny: McDonald emphasized a particular focus on identifying and prosecuting false applications for government funds.

Legislative and Political Context

  • Senate Confirmation: McDonald was confirmed by the U.S. Senate, receiving unanimous Republican support but limited support from Democrats.
  • Executive Leadership: The briefing highlighted that this specific focus on taxpayer fraud is a cornerstone of the President's broader directive to improve government efficiency and accountability.
"One of the things Colin is going to do is make sure that no fraud, no matter how big or how small it is, no fraud is small enough or big enough to look away from." — Vice President JD Vance
"To the American people, my promise to you is this: that I will work tirelessly day after day to ensure that if someone out there dares to steal your taxpayer dollars, there might just be a federal prosecutor on the end of that bad decision." — Assistant Attorney General Colin McDonald
"All the programs we pay into are premised on a high level of social trust, that our neighbors and friends will honestly participate in these programs and the government will make sure that everyone is being honest." — Andrew Ferguson
Good morning, welcome. We are here today because America has a fraud crisis. This is the most generous country on the planet. Every year, everyone in this room, your friends and your family part with trillions of their tax dollars to help out their friends, families, and neighbors in tough times. All the programs we pay into are premised on a high level of social trust—that our neighbors and friends will honestly participate in these programs and the government will make sure that everyone is being honest. And what President Trump has discovered is that this has largely not been going on. A coalition of poor government within the federal government under the previous administration and blue states have made it so that billions and billions of dollars of American tax dollars go to fraudsters. These are both citizens and foreigners who come here to take advantage of America's generosity and largesse. That is ending today. And that is ending today because the President has convened the anti-fraud task force led by the Vice President to make sure that these funds don't go out the door to fraudsters anymore. And equally as importantly, today we are here to swear in a brilliant prosecutor to restore deterrence. We want to make sure people are afraid to commit fraud because they are afraid of the consequences and to make sure that people who do commit fraud go to jail where they belong. And that prosecutor is Colin McDonald, a brilliant career prosecutor from California who's worked for our brilliant Deputy Attorney General for the last year, helping get the Justice Department into shape after the Biden administration.
